When I completed for you, my sons, this book which is a part of knowledge in medicine in its entirety, and reached the goal therein through its clarity and explanation, I saw that I should complete it for you with this section which is the part of work by hand.
For surgical practice by hand is in our land and time completely nonexistent, so much so that its study was nearly discontinued and its effect lost. And what remained of it were only a few diagrams in the books of our predecessors, which hands corrupted and filled with error and confusion until their meanings became obscured and their benefit distant.
Therefore, I decided to revive it and compose this section on the path of explanation, clarification, and abbreviation, and to present images of sharp surgical instruments and all other tools used in surgery, for this is an increase in explanation and a fulfillment of what is needed.
The reason why there are no skilled craftsmen with their hands in our time is that the craft of medicine is long, and its possessor should first be trained in anatomy, which Galen described. So that he may understand the benefits of the organs, their structures and temperaments, their connections and separations, as well as knowledge of the bones, nerves, muscles, their number, origins, and the veins, arteries, and sinuses and their locations.
Therefore Hippocrates said, "Physicians in name are many, but in deed few, especially in the craft of hand," and we have mentioned a part of this in the introduction to this book.
For he who is not knowledgeable about what we have mentioned of anatomy is bound to fall into error that kills people, as I have witnessed many times from those who imagined themselves to be experts in this science and claimed it without knowledge or experience.
Indeed, I once saw an ignorant physician who incised a wen on a woman's neck and severed some of the arteries in her neck, causing her to bleed profusely until she died at his feet.
I once saw another physician who had advanced in the removal of a stone for a man who had been stabbed in the tooth, and the stone was large; so it crumbled, and he removed it with a piece from the bladder neck. The man died in about three days, and I had been summoned to remove it; I saw from the size of the stone and the condition of the patient that it was beyond my ability.
I saw another physician who made a living practicing medicine for some of our local leaders.
It happened that a black boy had a fracture in his leg near the heel with a wound, and the physician, in his ignorance, hastily bound the fracture on the wound with bandages and splints tightly and did not leave the wound room to breathe.
Then he released him to his own devices and left him for days, ordering him not to loosen the bandages until his leg and foot swelled up and he was near death.
I was summoned to him, and I quickly untied the bandages; he found relief and recovered from his pain, but the damage had become firmly established in the limb, and I was unable to restore it.
The corruption continued to spread in the limb until he died.
I saw another physician cauterize a cancerous tumor, and it ulcerated after a few days, greatly afflicting its owner. This is because if the cancer is entirely composed of black bile, it should not be treated with hot iron at all.
Unless it is in a member which can tolerate the removal of all of it.
Therefore, my son, you should know that work done by hand is divided into two parts: work that is accompanied by safety and work that is likely to result in harm in most cases.
I have warned in every place it comes up in this book about the work that involves risk and danger, so you should beware of it and refuse to do it.
So that the ignorant man may not find a way to speak and revile, take care for yourselves with caution and watchfulness, and treat your patients with gentleness and deliberation.
Use the best path leading to safety and a happy outcome, and beware of dangerous and difficult diseases.
Purify yourselves from what you fear may bring doubt into your religion and your worldly affairs, for it is the most lasting safeguard for your honor and the highest attainment in this world and the next for your stations.
For Galen said in some of his precepts, "Do not treat a bad disease lest you become bad physicians."
This treatise has been divided into three chapters.
The First Chapter.
Cauterization with fire and cauterization with caustic drugs, arranged in order from the most recent to the oldest times, and illustrations of instruments and iron for cautery, and everything needed for work by hand.
The Second Chapter.
Incision, dissection, bloodletting, cupping, surgery and the extraction of arrows and the like, all arranged in order, and illustrations of instruments.
The Third Chapter.
In splinting, setting bones, and treating dislocations and the like, arranged in order from the most recent to the oldest times, and illustrations of instruments.
The First Chapter.
On Cautery.
Before mentioning its use, it is necessary to describe the ways in which it is beneficial and harmful, and in what temperaments it is used.
I say that speech concerning the ways in which cautery is beneficial and harmful is lengthy discourse, a subtle science, and a hidden secret, about which many wise men have spoken.
And they differed in their opinions; I have summarized a little of what they said for fear of being too long.
I say that cupping is generally beneficial for all bad humors whether with a material or without, namely hot humor without a material and dry humor without a material.
As for dry humor with a material, they differed on it, some saying that cupping is beneficial and others saying the opposite, that cupping is not suitable for a disease caused by heat and dryness because fire is by nature hot and dry.
It is impossible to be cured of a hot and dry disease by a hot and dry remedy, and the one who says otherwise claims that cupping with fire may benefit from a hot and dry disease that occurs in people's bodies. Because when you add the human body and its moisture to the humor of fire, you arrive at a cooling effect for the human body.
And I say the same, because experience has shown me this many times, but it should not be attempted except by one who has practiced and trained extensively in the art of cupping. And he must have a thorough understanding of the differences in people's humors, the states of illnesses themselves, their causes, symptoms, and duration.
As for all other humors, there is no danger in them, especially cold and wet diseases, on which all physicians agree and are unanimous about the benefits of cupping.
Know, my son, that the secret of treating with fire cupping and its superiority to cupping with burning medicine is because fire is a simple element whose action does not extend beyond the cupped member and does little harm to other connected members.
Cupping with burning medicine, on the other hand, can extend its action beyond the cupped members and may even cause a disease in a member that is difficult to treat and may even kill it. Fire, due to its honor and generosity of essence, does not do this unless it is excessive.
This has been made clear to us by experience, through long service and care in the craft, and by grasping the true nature of things.
Therefore, I have dispensed with lengthy discourse; if it were not unbecoming to my book, I would reveal to you a secret and hidden fire, its method of action in the body, and its negation of diseases through philosophical and demonstrative reasoning which would pierce your understanding.
Know, my sons, that they have differed concerning the time best suited for cautery, and they have made spring the best time.
And I say that cautery is suitable at all times, because the harm caused by time is surpassed by the benefit obtained through cautery. Especially if the cautery is from necessary, strong pains, which cannot be delayed, lest what follows be a tribulation greater than the slight harm caused by time.
Do not imagine, my sons, what the common people and ignorant physicians suppose: that cautery which cures a disease will never recur, and make it obligatory.
For the matter is not as they think, because cautery is like a medicine which changes the temperament and dries up the humors which are the cause of pain.
Except that cautery surpasses medicine in the speed of its success, the strength of its action, and the severity of its power.
And it is possible that the disease may return at some time, according to the temperament of the patient, the strength and power of his illness, and what may occur in his body from the gathering of superfluities and negligence in acquiring them from food and the like.
Except if the disease for which cautery is used is a mild one and in an organ with few superfluities and humors. Such as cautery of a tooth for pain and the like, it may be that the pain will not return, or at least it will be less.
The common saying that cautery is the last of medicine is true, but not in the way they understand it, for they believe that no treatment is effective by drug or otherwise after cautery has occurred. The matter is different.
The meaning of the saying that cautery is the last of medicine is that when we have used various treatments for a disease and those drugs have not been effective, and then we use cautery as a last resort and it is successful, then it is said that cautery is the last of medicine. Not according to the meaning to which the common people and many ignorant physicians adhere.
The ancients have stated that cautery with gold is better than cautery with iron, saying this because of the moderation of gold and its noble character, and they said that it does not cause suppuration at the site of cautery. This is not true at all.
For I have tried it and found that it only does this in some bodies and not others, and cautery with it is better and more excellent than iron as they said, except that if you heat the iron in the fire like gold, it will not become apparent to you when it has reached the degree of heat that you desire for the redness of gold.
And because it quickly cools, and if you increase its heat in the fire, it melts and pours out, causing the craftsman to fall into trouble. Therefore, cautery with iron has become quicker and closer to correctness for our work, God willing.
I have arranged this chapter on cautery into sections ordered from the head to the foot to make it easy for the student to find what they need, God willing.
Chapter One.
Cautery of the Head: One Method.
This cautery is beneficial for the predominance of moisture and cold on the brain, which are the cause of headaches, frequent colds from the head to the area around the eyes and ears, excessive sleepiness, toothache, and sore throat. And in general for every illness which arises from cold such as paralysis, epilepsy, stroke and the like of these diseases.
The image of this procedure is to order the patient first to vomit with a purgative medicine purified for the head for three or four nights according to what necessitates the strength of the patient, his age and habit.
Then order him to shave his head with a razor then seat him between your hands squatting having placed his hands on his chest.
Then place the base of your hand on the base of his nose between his eyes and where your middle finger ends mark that spot with ink.
Then heat the olive-wood cautery iron whose image is this.
Expose from the bone the head of a pin or the size of a carob bean then raise your hand or else apply your hand with the same iron or another if it cooled. Until you see from the bone what I have mentioned to you then take some salt, dissolve it in water and soak a cotton ball in it, place it on the spot and leave it for three days.
Then place on it a cotton ball soaked in clarified butter and leave it on until the burning sensation from the fire subsides then treat it with the four-ingredient ointment until it heals, if God wills.
And they have said that the wound, the longer it remains open and discharges pus, the better and more beneficial it is.
And some have mentioned to cauterize the skin down to the bone and hold the iron until some of the thickness of the bone burns then remove what has been burned from the bone afterward and treat it.
And others have said that one should exaggerate the cautery so as to strongly affect the bone until it falls from the bone like a small nail or a small flake and they claimed that vapor of the head escapes from that spot and leaves the wound open for a long time.
Then it is treated until it heals, and I do not see these two types of burns at all except in some people and by chance. Leaving them with me is best, and farewell if that is the case. For the head weakens when its natural connection is separated, as we have seen in all other organs.
Especially when the patient's head is weak by nature, the first type of burn is safer and better with me, and with it I work, so do as I say and you will be safe if God wills.
Chapter Two.
Burns to the head also.
If chronic pain occurs throughout the head and lasts for the patient, and poultices, collyria, potions, ointments, and bandages are used, especially if he has been burned by the single burn that we described and nothing helps him, then look. If the patient's head is strong by nature and not weak, and he feels intense cold, then cauterize another burn slightly above that one. Then cauterize each horn of his head with a burn until the skin's thickness is gone and the bone is exposed to the extent that we have described. And cauterize a burn at the back of his head in the place known as the hatchet, and lighten your hand in this one and do not expose the bone because the patient will feel intense pain from it unlike the pain of all other burns on the head, and I will mention this burn in its place.
The iron with which the horns and back of the head are cauterized should be finer than the iron with which the middle of the head is cauterized, and this is its image.
Chapter Three.
On non-chronic migraines.
If pain occurs in a section of the head with headache and the pain extends to the eye, then purge the patient with purgatives for the head and use all the treatments I mentioned in the divisions of diseases. If that does not work, cauterize it on two sides. Either with a sharp burning medicine or with iron.
As for cauterization with burning medicine, it is to take one clove of garlic, peel it and cut off its ends on both sides, then make an incision in the painful area at the temple with a broad trocar until there is a place under the skin large enough for the clove.
Insert it under the skin until it is hidden, then tie it tightly with strong ligatures and leave it for fifteen hours, then untie it and remove the garlic, leaving the wound for two or three days, then dress it.
Cover it with cotton soaked in ghee until the area suppurates, then treat it with ointment until it heals, if God wills. If you wish, you can do this with some of the burning medicines that I mentioned in the eighteenth article on burning medicines.
As for cauterization with iron, it is done as follows: heat the iron which has this shape:
It is called a nail iron because its head is shaped like a nail with some grooves and a small protrusion in the middle.
Then place it on the painful area and hold your hand while turning the iron slightly, burning an amount equal to half the thickness of the skin. Lift your hand so as not to burn the artery below, which would cause bleeding, then soak cotton in salt water and place it on the area and leave it for three days. Then dress the cotton with ghee, then treat it with ointment until it heals if God wills.
If you wish, cauterize this sciatica with the pointed knife tip that comes from the iron and avoid cutting the artery in this unchronic sciatica, especially.
Chapter Four.
On Cauterizing Chronic Sciatica.
If you have treated the migraine with the treatment mentioned above and the division of diseases, and the treatment has not been successful, and you see from the cause something that cannot be remedied by what we mentioned in the first cautery with medicine or cautery with fire. The searing iron should then be heated until it glows white. You will learn the location of the pain with a line half the length of a finger or so, and leave your hand once while pulling it so that it cuts the artery and reaches toward the bone.
However, you should be careful not to touch the moving jaw when chewing, as this will burn the muscle or the nerve that moves it and cause paralysis.
And be wary of bleeding from the severed artery, for there is danger in its severance. Especially for those who do not know what they are doing and are not an experienced practitioner, it is best to leave this work alone.
The management of bleeding that occurs from the artery will be mentioned in its place in the book, God willing. If you see from the cause something that cannot be remedied by this cautery.
And you saw the body of the patient tolerating a cautery in the middle of the head as we described and treated the wound until it heals, God willing.
And if you wish, use the cautery we mentioned in the chapter on severing an artery with a double-bladed iron, as it is better and more effective than this one.
Chapter Five.
On Cautery for Ear Pains.
If pain occurs in the ear due to cold, and it is treated with purgatives and all the other treatments mentioned in the division, and the pain does not subside, heat the iron called "the point" which is shaped like this.
Then cauterize around the entire ear with it, as it turns, or all around them both if the pain is in them. And cauterize with a cautery a little distance from the base of the ear after learning the spot with ink, making the cautery ten points in each ear or thereabouts, then treat the spots until they heal, God willing.
Chapter Six.
Cauterization of Strength.
The strength that is treated by cautery is of the kind that occurs from phlegm as mentioned in the divisions of diseases.
And one avoids cauterizing the type that occurs from dryness and constriction of nerves.
When you have treated this type of strength with poultices, fomentations and gargles and your treatment has not been successful, then the patient should be cauterized with three cauterizations. One at the base of the ear, the second a little below the cheekbone, and the third at the junction of the lips.
Make your cautery opposite the affected side because relaxation only occurs on the side that appears sound.
And as for the image of the cautery, make one cautery opposite the upper end of the ear, a little below the horn of the head, and another in the cheekbone, its length being the length of the thumb.
Lower the cautery with your hand until it burns half the thickness of the skin, and this is the image of the iron, which is a type of surgical knife whose shape has already been presented, but it is slightly finer as you see. And the knife should have a slight increase in thickness, then treat the spot with what has already been mentioned until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Seven.
Cauterization for Chronic Stroke.
If the stroke has persisted and you have treated it with what we have mentioned, and your treatment has not been successful, and the patient does not have a fever, then cauterize him with four cauteries. On every century of its head, a cautery and a cautery in the middle of the head as we mentioned, and a cautery at the back of the head as previously stated.
As for the description of the cauteries as previously mentioned, it may also be cauterized with a cautery on the mouth of the stomach to be more effective, and then treat as previously stated.
Chapter Eight.
Cauterization for Forgetfulness.
That which is from phlegm, it is necessary that the patient be given first from large juleps and purifying herbs for the brain.
Then his entire head is shaved and a mustard plaster, as written in the article on plasters, is applied to the back of it repeatedly for it is a type of cautery.
And do that on the rank specifically mentioned there, for if he is cured by that, otherwise cauterize three cauteries in the back of his head aligned from the top of the head to the bottom of the neck.
And make the width between each cautery and the next one equal to the thickness of a finger, then treat the cauteries as previously stated, and if you desire more and the patient can tolerate it, then cauterize the middle cautery.
And if you desire more, then cauterize on the temples and treat it until he is cured, and the cautery iron should be olive-wood as previously described.
Chapter Nine.
Cauterization for Palsy and Relaxation of the Whole Body.
It is necessary to proceed with purifying the head with juleps and what we mentioned, then shave the patient's head, then cauterize a cautery in the middle of the head, a cautery on each temple of the head, and a cautery at the back of it.
And three on the vertebrae of the neck, and if you need more for relaxation of the body and the patient can tolerate it and the illness is strong and chronic, then cauterize four cauteries on the vertebrae of his back. Heat the cautery until it burns most of the skin, then raise your hand and treat it as previously mentioned so that it heals if God wills, and let the cautery be olive-wood.
Chapter Ten.
On Epilepsy.
Only an epileptic whose seizures are caused by phlegm should be cauterized. First, his brain should be purged with large enemas and all the treatments we mentioned in the division, if the patient is an adult and able to take medicine.
But if he is a child who cannot tolerate medicine, gargles and washes that purify the brain should be used for many days beforehand, along with an improved diet.
Then shave his head and cauterize him once in the middle of the head as previously described, and another cautery at the back of the head. And on each temple of his head, a cautery. If the patient is strong and able to tolerate it, cauterize him with the number of cauteries mentioned for the paralytic patient and relax the body on the hollows of the neck and back. The cautery should be olive-wood as previously described.
But if the patient is a child, make the cautery delicate in this manner.
Chapter Eleven.
On Cauterizing Melancholia.
If melancholia is caused by foul humors and thick phlegm, cauterize him with the number of cauteries we mentioned for the paralytic patient.
If melancholia is caused by a predominance of black bile and the patient's body is moist, purge his brain as previously mentioned in the division.
Then shave the patient's head and make a tight cake of linen like a circle, then lower it in the middle of his head while the patient is sitting upright, holding on to each side. Then take one pound of old sheep fat.
Then heat it over a moderate fire until the finger can tolerate it, then empty it in the middle of its head into the circle and leave it until it cools. Do this to the patient once a week along with all his good treatment so that he may recover if God wills.
And if you wish, cauterize it with many small punctures without holding your hand on the iron, for this type of cautery will moderately moisten the brain.
Then apply cotton soaked in ghee or chicken fat to it.
Chapter Twelve.
On cautery for watery eye.
If the beginning of watery eye is apparent to you by the signs mentioned in the division, hasten and make the patient wash his head and protect it from all moisture, and have him sweat in a bath on an empty stomach for several days.
Then, once his head is shaved, cauterize it in the middle of the head, then two cauteries on the temples if the beginning of watery eye is in both eyes, or on one side if it is in one eye.
Then cut all the veins and arteries under the skin with the iron, making the cauteries long in the width of the temples and beware of bleeding.
If you see any of it, cut it at once with whatever treatment is possible, and I will come to the wisdom in severing the arteries and preventing bleeding.
And two strong cauteries may be applied on the nape of the neck under the bones.
Chapter Thirteen.
On cautery for chronic tearing.
If tearing of the eye is chronic and persistent, and it comes from the veins and arteries on the outside of the head, and you are certain that this is due to cold phlegm that is thick and bilious, then cauterize it as described for the beginning of watery eye. A cautery in the middle of the head and two on the temples, and two on the nape beneath the bones, and if you need more cauterize one on each side of the tail of the eye at the end of the eyebrow with a small iron.
Chapter Fourteen.
On Cauterizing the Nose.
If you treat him with what we mentioned in the division and the treatment does not work, then hasten to make the patient fast for three nights, then shave his head and cauterize the middle cautery with an olive-oil iron. Then cauterize with a nail iron two cauteries above the eyebrows under the hair a little, and be careful not to cut the artery. This is a picture of the nail iron.
Chapter Fifteen.
On Cauterizing Relaxed Eyelid.
If an eyelid relaxes from illness or moisture, cauterize the eyelid with one cautery using this crescent-shaped iron.
And if you wish, cauterize above the eyebrows a little two cauteries on each side, spaced from the temples, and the length of each cautery is along the eyebrow.
Do not overdo your hand with the cautery, but only to the extent that one-third of the skin is burned, and the iron will be shaped like this.
Chapter Sixteen.
On Cauterizing the Eyelid.
If its eyelashes turn inward and sting the eye, then cautery is done in two ways. Either cautery with fire or cautery with a burning medicine, but if you cauterize it with fire, order the patient beforehand to leave his eyelashes alone if he is one who plucks them so that they grow long and even.
For if they sting him when they are sprouting, tighten his eyelids with a bandage so that they do not move until they grow. And when they have grown and become even, then place the patient's head in your lap and draw on the eyelid with ink a mark in the shape of an asparagus leaf.
The sign begins near the eyelash and then you place under the eyelid cotton soaked in egg white or the saliva of a hen, then heat an iron shaped like this.
Then it is cauterized according to the shape you have been taught, a little at a time in many sittings until the surface of the skin burns, which is shaped like an ash leaf entirely exposed and a sign of your work's success is that you see the eyelid has wrinkled and the hair has risen from the flesh of the eye.
Then raise your hand at that time and leave it for three days, then apply cotton with ghee to it until the scab comes off, then treat it with ointment until it heals, if God wills, but if something of the hair returns after a while and the eyelid relaxes.
Then cauterize that spot again as you did at first, and if the hair is in the lower eyelid, pluck it until it returns to its natural place so that it does not encroach upon the eye. As for cautery with burning medicine, it is to order the patient to leave the eyelash alone until it grows long and level.
Then make from paper a shape like an ash leaf, then take from the common soap and quicklime not yet quenched, one dirham's weight or thereabouts of each, and crush them all together thoroughly and do this quickly so that it does not dry. Then spread it on the paper you have made in the shape of an ash leaf and put it on one or both eyelids, and place cotton soaked in egg white under the eye, and the patient's head in your lap.
Place your index finger over the medicine and press it lightly, moving it whenever the patient feels the burning sensation of the medicine because he will find it pleasurable like fire. As long as he feels the burning, leave the medicine and move it with your finger, and when the burning subsides remove the medicine and wash the eye with water.
And see if you find the eyelid has risen as it rises when blushing with fire or cutting, otherwise put more of the medicine on the spot where the medicine has not taken effect and has not blackened until your work is complete and the eye blushes, then place cotton soaked in butter on it until the burn scab comes off.
Then treat it with palm ointment or another until it is healed, and you should, when working, be extremely cautious that nothing of the medicine falls into the eye; for if the eyelid relaxes after a few days, we fear something of the hair may fall into the eye.
So put the medicine on that spot of the relaxed eyelid again, as you did first, and then treat it until it is healed. This is an image of a leaf of the plant Asafoetida.
Know that people's eyes vary in size, so let your blushing be according to that, and this is not a subtle path of rightness for one who has experience in this craft.
Chapter Seventeenth.
On the ingrown eyelash that appears in the corner of the eye.
If you treat the ingrown eyelash as we mentioned in the sections on diseases and your treatment is unsuccessful, it should be cauterized in this manner.
Order the patient to place his head in your lap and have a servant hold his head between your hands, holding it so that it does not move or shake.
Then place cotton wool moistened in egg white or in the saliva of a fig on his eyes, then heat the iron which has this shape.
It should be hollow like the quill of an eagle's feather from one end, which is the end for cauterizing. If you wish, the other end may be open; if you wish, it may be closed like a pomegranate seed, but this hollow one is better for your work, God willing.
Then press the abscess if it is open and extract the pus from it, or if it is not open, then open it and extract the pus; then put on it a cautery iron which is very hot, hold it with your hand until it reaches the bone.
Move your hand slightly away from the eye towards the nose so that your hand does not err, nor does the patient become agitated and the cautery iron falls into the eyeball socket and spoils it; if it reaches the bone in the first application, otherwise apply the cautery iron a second time if needed.
Leave it for three days, then apply cotton soaked in butter and treat it with drying ointment until it is healed; if forty days pass and it does not heal, apply a sharp corrosive medicine so that the bone is exposed and clean it according to what will be mentioned in its chapter, God willing.
Another method of cauterizing an abscess, mentioned by some of the ancients, is to intentionally make a cut at the site of the abscess and then insert a thin trocar into the same incision, its image is as follows.
Pour into it a weight equal to one dirham of molten lead and hold the trocar firmly with your hand so that it does not move at all, lest the molten lead flows into his eye; you should place cotton soaked in egg white or water on the patient's eye.
For the lead burns the site of the abscess and heals it in an astonishing way; if the abscess is healed by what we have mentioned regarding cauterization and treatment, otherwise it is necessary to use nasal puncture and return the abscess to the nasal passage as will be mentioned in its appropriate place.
Chapter Eighteenth.
On Cauterizing a Lip Fissure.
A fissure often occurs in the lip, called a harelip, especially in the lips of boys, as it frequently happens; if you treat this fissure with what we have mentioned in the division and the treatment is unsuccessful, then heat a small, pointed cautery iron to this shape:
Let her cavity be on the thinness of a knife, then put it in a bandage with haste into the same incision until the cautery reaches the depth of the incision, then treat it with cerate so that it heals if God wills.
Chapter Nineteenth.
On the cauterization of a wound in the mouth.
If a tumor appears in the root of the gum or palate or roots of molars, then it dries and bursts, and pus flows out becoming a fistula, and if treatment is not successful, then a cautery iron should be heated to a size that fits within the fistula.
Then carefully insert it into the fistula hole, and hold your hand until the iron reaches its deepest point, doing this once or twice, then treat it afterwards as we have previously mentioned so that it heals if God wills.
If the matter is discharged and it heals, otherwise, it is necessary to examine the area and remove the decayed bone as will be explained in its chapter if God wills.
Chapter Twentieth.
On cauterizing molars and relaxed gums.
If the gums become relaxed due to moisture and the molars move, and medications are not effective, then place the headrest in your lap and heat the iron whose image will be shown after this, placing the tube over the molar.
Carefully insert the iron into it with haste and hold your hand for a short time until the patient feels the heat of the fire has reached the root of the molar, then lift your hand and repeat the iron application several times as desired, then have the patient fill his mouth with salt water.
He should hold it for an hour and then spit it out, as the moving molars will become fixed and the relaxed gums will tighten, drying the corrupt moisture.
Chapter Twenty-One.
On the pain of toothache.
If the toothache is from cold or there are worms in it and medication does not help, then cauterization should be done in two ways: either with butter or with fire.
As for cauterization with butter, take cow butter, melt it in an iron ladle or a seashell. Then take cotton and wrap it around the end of a probe. Dip it into the melted butter and quickly place it on the aching tooth, holding it until it cools.
Repeat this several times until the heat reaches the root of the tooth. If you wish, soak a wick or cotton in cold butter and place it on the aching tooth. Then put a heated iron on top of it so that the heat reaches the bottom of the tooth.
As for cauterizing it with fire, take a copper or iron tube, making sure it has some thickness so that the heat of the fire does not reach the patient's mouth. Then heat an iron shaped like the one shown and place it on the same tooth. Hold your hand there until the iron cools down. Repeat this several times.
The pain will go away either that same day or the next. After cauterization, the patient should fill their mouth with good butter and hold it for an hour before spitting it out. This is a picture of the iron.
Cauterize with whichever end you prefer, to the extent that is possible. This is a picture of the tube.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
On Cauterizing Warts.
If warts are from phlegm and cold humors, and do not mature with medication, and you desire quick maturation, heat a hollow iron, shaped like the one shown here, with openings on both ends.
Let the smoke come out at the cautery from the other end and place it protected on the same tumor once or twice if needed until it reaches the depth of the tumor, but if the tumor is small, place the iron to the extent of the tumor.
Then leave it for three days and carry on it cotton soaked in butter until what was burned by the fire goes away, then treat it with ointment and wicks until it heals, if God wills.
Chapter Twenty-Third.
On cautery for hoarseness and shortness of breath.
If phlegm overwhelms the trachea, especially if this is accompanied by a cold temperament, the patient should first be purged with laxatives, then cauterized in the hollow of the neck at the base of the pharynx in a low position.
Be careful not to reach the pharynx with the cautery and burn no more than half of the skin, then cauterize another point at the junction of the neck in the last vertebra prominently. The iron should be pointed as previously described.
Then treat it as previously mentioned until it heals, if God wills.
Chapter Twenty-Fourth.
On cautery for lung disease and cough.
If the cough and lung disease are due to cold phlegm and the patient does not have a fever or consumption, and the illness is chronic, cauterize two points above the collarbones in low, soft, empty spaces, and another point in the middle of the chest between the breasts.
The iron should be pointed as previously described, or if you wish, cauterize in a punctate manner with the iron called "al-nida," whose shape has been previously shown in puncturing and earache. The number of points should be from thirty to approximately that amount.
Then treat it as previously mentioned until it heals. You may make a three-pronged iron in this shape, and hasten cauterization with it because you cauterize three points at once. This is the image of the iron:
Chapter Twenty-Five
In the Armpit.
If the head of the humerus has become dislocated due to slippery humors, or if it has not been fixed when being put back in place so that this becomes habitual, and it is then repeatedly dislocated with the slightest movement, as we have witnessed, then the jaw should first be put back into place. The patient should then lie on his back or on his right side, and the skin inside the armpit should be lifted upwards with the fingers of your left hand if the joint has been dislocated inwards.
Then a heated iron with two prongs, such as that pictured, should be applied.
The skin should then be cauterized with it until it penetrates to the other side, producing a burn mark of four dots. It may also be cauterized with an iron having three prongs, in which case the burn mark will consist of six dots, with the prongs resting on the fleshy part of the armpit.
This is a picture of an iron with three prongs.
The number of prongs may be increased by one, making a total of eight dots. Afterwards, pounded alum mixed with salt should be applied to the burns, and the patient should be kept at rest and the limb immobilized for a time until it strengthens. If the dislocation is upwards, which rarely happens...
…a single, well-placed cautery above the elbow should be applied, or many small punctures may be made. This will strengthen the joint and eliminate the moisture, causing the patient to recover.
Chapter Twenty-Six
On Cauterizing the Abdomen.
If the stomach has suffered from a cold and excessive moisture, causing it to deviate from its normal state and resulting in frequent attacks that have not been relieved by various treatments, then the patient should lie on his back with his legs and arms extended, and three cauteries should be applied. Make an incision under the spoon of the chest as wide as a finger with a heated iron, and two incisions below on either side of the first one until they form a triangle, spacing them apart so that they do not join when they heal. The depth of the incisions should be one-third the thickness of the skin.
To this extent and no more.
And if you wish, make one large cautery in the middle of the stomach in this manner.
The iron with which this single cautery is made should be circular in shape as shown.
The stomach may also be cauterized in a punctate manner for those who are afraid of this cautery, and this is done by marking points on the stomach with ink in the desired quantity, then cauterizing them with a pointed iron, and treating them with the aforementioned treatment until they heal.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On Cauterization of the Cold Liver.
If the liver presents with pain due to coldness and dampness, or a strong wind, which has caused it to depart from its natural temperament excessively, and the patient has been treated as mentioned in the division previously but without success. The patient should lie on his back and three cauteries marked in this shape and size exactly, should be applied to the liver below the ribs where the elbow of a man ends.
The distance between each cautery should be the width of a finger, and the cautery should be straight along the body. Do not press hard with the iron.
The depth of the burn should be half the thickness of the skin, no more. The patient should stand on his feet. If he is unable to stand, he should lie down with his legs extended and arms raised. This is an image of the iron.
You may cauterize these with a pointed iron if you have experience and skill in the craft, but be careful not to press too hard during cautery so that it burns all the skin, reaches the abdomen, and damages the intestines. The skin is thin in that area, so be aware.
Chapter Twenty-Eight
In the treatment of a liver abscess with cautery.
If an abscess appears in the liver and you wish to know whether the swelling is in the flesh of the liver or in its peritoneum, then if it is in the flesh of the liver, the patient will experience heaviness and pain without sharpness.
But if it is in the peritoneum of the liver, there will be severe sharpness with the pain, and you see that doctors have become weary of treating it, then the patient should lie on his back, and then a cautery iron should be applied to the warm spot.
Then the iron should be heated in the fire; it is an iron resembling a plumb, and this is its image. It should be cauterized with a single application until it burns all the skin, and the cautery is continued to the peritoneum so that all the pus comes out; then it should be treated like other abscesses until it heals.
This type of cautery should not be used by anyone except one whose training in the medical profession has been long and who has repeatedly treated these diseases by experience; only then should he undertake such work, and I prefer to leave it at that.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On the cauterization of a hydrocele.
The ancients mentioned cauterization with the root of a plant called zarnad for a cold hydrocele in the following manner: Take one dried, long root of zarnad, as thick as a finger, dip it in oil and heat it in fire. Then cauterize it with a single application between the connection of the collarbone to the neck, and two small cauterizations a little below the testicles, slightly inclined towards the side under the chin, and two large cauterizations above the breasts, between the third and fourth ribs.
And two more cauterizations between the fifth and sixth ribs, slightly inclined backward, and one in the middle of the chest, and another above the stomach, and three cauterizations from behind—one between the shoulders, and two on either side of the spine below the cauterization that is between the hands.
Your hand should not be plunged deep into the wound, but rather there should be a branding on the surface of the skin. Some of the ancients have mentioned that some people used to use an iron poker resembling a millstone, heat it and insert it between the ribs until it reached the tumor itself.
And the tumor is extracted as we mentioned in connection with a liver tumor. In this instance, cauterizing with a hot iron is extremely dangerous; either the patient will die immediately, or an incurable fistula will develop at the site.
Chapter Thirty.
Cauterization of the Spleen.
If you have treated splenic disease as described in the section on treatment, and your treatment has not been successful, then cauterization may be performed in three ways, all of which are correct: one is to brand it with three or four cauterizations lined up along the length of the spleen.
These cauterizations should be done in the shape of those for the liver, as previously described, with a space between each cautery the width of a finger or slightly more. The iron should be of the same type as that used for liver cautery and your hand should not be plunged deep into the wound. The patient should lie on their back during the procedure.
The other method involves raising the skin opposite the spleen, where the patient's left elbow ends. The skin should be raised to a width equal to that of the body so that the cauterizations fall along the length of the body.
Then insert the heated cautery irons, well protected, until they penetrate through to the other side of the skin. Withdraw the irons and you will have made four cauterizations. If you prefer, you may use a three-pronged cautery iron instead.
Then treat the site of the cauterizations, allowing it to drain pus for many days. This is more effective than any of the treatments previously mentioned.
Chapter Thirty-One
On the treatment of dropsy.
Cupping is only beneficial for ascites, especially the dry type. If you have treated the patient with dropsy with the types of treatment mentioned in the division and your treatment was not successful, you should apply four cuppings around the navel and one cupping on the stomach.
Another cupping on the liver, one cupping on the spleen, and two cuppings behind his back between the shoulder blades - one opposite his chest and the other opposite his stomach. The depth of the cupping should be close to the thickness of the skin, then the cupping is left open so that pus can drain for a long time.
And do not neglect the patient's treatment after cupping with what is appropriate so that both objectives are achieved and recovery is hastened. The shape of the iron with which the back is cauterized is nail-shaped, as previously described, and the one with which the stomach is cauterized is olive-shaped.
Chapter Thirty-Two.
On cupping the feet and legs.
If the feet become swollen in a patient with dropsy and are filled with yellow water, cupping should be applied on the back of the foot in the hollow between the little toe and the next toe.
Place your hand with the iron and do not bend it, then lift your hand and do not return it at all, for yellow water will drain and the iron will be in this shape.
Then two cuppings are applied to each leg, with the cupping done with the sharp end of the iron. The cupping should be along the length of the leg, one below the knee and the other lower down towards the middle of the leg.  Two cuppings should also be applied to each thigh.
Leave the cupping open without further treatment for a long time so that water drains from it, and then treat it with all that we have mentioned.
Chapter the Thirty-Third
On Diarrhea.
If the diarrhea is from cold and moisture until it weakens the retentive and digestive powers that are in the stomach and intestines, and this is treated by types of treatment but does not recover, and you see the patient able to tolerate cupping and of abundant strength, then cup him with a large cupping on his stomach. As previously mentioned, cup the stomach with a circular iron and four small cups around the navel with a fine pin-head iron, and one large cup on the cotton above the coccyx or two.
And if you see that the moisture is abundant and the patient able to tolerate it, cup him on the groin and one on each buttock, and perhaps add two small cups to the stomach near the large one; for this is a successful treatment whose benefit will not be missed if God wills.
Chapter the Thirty-Fourth
On Cupping for Hemorrhoids.
If there are chronic hemorrhoids in the anus, many or one, and they are from thick cold humors or corrupted moisture, and treated as we mentioned in the division but to no effect, then cup the patient with three cups on the lower part of the sacral vertebrae below the waistline a little, triangularly.
And one cup under the navel, about two finger-widths apart. And if you think that his stomach has become cold and his food does not digest, and you see his face swollen, then cup him on the stomach with a large cup as previously mentioned, and one on his liver. And another on the spleen with a fine pin-head iron, and leave the cupping open for a while, then treat him until he recovers if God wills.
Chapter the Thirty-Fifth
On Cupping for Warts.
After cutting them off, heat an iron that resembles a millstone and then insert it while still hot into the same cut warts and press your hand down until the iron reaches the mouth of the vein from which blood flows; do this once or twice.
If the warts are numerous, cauterize each one as we have described, then treat them with appropriate ointments until they heal. If you cauterize a large wart on cotton, it will be more beneficial.
Chapter Thirty-Six.
On the cautery of a fistula that is in the perineum and its surroundings.
If the patient does not respond to incision and the work we have described in his place, he may be cured by cautery. And if a fistula occurs with pus and corrupted humors running from it, the first thing you should do is measure it with a thin probe.
Then determine the depth of it with the probe, then heat a cautery iron resembling a wedge and insert it heated into the same fistula, along the straight course of the fistula and to the depth that the probe entered, repeat the cautery until all those corrupted humors are burned away once or twice or thrice according to your need.
Be careful not to burn a nerve if there is one, or a large vein, and be aware if the fistula leads to the bladder or the bowels.
Be cautious of all these places, and only do this if the fistula is in a fleshy place and you have determined that it is impassable, then treat the area until it heals. If the area closes up and the discharge stops and it remains so for a time, know that it has healed completely.
And if the discharge does not stop, know that it is passable or there is a large corrupted bone in its depth, or something similar to what will be mentioned in its place.
Chapter Thirty-Seven.
On the cautery of the kidneys.
If pain occurs in the kidneys due to cold or harsh wind and a consequent lack of union in the patient, you should cauterize it on the two sides on the same kidney with a pointed cautery iron that has been previously mentioned; perhaps you may cauterize it three times in the same session, resulting in three cauteries in a row which will be most beneficial.
Chapter Thirty-Eight.
On the Bladder.
If weakness and relaxation occur in the bladder due to cold and moisture, so that the patient cannot retain urine, make incisions on the lower part of the perineum, above the bladder, where the pubic hair begins. Make one incision on the right side of the perineum and another on the left.
Each incision should be as long as a thumb joint. Make another incision in the lower back, or two if necessary. The cautery iron should be pointed as previously described.
Chapter Thirty-Nine.
On Cauterization of the Womb.
If a substance of cold and moisture develops in the womb, preventing conception, corrupting its humor, and hindering its functions, or if pain occurs during menstruation, then three incisions should be made around the perineum as described for cauterization of the bladder. One incision should be made on the cotton under the lower back, or two if necessary. The cautery iron should be pointed.
Chapter Forty.
On Cauterization for Hip Displacement.
Erroneous humors may accumulate around the hip joint, causing it to dislocate. The sign is that one leg will be longer than the other when measured. You will find a gap where the dislocation has occurred. A semi-circular incision should be made on the hip itself.
The incision should be made so that the center of the circle coincides with the affected area, following its curvature. The cautery iron previously described for cauterizing the stomach should be used. If this is not available, make three incisions with a large olive-shaped cautery iron.
The incision should be deep enough to penetrate the entire thickness of the skin. Then, treat it until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Forty-One.
On the cauterization of the sciatic nerve.
If pain occurs in the hip and its cause is cold and moisture, and the patient has been treated as mentioned in the division but the treatment is unsuccessful and prolonged, then it is necessary to lighten the thick humors of the patient with marshmallow or colocynth, or the like.
Then cauterize and direct the cautery in two ways, either with burning medications or with fire. Cautery with fire is done in many ways, one of which is to cauterize on the hip itself three triangular cauteries in this shape.
Make the depth suitable, and after each cautery and the next, make a distance equal to the thickness of a finger. The iron should be olive-shaped. Sometimes one cautery may be applied in the middle on the top of the hip itself, making four cauteries. If you wish, you may apply it with the circle previously mentioned for dislocation of the hip, using one cautery.
Make the circle encompass the entire hip and burn all of the skin's thickness. This is the characteristic of cautery for sciatica if the pain persists in the hip itself and does not extend to the thigh and leg.
But if it extends to the thigh and leg, apply two cauteries on the thigh at the location the patient indicates with pain, and one cautery above the popliteal fossa by four fingers towards the ulnar side. The iron should be thin and the depth of the cautery should be only as thick as the skin.
If they indicate that the pain extends towards the toes of the foot, cauterize where they point with a punctate iron three, four or more times if needed. If they point to pain below the knee towards the leg, apply one thin cautery there.
Take care in all your cauteries not to burn a nerve or large blood vessel, as this could cause the patient a severe injury or disability.
I have seen one or two people who cauterized above the ankle and put a poultice on the cautery, causing the leg to swell until the swelling reached the foot, with all of it becoming perforated and spoiling all of the limb; then diarrhea and death occurred afterward. If the pain is on both sides, cauterize them in this exact same manner.
Some of the wise scholars have mentioned that when cauterizing the hip, it should be done in this manner: they make a sort of crucible from iron with a diameter of half a hand-span. At its mouth, it should be as thick as the pit of a date or slightly less. Inside this crucible, there is another crucible and a third one within that.
The space between each pair of crucibles should be the width of a thumb joint. The crucibles should be open on both sides and their height should be about a handbreadth or two. A handle made of iron is firmly attached to the crucibles, and this is its form.
Then it is heated in the fire until it glows red and the sparks are removed. It is then placed on the hip bone and sciatic nerve, with the patient leaning on the proper side, cauterizing three circular burns at the same time. Then it is left for three days and dressed with butter. The wound is left open for several days.
It is then treated with ointment until it heals. The author of this book said that this type of cautery is rarely used due to its ugliness and horrifying appearance, as well as the scarcity of those who can endure it; however, it is a good cautery for those who can bear it and are struck in the proper place.
As for cautery with burning medications, it is done by making two crucibles shaped like funnels, into which the retorted sublimated mercury (rat tail) should be poured, made of either copper or iron.
Their rims should be about two handbreadths high, and the space between them should be about the width of a finger. They should be open at the bottom and open at the top, with some being soldered together in this manner.
Then you lower it onto the right hip and thigh while the patient lies on his right side. Hold your hand firmly, then pour between the two circles hot, sharp water heated by a small fire pot, and hold it for a quarter of an hour. The patient will endure its sting and burning, for he will find pleasure like fire.
Until the sting subsides, then lift your hand with the instrument and wipe away all the sharp water from the hip with fresh water. Then leave it for three days and apply butter to it until the blackness of the burn is gone. Leave it for several days until pus flows from it, for this is most beneficial.
Then treat it with ointments until it heals. If the pain extends to the thigh or leg, make him a mold that holds water according to what you made for the hip. This is the description of sharp water, and its preparation has been proven in the treatise on the Correction of Medicines.
Take a portion of saltpeter and quicklime, each one part, crush them together, and put them in a new pot which has been pierced with a single small hole at the bottom so that the mordant enters it, and place another glazed pot beneath the base of the first pot.
Pour fresh water over the saltpeter and quicklime to cover them by a finger, after firmly squeezing them with your hand. Leave the pot until the sharp water descends into the glazed pot below, then collect all of that water and pour it over other new quicklime and saltpeter.
It will then be very sharp and effective for many medical purposes and for cauterizing all other organs, as it acts like fire itself. There are also medicines with which the hip can be cauterized, such as thapsia, bladder honey, lime mixed with soap, and crushed together.
Galen relates a story about an ancient man who treated hip pain and sciatica, greatly exaggerating its effectiveness and claiming that it needed no other treatment and healed in a single application.
Even it is possible to introduce him into the bath carried and he comes out of it fully healed. It is to be taken from the green plaster, if there is no green one, then the fresh dry one is taken, finely pounded with a little fat and placed on the hip where the pain is or in the leg or thigh.
It is tightened and left for three hours or as long as the patient feels relief from the burning, then he is introduced into the bath. If his body sweats, he is put into the basin, for the pain will go away and he will be healed by God's permission. If he is not healed, the poultice is applied to him again after ten days once more.
It will heal, God willing, and know that no one should use cautery with these remedies except after the body has evacuated, and Dioscorides mentioned that if goat hair is used to cauterize sciatica, it will be beneficial.
The cautery is done in this manner: wool soaked in old oil is placed on the deep spot between the thumb of the hand and the wrist, which is closer to the wrist, then take a dry goat hair.
Ignite it with fire until it becomes a coal, then place it on the wool and leave it until it goes out, then take another one and continue to do so until the sensation reaches from the middle of the upper arm to the hip and the pain subsides by God's permission. This type of cautery is called hair cautery.
Chapter forty-two.
On the cautery for back pain.
Back pain may occur for many reasons, such as a fall or blow or excessive purging and the like, and it is due to a buildup of cold, wet matter. Cautery falls into this category alone.
That which is due to a buildup of cold matter, after the patient has evacuated with hellebore or the like, should be cauterized on his back where the pain is, in three rows across the table itself, having first marked the location with ink. In each row there should be five cauteries or more according to what you see as the patient's endurance and strength.
It should be cauterized with a pointed iron, and if you wish, cauterize it three or four times with a medium-sized nail iron in this manner.
Chapter Thirty Third.
In the beginning of cauterization, a swelling.
This condition often affects young children, and its initial sign in a child is shortness of breath upon standing and moving. You will find at the end of his back vertebrae a knot that has protruded beyond all other knots.
If you see this and want to stop it, cauterize it with a round iron in this manner. Let the cautery be applied to each side of the vertebra evenly, and if you wish, cauterize it around the vertebra with a pointed iron in two or three rows, making sure the dots are close together. Then treat the area until it heals as we have described.
And be careful to use cautery on the swelling that is from nerve constriction.
Chapter Forty Fourth.
On cauterization for gout and joint pains.
If joint pains are from cold humors settling into any part of the body, and if the pains occur in the legs, it is customary for physicians to call this gout, especially.
If gout, which is caused by cold, is treated with the types of treatment mentioned in the division and the pains do not go away, then cautery will remove them. Cauterize around the joint of the leg with many cauteries after vomiting, using a medium-sized olive iron in this manner.
And if you need to cauterize the front of the leg, do so with a pointed iron. If the pains rise to the knees or other joints, which often happens, then cauterize each knee three or four times on each side with this same olive iron.
If you need more than this, cauterize it and do not delve deep with the cautery, but let it be about the thickness of the skin. If the pain ascends to the thighs or back, use what we have mentioned about cautery in its chapter.
If the pain is only in the hands, we place dots around the joints as if drawing two lines. If the pain remains in the fingers, we place a dot on each knuckle and on the back of the hand. If the pain ascends after a few days to the elbows or shoulders.
Cauterize them from all sides, and the patient should not be without good treatment and medication. For if he eats well and expels phlegm, he will recover with this cautery, God willing.
Chapter forty-five.
On the Cauterization of the Foot.
If a hernia appears in the groin and some of the intestine and fat descend to the testicle, and this is recent and slight, then you should order the patient to abstain from food for a day and use laxatives that will expel feces from his bowels.
Then he lies down on his back between your hands and you order him to hold himself so that the fat or intestine protrudes, then push it back in with your finger. Then mark beneath the hernia on the pubic bone a sign resembling half a circle with its ends pointing upward.
Then heat an iron in this shape.
Until it glows white, emitting sparks, then push the intestine or fat back into its place, and have a servant put his hand on the spot so that the intestines do not protrude again; having parted the patient's legs and placed a cushion underneath him, and another servant holds his legs.
Another servant is on his chest holding his hands, then lower the iron onto the mark itself, hold it upright and steady until it reaches the bone or repeat it again if it didn't reach the bone the first time.
Preserve your effort from the protrusion of the intestine while cauterizing, lest you burn it and thereby cause the patient either death or great suffering. Know that if you do not reach the bone with the cautery, your work will not succeed, and the cautery iron should be gentle according to their ages, both for children and adults.
Then treat the cauterized spot after three days with butter until the dryness of the fire goes away, then treat it with all ointments until it heals, and let the patient lie on his back for forty days until the wound closes.
You should make his diet during his treatment consist of things that soften his stomach so that the intestine does not protrude when straining and defecating. Then, if he wants to get up after forty days, use a tight bandage and keep it for another forty days, and reduce exertion, fullness from food and drink, and loud shouting.
Indeed, if he follows this treatment, he will recover completely, God willing. I will mention the treatment of hernia by incision in its chapter, God willing. As for the hernia that occurs in the rest of the abdomen and is in its beginning, if you want it not to increase, cauterize a circular part from it according to its size. Let what you burn of the skin be like a third of it, then treat it as we have mentioned; it will not increase, God willing. Some of the ancients mentioned cauterizing the hernia with a triangular iron in this shape.
After you learn about the hernia by drawing a line across the upper side of the hernia and another line downward, place your hand in the middle and cauterize it with one, nail-like cautery; the first cautery is easier and better, God willing.
Chapter Forty-Six.
On Cauterizing a Boil.
If pain occurs in some organs due to a fall or blow, and that pain lasts for a long time, and medication does not alleviate it, then it should be cauterized with a pointed iron, the cautery being applied to each organ according to its size, weakness, and strength.
If the pain and inflammation are relieved by the first cautery, then he is cured. Otherwise, it should be repeated, for it is customary for these pains to spread from one organ to another nearby, so cautery should follow them until the patient is cured.
Chapter Forty-Seven.
On Cauterizing Leprosy.
As for lepers, they may greatly benefit from cautery, especially those with leprosy caused by the putrefaction of phlegm and black bile.
If you wish to cauterize him, examine it first. If the leprosy is in its early stages and you have treated it with what we mentioned in the division, but it has not subsided or stopped, and you fear that corruption will seize all his humors, then apply five cauteries to his head. One in the usual middle of the head, the second below it towards the forehead at the end of the hair, two on the temples, and one behind on the nape. Apply the cautery a little more strongly until it has a slight effect on the bone.
Scales will peel off from it, making it easier for the thick vapors to escape. Cauterize the spleen in the same way as described above. If the leprosy has persisted in the patient and has appeared clearly, then these cauteries that we mentioned should be applied to the head. Apply a cautery to the tip of the nose, two on the cheeks, two on the nape, six on the vertebrae of the back, and one large cautery on the coccyx at the base of the tailbone, another above it in the same area, and two on the calves, one on each. Two on the knees, one on each knee, two on the elbows, two on the elbows, two on the shoulder joints and one on each joint of the fingers of his hands and feet, a number on each heel of his feet and his wrists.
And preserve from the nerve that is on the back of the heels lest you burn them, and it may be cauterized on the pubic bone and another on the mouth of the stomach and another on the liver, and know that the more you increase cautery the more beneficial and effective it is.
And know that the patient does not find pain from cautery as the healthy person does because his body has become numb, and the cauteries should be of large and small size according to the limbs and joints as previously mentioned of the qualities of the irons.
Then treat the cautery with frankincense gum mixed with honey and all other treatments until it heals, God willing.
Chapter forty-eight.
In cauterizing numbness, when a limb from the limbs is numbed and treated with medicines, ointments and bandages and does not recover, cauterize the same numb limb with cauteries according to what it deserves, whether the bone is large or small, and let your cautery be shallow and penetrate the skin slightly.
Then treat it with ointment until it heals, and sometimes cauterize some of the numbness that occurs in the hand and foot in cases of back pain at the exit of the nerve that moves that limb, so the numbness goes away.
And let no one undertake this except who is insightful in the anatomy of organs and the exits of the nerves that move the body.
Chapter forty-nine.
On cauterizing leprosy.
If leprosy has advanced and no medical remedy is effective against it, cauterize it with a shallow cautery according to the thickness of the skin so that the whiteness disappears and its color changes.
Then treat it with lentil flour mixed with rose oil, plantain leaf, and the blood of a pigeon or finch, one part from each, all mixed together and spread on a cloth, applied to the area until healed.
Chapter Fifty.
On the Removal of Cancer.
If the cancer is in its early stages and you wish to stop it, cauterize it with a circular iron, turning it around the growth. Some sages have mentioned cauterizing a significant area in its center, but I do not approve of this as I expect it to ulcerate; I have witnessed this several times.
The proper method is to cauterize around it in a circle, as mentioned, or with many cauterizations.
Chapter Fifty-One.
On Cauterizing the Hemorrhoid.
If someone has a hemorrhoid that is slow to mature, either due to the effectiveness of the factor causing it, or because of the patient's age if they are elderly and have little blood, or simply due to time, and you wish to hasten its maturation, cauterize around it with many small, dotted cauterizations.
Then leave it alone; it will hasten its maturation. If you wish to cauterize it, use an iron shaped like this.
Lower it into the center of the hemorrhoid until it pierces the skin, and let the cauterization be such that pus can easily flow. Then treat it accordingly until it heals.
Chapter Fifty-Two.
On Cauterizing a Fistula.
A fistula is a corruption that progresses through the body, consuming it like fire consumes dry wood. If you see a fistula in a location suitable for cauterization, apply many small and large pointed irons according to what is appropriate for the location of the fistula.
Then pare it from all sides until all the corruption is eradicated and nothing of it remains, then leave it for three days and apply powdered sulfur mixed with oil to the cauterized areas until all the scabs and corruption are removed.
Then treat it with flesh-producing ointments, and if after three days you see that the flesh is growing healthily without corruption, then leave it. Otherwise, cauterize what remains of the corrupt areas, and the ulcer may be treated with a caustic medicine.
For it acts as fire, more quickly beneficial, and I have mentioned treating it with a caustic medicine in the division, so take it from there when you need it.
Chapter thirty-three.
On the cauterization of reversed and unreversed nails.
This condition often occurs in the soles of the feet, and it is something rough that pains a man. Cauterization for this can be done in two ways: either by fire or by caustic water.
As for cauterization by fire, heat a hollow iron tool shaped like an eagle's feather, made of iron to the extent that it surrounds the nail from all sides and is thin at the edge. Then lower it while hot onto the nail and turn your hand with the iron around the nail until the iron reaches the depth of the nail.
Leave it for three days so that it swells with pus, then bandage it with wild barley flour pounded with salt and leave the dressing on for a night, as it will come off from its roots. Then treat the wound site with flesh-producing ointment until it heals. If the nails are not reversed.
This often occurs on the surface of the body, especially in the hands and feet. You should take a tube made of copper or iron or an eagle feather quill and lower it onto the nail or wart. Then pour a small amount of caustic water into the tube.
Hold your hand and turn it while squeezing slightly with your hand so that the tube's edge touches the base of the nail, allowing the sharp water to find its way to the base of the nail. The patient should endure the sting of the sharp water for a moment.
Then you leave it, and the nail will detach from its base. You do this one by one until you have removed all of them from the body, then you treat the spots after they are removed with ointments that promote flesh growth.
Chapter Forty-Five.
In the Removal of the Nail.
If someone suffers from a nail affected by cold in the nerve or from a fever, a quarter of it or something else, then four or five cauterizations should be applied on the back of the neck between each cauterization and one on its chest. And a cauterization with an olive iron should be applied to the stomach, for this will soothe the nail and hasten the ripening of the cold ailment.
Chapter Fifty-Five.
On Cauterization of a Swelling Occurring in the Body.
Ugly boils may erupt in the body, originating from thick, putrid cold matter; upon their first appearance, a gentle cauterization should be applied to the head of each boil with a slender rod whose tip has been ignited by fire or with the root of long asarabacca or an lentil-sized iron.
Similar cauterization may be applied to the mammillary glands upon their initial eruption in this manner so that it will not worsen, and the beneficial effect will dissipate, and the patient will recover. However, this should be done only after the patient has undergone bloodletting.
Chapter Fifty-Six.
On Cauterization of Hemorrhage Occurring Upon Severing an Artery.
Hemorrhage from a severed artery often occurs during external wounding, incision of a tumor, cauterization of a limb, or similar procedures, making it difficult to stop the bleeding.
If this should happen to anyone, hurry your hand to the mouth of the artery and put your index finger on it and press it down firmly until the blood is stopped under your finger and nothing comes out. Then place several small and large olive pits in the fire and blow on them until they become very hot.
Then take one, either small or large according to the wound and the place where the artery has burst open, and lower the pit on the vein itself after quickly removing your finger, and hold the pit until the blood stops.
If the blood rushes out when you lift your finger from the mouth of the artery and the pit goes out, take another pit quickly from those prepared in the fire, and continue doing this one after another until the bleeding stops.
Be careful not to burn a nerve that may be there, for it will cause the patient further suffering. Know that if blood flows from an artery, it cannot be cut, especially if it is a large artery, except in one of four ways: either by cauterization as we have said or by amputation if it has not already been amputated. For if it is amputated, its ends will contract and the bleeding will stop. It can then either be tied tightly with threads or have medicines applied that staunch the blood and bandages tightened firmly around it.
As for whoever tries to cut it with a ligature or by tightening cloth or applying burnt objects, and the like, the bleeding will not stop at all except in rare cases. If someone experiences this and does not have access to a doctor or medicine, let them quickly place their index finger on the mouth of the wound itself as we have described.
Press it down very hard so that the blood is confined and flows from above the wound onto the artery and finger. Do not remove it with cold water, always keep pressing until the blood freezes, thickens and stops. During this time, look for what you need in terms of cauterization or medicine.
Chapter Two.
On incision, scarification, bloodletting and surgery, and the like.
He who came before us has said that in the first chapter we mentioned every disease which can be treated with fire cautery and burning medicaments, its causes and reasons, its instruments, and the figures of the cauteries, and we have made this into chapters from beginning to end.
And I follow in this chapter the same path so that it is easy for the student to attain his goal.
Before I begin with that, you should know, my sons, that this chapter contains more marvels than the first chapter on cautery.
Therefore, caution should be even greater in this chapter because the work often involves vomiting blood, which sustains life, when an artery is opened or an incision is made on a tumor, abscess is lanced, surgery is performed, an arrow is removed, or an incision is made for a stone, and the like. All of these are fraught with danger and fear, and death often results from them.
And I advise you against acting in doubtful matters, for in this profession you may encounter people with various diseases; some of them may be weary of their illness and find death preferable.
Because of the severity of their sickness and the length of their suffering, some are determined to die, and from those who may offer you wealth to provide them with health, though their illness is formidable - do not help those who approach you in this manner.
Let your caution be greater than your desire and eagerness, and do not undertake anything except after you have certain knowledge that will lead to a favorable outcome. In all treatments for your patients, use the advance of knowledge and warning as guides to safety.
For this will help you gain praise, glory, renown, and gratitude. May God inspire you with righteousness, my sons, and not deprive you of truth and success, for that is in His hand; there is no god but He.
This chapter has been arranged in sections, from the century to antiquity, on what was mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, so that you may easily find what you desire, God willing.
The First Chapter.
On the treatment of water that gathers in the heads of boys.
This ailment often occurs in boys at birth if the midwife presses the boy's head without gentleness, and it may also arise from a hidden cause that is unknown.
I have not seen this ailment in anyone other than boys, and all those whom I have seen with it quickly succumbed to death; therefore, I decided to abandon working on it. I once saw a boy whose head was filled with water, and the head grew larger every day. Until the boy could no longer sit up due to the size of his head, and the moisture increased until he died.
This moisture either gathers between the skin and the bone, or it gathers under the bone on the peritoneum. The procedure for this is as follows: if the moisture is between the skin and the bone and the swelling is small, one should make a single cut across the middle of the head, about two spans long, so that the moisture may flow out. This is the shape of the surgical knife.
But if there is more moisture and the swelling is larger, make two intersecting cuts in this pattern:
And if the moisture is under the bone, a sign of which is that you see the sutures of the skull open on all sides and the water descends when you press it inward with your hand - this is not subtle - then make three cuts in the middle of the head in this pattern:
After making the incision, all the moisture will come out; then tighten the incisions with cloths and bandages, and then soak them from above with syrup and oil until the fifth day. Then untie the bandages and treat the wound with poultices and ointments. Do not leave the head tightly bandaged, and feed the patient with dry food that is low in moisture.
Until the member is strengthened and healed, another prescription for incision is to look where the bone appears at the meeting of the water, for it may be more in the back of the head or in its front, or on the right or on the left.
So you intend by incision where the swelling appeared to you and the filling of water, so cut it as much as you can while preserving that you do not cut an artery so that bleeding occurs and the patient dies from that bleeding along with vomiting moisture.
Chapter Two.
Cutting the arteries behind the ears, known as the carotids.
When one is stricken with acute colds to the eyes or chest, and it persists and medication does not work, then cutting these two arteries is prescribed as treatment.
If you wish to cut them, then shave the patient's head with a razor, and then rub the spot with a coarse cloth so that the artery appears. Then tighten the patient's neck by pulling on their garment, and look where the pulse beats and the two depressions are located behind the ears, for they seldom hide except in some people.
Then mark them with ink, and cut them with a dissecting knife down to the bone, doing so across the head. If you wish, insert the knife under the artery and trace it upwards with cuts, making the length of the incision about two fingers' breadth.
