For when the artery is severed, blood comes out in a throbbing, pulsating manner, surging forward and back repeatedly. If the artery does not appear to your senses, then you should estimate three finger breadths behind the ear, mark it with ink, and cut down to the bone.
Six ounces of blood are sent on the average, and perhaps more or less may be sent, all according to the apparent strength of the patient and the fullness of his arteries; then look at the wound, and if any part of the peritoneum remains upon the bone, cut it off.
So that a hot tumor may not develop, then draw the wound together with a linen wick and treat it with ointments until it heals.
Chapter Three.
Cut the arteries in the fingers.
If a person suffers from chronic migraine or acute attacks brought on by humid heat and muscular pains in the fingers, or severe chronic headache, and is treated with the various remedies of medicine without success, we have found in these cases that cutting or cauterizing the arteries of the fingers as described is effective.
The procedure for cutting them is as follows: the patient should shave the hair on his fingers, and then aim for the superficial artery in the finger; it will be apparent to you by its pulse, and it rarely escapes notice except in a few people.
Or if the cold is severe and it is hidden from view, have the patient constrict his neck with the help of his garment, then rub the spot with a cloth or compress it with hot water until the artery becomes clearly visible. Then take the scalpel, which is pictured here. Then gently peel back the skin with it until you reach the artery; insert a snare and pull it upward until it emerges from the skin, freeing it from the fascia underneath on all sides. If the artery is thin, twist it with the end of the snare. Then cut off a portion of it, as far apart as its ends can retract without bleeding; for if it is not completely severed, the blood will not recede. Then draw off from six to three ounces of blood.
If the artery is large, it should be tied in two places with a strong doubled thread. The thread should be either silk or from musical instrument strings so that gangrene does not hasten towards it before the wound heals and bleeding occurs.
Then cut the part between the ligatures, doing so at that hour or a later time. If you wish, cauterize it close to the bone with a searing iron until its ends are severed. This will serve the same purpose or even better, as we have said.
However, if the patient has a fever or an unbalanced temperament, cauterization is more beneficial because it helps destroy moisture. After ligating the arteries, the area should be packed with soft cotton and covered with tight bandages. After the ligatures are removed, treat it with dry medications that promote flesh growth and apply a bandage until it heals, God willing.
If bleeding occurs from the artery during your work, hasten to cut it either by cauterization or fill the area with alum and press firmly until the blood stops. If none of these are available, place your finger there until the blood clots.
Rinse the area with very cold water to soothe the burning and bandage it properly. It is lighter and easier than ligating the artery to cauterize the vein with this searing iron after marking the locations with ink.
Lower it while very hot until it reaches the bone and the vein is severed in two places so that what is between its ends does not join at all with this cauterization. This is an image of the iron.
The searing irons are sharp like small blades, but they are much less sharp than a knife because if they were as sharp as a knife, cold would set in quickly and the flesh would not cut easily. If there is any thickness, it will retain the heat of the fire.
The flesh is cut quickly, and this is the best of all actions, the lightest and easiest, and let the space between the knives be the thickness of a finger.
Chapter Four.
In the treatment of continuous hot tearing into the eyes.
If the tears are continuous and flow from the veins on the head, outside, and are not benefited by any medical treatment with drugs, and you see the face of the patient has reddened. And he feels a tingling in his forehead like the crawling of ants and his eyes are shaking, wet, their healing has been confirmed and their eyelids have become stuck together from the severity of the tears, so the first ones in treating it resorted to this action, and they call it treatment by the sword, which is that you order the patient to shave the hair on his forehead.
Then three parallel incisions are made in the forehead, along its length, each incision being about two finger lengths long. The first incision is parallel to the length of the nose, in the middle of the forehead, the second is a short distance away from the movement of the muscle in the temple and the third is on the other side.
Be careful not to cut the arteries on either side and keep your hand away from the connection of the jaws.  Let there be three joined fingers' distance between each incision, and have absorbent cotton pieces or many dry cloths ready to soak up the blood.
Then insert the sharp double-pointed trocar which is pictured here. From the incision next to the temple to the middle incision, and scrape off all the skin between them along with the membrane on the bone. Then do the same from the middle incision to the third, then remove the trocar. And insert into the first incision another instrument called a knife, sharp on one side and smooth and blunt on the other. This is its picture:
And its sharp side is turned upwards towards the flesh adhering to the skin and its smooth side towards the bone, and it is pushed until it reaches the middle incision, and all the vessels descending from the head to the eyes are cut with it without the cut reaching the outer surface of the skin.
Then you do the same in the middle incision to the other incision, and after a moderate amount of blood has flowed, you press on the places where the clotted blood is cut, then you place a wick of cotton in each incision and put a dressing soaked in syrup and oil or vinegar and oil on it.
So that a hot swelling does not occur, and on the third day the ligature is removed and you use a lot of rinsing with lukewarm water, then treat it with balsam of Peru ointment mixed with rose oil and all that which you treat wounds with until it heals, if God wills.
Chapter Five.
On the treatment of tears and discharge to the eyes from within the head.
When someone has frequent, sharp, stinging discharges and sees their eyes as two small, weak things, and their eyelids are ulcerated and their eyelashes fall out. And there is a sharp, painful ache deep in the head and continuous sneezing, then know from these symptoms that those substances and discharges come from many deep vessels, and the best treatment for it is this: order the patient to shave their forehead, then make one incision in the middle of the forehead.
And there is a sharp, painful ache deep in the head and continuous sneezing, then know from these symptoms that those substances and discharges come from many deep vessels, and the best treatment for it is this: order the patient to shave their forehead, then make one incision in the middle of the forehead. Or raise it a little horizontally and begin the incision from the left temple to the right temple, the incision being down to the bone, keeping your hand furthest from the temporal muscles that move when chewing, until the bone is exposed and all the blood has been dried with a sponge.
Then separate the edges of the incision with loose cotton or a linen twist, then tighten it from above with bandages and soak the bandages in syrup and oil so that a hot swelling does not occur. When you remove them and see that the hot swelling has subsided, you should rub the bone until the flesh begins to grow there.
Then treat it with a drying regimen that promotes flesh growth, such as taking two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony, and making an ointment from them to be used in the growth of flesh in such wounds.
Chapter Six.
In the treatment of what falls into the ear.
Everything that falls into the ear is one of four types: either a mineral stone or something like stone, such as iron and glass; or a plant seed such as chickpeas, kernels, and the like; or something liquid such as water, vinegar, and the like.
Or an animal. When a pebble or something like a pebble falls into the ear and does not exceed what is in the ear, expose the ear to the sun; if you see the pebble only smear some violet oil or cerumen on it, then try to remove it with a head movement or sneezing with a cane. And plug the nostrils when sneezing after placing a band of cloth or wool around the ear and extending the ear upwards, for it often comes out with this treatment. If it does not come out, then try to remove it with a fine hook, which is pictured here.
If it comes out with the hook, or else try to remove it with a fine, flexible blind fishing line. If it does not come out with that, then make a copper tube and insert the end of the tube into the ear canal properly.
Seal what is around the tube with softened pitch so that the wind has no path except through the tube, then draw it with your breath in a strong pull and it often comes out this way. If it does not come out as we have described, then take a little mastic gum or prepared birdlime used to catch birds and place it at the end of the rod.
After wrapping it tightly with cotton, then insert it gently into the ear canal after drying the ear from moisture. If it does not come out with everything we have described, hurry to incise before a hot swelling or spasm occurs.
The description of the incision is as follows: you should first make a cut on the patient's temple and let out blood according to his strength. Then, sit the patient before you, turn his ear upwards and make a small incision at the base of the ear near its cartilage in the lowest part.
The incision should be crescent-shaped until you reach the stone, then remove it as best you can with instruments. Then quickly sew up the incision and treat it until it heals.
If the object that has fallen into the ear is one of the grains that swell and become engorged, try to remove it as we have mentioned. If it does not come out, take a fine, delicate scoop like this.
Try to cut that type of grain that has fallen into the ear with it, but only do so if you are certain that the grain has become moistened by the ear's vapor until it becomes many small pieces. Then remove them with a blind syringe or a fine forceps or by suction.
As we mentioned, it is easy to remove it. As for water that has entered the ear, the patient should first sneeze with a reed, having filled his ear with some soft cotton while lying on the ear that contains the water; if it comes out that way.
Otherwise, take many smooth, flat pebbles the length of your fingers and warm them slightly in a fire. The patient should insert one of these into the hole of his ear, leaning on one leg from that side and striking another stone against the stone in the ear.
He should continue to do this with stone after stone until all the water comes out. The water may also come out by taking a blade of grass or a feather and inserting one end into the ear, while burning the other end with fire so that most of it is charred; then repeat with another feather.
Do this several times until all the water comes out or draw it out with a tube as mentioned previously in the case of calculi. As for removing the animal inside, look and see if it is small-bodied like a flea or similar. If so, treat it as I have mentioned in the section on division.
If its body is large and perceptible to the senses, try to extract it with forceps and hooks. This is easier than anything that becomes lodged in the ear. As for extracting worms generated in the ear, if you have treated them as mentioned in the section on division in the article on catarrhs, and your treatment has not been successful.
You should then look into the ear in the sunlight. If some of the worm appears to you, extract it with forceps or fine hooks. If nothing appears, take a tube shaped like this:
Narrow at the bottom and wide at the top, insert the narrow end into the ear as far as the patient can tolerate, then suck strongly. Do this several times until all the worms come out. If they do not come out for you, seal around the tube with wax as I have mentioned to you in the case of calculi.
If they do not come out as mentioned, use the catarrhs that I have mentioned, which were tried by those who came before to kill worms. You will find this in the article on catarrhs. Pour your ointments and medicines into the ear with this instrument: and this is its shape:
As you see, it is made of silver or brass, narrow at the bottom with a small hole, and wide at the top. If you wish, the cannon in the inside of the tube can be made of solid brass. If you wish, you can take a lead pipe and wrap cotton tightly around its end.
Then pour the ointment or juice or whatever medicine you want into the tube while it is in the ear. Then insert the lead pipe with cotton from above and squeeze it moderately with your hand until the ointment is driven into the auditory canal and he feels it.
Pour into the ear of the patient, and let what you pour in have been slightly warmed in the fire; be careful that what is poured into it is not too cold or too hot, for the ear cannot bear it.
Chapter Seven.
In the Treatment of Deafness Affecting the Ear.
Some children may be born from their mothers with their ear canals not pierced.
It may also happen that some people have an obstruction in the ear canal from a wound or growing flesh, and this obstruction may be deep within the ear hole, undetectable to the sight.
It may also protrude slightly from the hole and be visible, but what occurs deep in the ear and is undetectable by sight is usually difficult to cure. What is visible should be treated by placing the patient's ear in the sun. Examine it, and if you see the obstruction, open it with a delicate perforator which has this form:
Its tip should be slightly wide, and the rest of the perforator smooth on both sides so as not to injure the ear. If the obstruction is flesh that has grown in the ear canal, grasp it with a fine snare and cut it off carefully until all the flesh is removed.
If the obstruction is deep in the ear, take a fine, smooth probe, heat it slightly in the fire, and insert it into the ear canal. If you feel the obstruction and the patient experiences difficulty hearing, gently push it.
Be careful not to injure the nerve of the ear, and then place in the ear a wick equal to the width of the hole, having saturated it with Egyptian ointment. Continue this for several days until you are sure that the wound has healed or take a wick, soak it in water, and sprinkle powdered glass on it.
Use this, and if you see that the ear has become swollen and inflamed, remove the wick and replace it with another one saturated with a liniment made of rose oil so as to soothe the inflammation, and then treat it until it heals.
If bleeding occurs, immerse a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it on the ear, and use all the treatment that stops the aforementioned bleeding mentioned in the article on catarrhs.
Chapter Eight.
In the treatment of warts that appear on the eyelids.
These warts that appear on the eyelids may be wet or dry, so they should be grasped with forceps or a snare and cut at their base with a scalpel, then powdered alum should be applied to the area.
If blood flows from them, cauterize it with a gentle lentil-sized iron, preferably after cutting. It often recurs if anything remains of its root. If cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and do not return.
Chapter Nine.
In the treatment of cold affecting the eyelids.
It often appears in the eyelids something resembling a cold in its intensity and stiffness.
This is why it is called a cold, which is a gathering of thick moisture in the upper and lower eyelid. The procedure is to examine it. If the swelling protrudes on the surface of the eyelid and moves in all directions, then its treatment is easy: make a transverse incision over it.
Then carefully peel it off from all sides until you are free of it, and hook it with a snare and cut it. If you cannot cut it except after penetrating the eyelid, this will not harm the patient at all. If the incision is large, bring it together with stitches and treat it until it heals.
If it is small, there is no harm in leaving it, as the ointment will bind and seal it. If the swelling is inclined towards the inside of the eyelid towards the inner surface, turn the eyelid over and hook the swelling with a snare without needing to make an incision, and cut it from all sides.
If you cut the eyelid, it will not harm anything. Then wash the eye after cutting away the bandage with salt water and treat the area with something that heals until the patient recovers.
Chapter Ten.
In the treatment of ophthalmia that occurs in the eyelid.
Ophthalmia is a redness that occurs in the layers of the upper eyelid and it mostly affects boys. It weighs down their eyes and makes them susceptible to colds at night, and they are unable to look at sunlight. So that tears flow quickly to them.
Therefore, you see them leaning on their faces always and sleeping on them, with their eyelids wet under the eyebrows, swollen, and the ophthalmia clearly visible. When you press the area with your fingers, you feel the ophthalmia between them. The procedure is to have the patient place his head in your lap.
Then take a linen cloth and prepare a wick from it, making a circle from that wick large enough to enclose the ophthalmia on all sides. Place it over the ophthalmia and press with your fingers on all sides to gather the ophthalmia in the center of the circle.
Then cut in the center of that moisture with a lancet, making a horizontal incision no larger than the one made during venesection. In depth, however, you should cut through all the skin until you reach the ophthalmia.
Often, the ophthalmia will protrude from the incision in the form of a white lump of fat. Grab it with a cloth wrapped between your fingers and pull it outward while turning your hand right and left until it is removed. Be careful not to widen the incision lest the lancet touch the eye and injure it.
If the ophthalmia does not appear in the first incision, you should carefully widen the incision slightly until it emerges. Then grasp it as we have said, and soak a cloth in vinegar and water, place it on the area, and bind it with a bandage. Some people crush salt instead.
And place it in the wound so that the remaining moisture dissolves, then treat it until it heals. If a hot swelling occurs at the site, treat it with cooling poultices until it is healed.
Chapter Eleven.
On Kinds of Ocular Cauterization.
If excessive eyelashes grow on the eyelid, beyond the natural course, beneath the normal lashes and become inflamed, they will harm the eye and cause various diseases such as constant tearing, relaxation of the eyelids, leukocoria, and opacity, to such an extent that it may lead to blindness.
Cauterization of the eye is performed in four ways: either by cautery with fire, or with a caustic drug as previously mentioned in the chapter on cautery; or by excision and suture, or by means of a reed, as I have already described.
You should place the patient's head in your lap and then flip the eyelid with your left hand. If it flips, that is sufficient; otherwise, insert a needle with thread from below the eyelid and pass the needle with the thread through from above. This should be done near the eyelashes themselves. Draw the thread upward along the eyelid.
Turn it with a speculum, then incise the inner part of the eyelid without the excess eyelashes using a scalpel from the larger forceps to the smaller ones. Then loosen the thread and place a small dressing of cotton or linen underneath the eyelid. Finally, apply a bandage to the eyelid with mastic.
Make it like the shape of an ace leaf, but the shape should be according to how much you want to lift the eyelid because people differ in this; some may need you to cut a suitable amount from the eyelid depending on how relaxed it is.
Others may need less, all depending on the relaxation of the eyelid. Then incise with a scalpel along the lines you marked, starting from the larger forceps to the smaller ones. Each incision should be near the natural eyelashes and about the width of a speculum.
Then the snare is inserted into one of the corners of the skin, then it is peeled off completely, then the two lips are brought together with a needle and a fine woolen thread, the blood is wiped away and what remains of the threads is glued onto the eyebrows with something sticky.
If you wish to do so, or else it does not matter, then the stitches and threads remain for about three or four days, then it is treated, and if you wish you could leave the wound without stitching and treat it with what dries and constricts, for the eyelid rises when the wound is closed and joined, and stitching is better.
This method of cauterization, which was mentioned by those who came before, has a benefit for the patient and is a good procedure with no danger. There is another method of cauterization as well, which is to draw a shape on the eyelid like the shape of an oleaster leaf, as we have described. Then the eyelid is lifted with three forceps, which are either separate or grouped in this way:
Then what remains of the eyelid is cut with a small pair of scissors in this way, cutting moderately:
If you cannot hold the forceps and they do not stay in place for you, then take a needle with thread in it and insert it into the middle of the shape, insert another thread near the larger angle and a third thread near the smaller angle, then gather the threads between your fingers moderately and lift them up moderately with your hand.
And lift the whole marked skin as we have described, then bring the two lips of the wound together with stitches and treat it until it heals. If a hot tumor appears when you cut or make an incision, soothe that tumor with cerate and other cooling ointments.
It may also happen that the lower eyelid turns inward, and what we have mentioned should be used for cutting, stitching, cauterization with fire and with caustic drugs, and cauterization with fire or with caustic drugs is more effective and lighter on the patient than cutting and stitching, and cutting and stitching are better in them.
Cauterization with sugarcane is done in this way: you turn back the eyelid and make an incision on the inner side as described before, then make two canes or thin pieces of wood as long as the eyelid and narrower than a lancet.
You cut their ends on both sides where the threads will be attached, then carefully bring them together on what remains of the eyelid and tighten the canes from both sides very tightly, leaving them for several days. The stretched skin will die, turn black, and rot.
It will fall off on its own, but if it is delayed, cut it with scissors and then treat it until it heals. When the wound unites, the eyelid will rise and the eyelashes of the eye will not sink. This is an image of the canes, made in this shape and size exactly in length and width:
Chapter Twelve.
On raising ingrown eyelashes from the eye with a needle.
If it is one or two hairs, the procedure is to take a fine needle and thread a very fine, smooth silk thread through it. Then gather both ends and tie them into a very delicate knot, the length of the thread being about a span long.
Then attach another thin thread to the needle, shorter than the first one, and tie both ends if you wish. Have the patient place their head in your lap, preferably near sunlight so that the work can be clearly seen, as it is a delicate procedure. Then insert the needle with the thread through the base of the ingrown hair.
Quickly push it through the eyelid until it emerges above the natural hair, then pull the thread up toward the needle and thread that ingrown hair into the needle's eye if there is only one or two, or at most three.
Then pull your hand upward so that the hair comes out with the needle in the eyelid along with the natural hair. If you pull on the needle and the hair does not come out, pull the needle down with the thread you attached to it until the needle emerges from the upper hole.
Then return the hair to it and pull them, do this until it rises in the upper hole and you see it with your eye. Then loosen the forceps and the thread and pull them out, tighten the eyelid and leave it tight for two or three days until the hair is fixed in its place and flesh grows on it.
If the hair is short, either add a long hair from natural hair to it so that it rises with it, or leave it until it grows after a while, and then lift it.
Chapter Thirteen.
In the treatment of a sty that occurs in the upper eyelid.
The sty that is exposed to this is called a chalazion, and this sty can be either natural or accidental, the accidental one being from the healing of a wound, a scratch, or something similar.
The procedure for treating it is to cut that induration, separate its lips, and place a wick of linen between them, tying it until it heals. It should not be treated with things that dry and constrict.
For if you do that, the sty will return worse than before, but use things that soften it, such as fenugreek and a compress with water in which hyssop and flaxseed have been boiled, and a diachylon plaster dissolved with some ointment, and apply it to the wick and treat it.
The goal of treating it is to try every way to prevent it from healing in the shape it originally had, and this work we mentioned is only some improvement of the sty, not returning the place to its original condition.
Chapter Fourteen.
In the treatment of a sty that occurs in the lower eyelid.
This sty, which is from below, is truly called a hordeolum and can be either natural or accidental. The accidental type is from a wound, scratch, burn, or something similar.
The way to work with it is to take a needle with a double thread and insert it into the flesh, passing it from the left armpit to the right armpit until the thread is at both ends of the flesh. Then you pull the flesh upwards with the needle and cut it with a wide chisel.
If the eyelid returns to its proper shape, otherwise you take a pledget and place it on the incision site and turn the eyelid with it, making two incisions in the inner side of the eyelid. The ends of the incisions should be from the corners of the cuts that were made until they meet, forming an angle. Until they come together, its shape will resemble this shape: .
It is the shape of the Greek letter lambda, then you remove that flesh so that the sharp side of it is lower than what follows the eyelid, then you bring together the separate parts with two sutures sewn with wool thread and then treat them with the softening and loosening medicines we mentioned until it heals.
And if the rupture appeared from a cut, stitch or burn, then a simple incision should be made without hair on the eyebrows as mentioned above, then the lips are separated with a twist as we mentioned.
In summary, when treating a rupture whether it is from above or below, you should proceed according to the shape of the rupture as it presents itself, for it may vary greatly in appearance and the skilled craftsman will find a way by any means possible.
So that the shape returns to its natural form or approaches it, and the same disease will guide you to what is suitable for it in terms of work and instruments in most cases.
Chapter Fifteen.
On the Adhesion of the Eyelid to the Conjunctiva or Cornea.
This adhesion may occur in many people, with the upper eyelid adhering to the white of the eye, preventing it from moving easily and hindering its natural functions. Therefore, you should insert the end of a pledget under the eyelid.
Raise it upwards or extend it with a thread, then cut the adhesion with a fine scalpel, not one sharpened for cutting but rather slightly blunted so as not to alarm the patient during the procedure, lest the scalpel slip and injure the eye with its sharpness; your cutting should be like peeling away that adhesion.
And anticipate that once the eyelid returns to its natural state and the adhesion heals completely, you should then pour saline water into the eye to wash it, or drip a solution of zangari ginger in water; afterward, separate the eyelid from the eye with a linen wick.
Place over the eye a compress moistened with egg white, and after the third day, use softened poultices until it heals.
Chapter Sixteen.
On Excising the Chalazion and Removing Fleshy Growths.
A chalazion comes in two types: either it is nervous, resembling a thin, firm membrane; or it is non-nervous, resembling a solid, white lump. If iron touches it or a thread attempts to grasp it, it breaks apart.
Neither type allows a thread to be passed through it; both begin at the larger conjunctiva and gradually spread until they cover the viewer and obstruct light and eye movement.  The procedure for excising them is as follows: have the patient rest their head in your lap.
Then have them open their eyes and lift the eyelid with your hand; grasp the chalazion with a slightly curved thread and extend it upwards. Take a needle, insert a horsehair or cowhair and a strong thread into it, and bend the tip of the needle slightly. Insert the needle into the center of the chalazion.
Pass the needle through, tie the thread around the chalazion, and pull it upwards. Peel away the side of the chalazion adjacent to the iris using the hair as if spreading it out to its end. Then cut the remaining portion at the base of the conjunctiva with a fine scalpel or small scissors, leaving the natural conjunctiva flesh intact.
So that there may not be continuous tearing from the piece cut, and the cicatrix may extend with the thread alone or with a string as we have said. Then the smooth-edged knife is used to peel off the end of which this is the image:
And take care that the knife does not touch the corneal membrane, for a perforation would be made and the pupil would dilate. Then after the cut, put into the eye some powdered salt or ginger powder, and leave it until the next day, then treat it as is proper until it heals.
But if the cicatrix is not nervous and you cannot insert a needle into it nor fix a thread there, this type of cicatrix is often treated with medication and heals as described in the section. If you wish to cut it, open the eye of the patient.
And take a delicate smooth-edged knife, weak at the point, like this: And pare the cicatrix from above with delicacy. If you see that it loosens and crumbles, and the paring has only affected it, put in the eye at once some ginger powder or red pepper or powdered salt. Bind the eye until the next day.
Then repeat the process until it is all gone, unless you encounter a hot swelling in the eye. Leave it and treat the hot swelling until it heals. Then repeat the paring until it is healed. As for fleshy growths...
If the growth pains the eye severely, suspend it with a thread and cut off some of it, but do not overdo the cutting lest there be continuous tearing. Then put ginger or pepper into the lachrymal sac until it heals.
Chapter Seventeen.
On Cutting the Pterygium and Excess Flesh That Grows in the Eye.
Sometimes red flesh grows on the eyes of some people until it covers the pupil or comes close to it, or overflows onto the eyelids. The eyelids may even turn outward and resemble a pomegranate flower.
The procedure involves the patient placing their head in your lap and then opening their eyes, while you grasp all of that red flesh with suitable forceps.
Or you may hold it with a clamp or forceps, and then cut the flesh one by one until all of it is gone, while preserving the eye so that it is not harmed during the procedure. Your cuts should be made either with the scalpel we described for cutting eyelashes, or with small scissors. Like those used to pluck hairs, as will be shown in the chapter following this one.
Once you have finished cutting and all the growth is gone, fill the eye with pounded salt or drop into it red lead or a similar caustic medication. Apply cotton wool soaked in egg white to the outside of the eye to prevent hot inflammation.
If some of the growth remains, and bleeding is profuse and you fear hot inflammation, leave the eye alone and treat it with something that will soothe the inflammation. Then return to work on it until it heals. Do the same for any excess flesh of this type that appears in the eye.
However, you must avoid treating any weak eye, either by nature or appearance, with iron instruments and do not put your hand to any of these procedures until you are sure that the organ can tolerate it.
Chapter Eighteen.
On Plucking Hairs From the Eye.
Hairs are red vessels that grow on the eye, preventing vision and weakening it over time.
Then comes the ligature, which is to order the patient to place his head in your lap and then suspend those veins with one or two silk threads according to your skill. The threads should be fine and capable of bending in this manner:
Or they may be double threads in a single strand, in this manner:
Then, with fine scissors, carefully cut those veins and wipe away the blood from time to time until you see that the eye has lost those veins and the blood has clotted. Be careful not to injure the eye with the tips of the scissors, and work facing the sun during daylight hours.
And be very steady in your work so as not to cut anything other than those veins. When you are finished, cauterize the eye with green or red vitriol, so that its sharpness will consume whatever remains of the vessels. If you cannot ligate them all at that time, bandage the eye with something that soothes it.
Leave it for several days until the pain subsides and the hot swelling disappears, then repeat the procedure in the same manner until this image of the scissors is gone.
Leave it [with the patient's head in the lap of the student, and another wiping away tears and ensuring clear vision at the front and back of the eye], and know that the principle in this work is to open the eye with fixed or triangular clamps held by a student.
Gaze intently, with the patient's head in the lap of a student and another wiping away tears and ensuring clear vision at the front and back of the eye. You have the choice to begin the ligature from the top or bottom of the eye;  it is preferable to first tie two threads on the upper side, one at the end of the ciliary body and the other at its beginning.
Hold them between the middle and index finger, then suspend threads underneath opposite them. Hold these with the ring and little finger, lightly lifting your hand until you see all the vessels have risen. Then cut them off with the smallest scalpel if you choose to use a scalpel, or with a fine probe if you prefer.
And insert the needle holder into the needle and elevate all the paths until it rises above the skin, and do not cut the conjunctiva for tears and blood will obscure you.
Then cut with the scissors what exceeds the limbus from above and below so that nothing remains protruding except in the cornea, for if you are able to raise it from the cornea, remove it with the needle holder, this is best, and it may become available in some people.
And if you cut it, it will be shaved for a few days, its pain will subside and the hot swelling will disappear, then resume working on it.
Chapter Nineteenth.
On Returning the Feather to the Nose.
Physicians call the feather a nasal suppressor if they treat it with cautery or with a sharp burning medicine, as previously described, and it does not heal; there is no remedy in this case except to incise the swelling when ripe and extract all the moisture or pus within it.
Until the bone is exposed, and if you see decay or blackness in it, scrape it with an instrument of this form:
It is called a rough-headed scraper, made of Indian iron and its head is round like a button, engraved with a fine file or eskalfash mark. Place it on the decayed part of the bone, then turn it between your fingers while slightly pressing your hand until you know that the decay has been removed.
Do this several times, then bind the area with drying astringent medicines. If the area closes and flesh grows in it, and if the discharge stops for a period of forty days and does not recur or become inflamed, and no incident occurs.
Then know that it has been cured, otherwise there is no remedy except to return the nasal suppressor to the nostril in this manner: expose the bone again with iron or a sharp medicine. Once the bone is exposed, take a spatula of this shape:
The end of the iron should be triangular, and its shaft conical, brought to a point at the end, as you see. Then place it upon the bone itself near the tumor and a little distance from the eye with your hand.  Then turn your hand with the instrument until the bone is pierced and the patient feels it.
If he feels the wind coming out when he puts his hand on his nose, then bind the place with drying astringent medications as I have taught you. And if the flesh grows and the place becomes firm, then the matter that was flowing outward will return to the nose, and this will be easier for the patient, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
Returning a Protruding Eye.
If the eye protrudes entirely and no defect or deficiency has occurred in vision, then you should soothe the patient and then fix him in a head bandage, then apply a collar to the neck without fail and suck gently.
Then place a poultice on the eye made of acacia, patience, frankincense, and myrrh, then bind the eye tightly over the poultice with many bandages; the recipe for the bandages is to take many soft rags and fold each one into four folds, making many of them.
Then place them one on top of the other according to the eye's need, and bind tightly with a strong rag. Then replace the collar with suction without fail, then remove the collar and leave the eye bound for a day and a night.
If the bandage loosens during that time, it should be tightened again. Then remove the bandage after a day and a night as we said. If you see that the eye has returned, otherwise repeat the poultice, bandages, tightness and collar until it returns.
Chapter Twenty-One.
On Cutting a Cataract.
If a rupture occurs in the lens layer, then protrudes and emerges outside the eyelids, resembling an onion, and disfigures the human form, and you wish to cut it out, proceed as I describe.
It is done by inserting a needle into the base of the abscess from below upwards, then inserting another needle with a doubled thread into it from the side of the ulceration and passing it through, leaving the first needle in place.
Then the place where the doubled thread bends is cut and some of the abscess is tied around the needle, which is then tightened well. The needle is then removed and wool moistened with egg white is placed over the eye and left until the threads fall off with the abscess, after which the eye is treated with something that strengthens it.
Until the wound is healed, and the abscess may be tightened from all sides with a circle of cloth so that the eye becomes more prominent, then it is treated with a thin probe that reaches into the depths of the eye, so that the egg-white moisture flows out and the eye descends and softens in its place, then it is tightened until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
On the Treatment of a Cataract.
This ailment, which is called a cataract, is a fluid-like substance that gathers in the eye but is not actually water. The procedure for this involves having the patient sit upright on a chair and then taking their head in both of your hands.
You move it until you see the fluid descend downwards with your eye, and then fix it so that it does not move again. Then light is released and the patient can see things as they used to. If the fluid does not descend, we know that it is water. If the descent of the fluid is not possible as mentioned,
then seat the patient in front of you, and take a thin probe and make a small incision above the corneal membrane at the junction of the conjunctiva with the corneal membrane in the limbus, so that the substance is expelled.
Once it has been expelled, pour hot water into which honey or boiled licorice and honey have been added into the eye. Then treat it with all other treatments until it is healed.
Chapter twenty-third.
We have mentioned the types of water in the division and which is suitable for washing, with detailed explanation, so take it from there according to its correctness. Then, the patient should sit in front of you, squared towards the light near the sun.
Tie his sound eye and pull it tight, then raise the eyelid of his eye with your left hand if the watery eye is the left one or with your right hand if the watery eye is the right one.
Then place the tip of the syringe near the limbus, with the thickness of a reed, in the same white part of the eye from the side of the smaller pupil, then push the syringe firmly while turning it with your hand until it enters the white of the eye and you feel the syringe has reached something empty.
The syringe should go to a depth equal to the distance from the pupil to the edge of the black, which is the limbus of the eye. Since the copper you see in the same way that the viewer sees it due to the clarity of the corneal membrane, then turn the syringe upward to the place where the water is.
Then press it down again and again, if the water comes out from its hour, then the patient will see what his sight has opened from its hour, and the syringe in his eye, then rest for a while. If the water rises, lower it again without taking the syringe out.
If it settles and does not rise, carefully take out the syringe while twisting your hand a little, then dissolve a little fine rock salt in water and wash the eye from inside, then put mashed linen or a damp cotton wool on the whole eye outside.
Anoint it with rose oil and egg white and tie it with the sound eye. As for us and the people of our time, we put crushed cumin with egg white on it. If the syringe does not enter the eye due to its hardness because some people have very hard eyes… It is necessary to take the scalpel which is called a briquet, which has this shape:
Then pierce it with the briquet only, and do not deepen the hole; rather, gently touch the point of the instrument to a suitable place on the ligature, then insert the trocar as we have mentioned. When your work is complete, prepare the patient lying firmly on his back in a darkened room.
He must abstain from all movements and coughing, and his food should be what softens by nature. He should not move his head right or left at all. The bandage should remain in place until the third day, then it is loosened in that darkened room, and his sight is tested.
Show him objects, then reapply the bandage until the seventh day. This should not be done during or after the operation, but rather it should be avoided before the water rises quickly due to intense gaze.
If a hot swelling appears, the eye bandage should be loosened before the seventh day and this should be remedied with what soothes the swelling until it subsides; then release his sight and place a veil over his face that trains his vision from underneath for several days while he is in that darkened room.
He should then gradually leave the house and behave normally. Know that a learner cannot dispense with observing this procedure many times before performing it himself. I have heard from some Iraqis that one of them said he makes in Iraq a perforated trocar that absorbs water.
I have not seen anyone in our country make this, nor have I read about it in any of the books of our predecessors. It may be a recent innovation. Here are images of different types of trocars so that he can learn about them:

Chapter Twenty-Four.
In the Treatment of Flesh Growing in the Nose.
Various kinds of excess flesh may grow in the nose, some resembling many-legged scorpions and some being cancerous, hard flesh of a dark color, and some being soft flesh not of a dark color.
Now, as for this flesh which is soft and neither malignant nor cancerous, the patient should sit before you facing the sun, open their nostrils, and have a thread passed into that flesh. Then draw it out and cut off what it has grasped with a fine, sharp scalpel from one side only.
Continue doing this until you are sure that all the flesh is gone. If some remains that you cannot cut, scrape it off carefully with one of the fine instruments so that nothing is left. If blood overcomes you or a hot swelling appears,
treat it accordingly. If, however, the tumor is malignant, quickly remove it so that the bleeding stops and all the flesh is gone. Then pour vinegar and water or a drink into the nose. If the nose opens and the moisture flows down to the throat.
Know that it has been cured. But if the moisture does not pass as it should, know that there is flesh still growing at the top of the loose bones which the instrument did not reach in cutting.
Then take a thread of linen, somewhat thick, and tie many knots in it, spacing them out by the width of a finger or less. Let the patient insert the end of one thread into his nostril with a rod or whatever else is possible after fashioning it like a needle, and draw on it gently until it reaches the cartilage.
It will come out at the back of his throat. Boys in schools often do this sort of thing, and it is easy for anyone who wants to learn. Then gather both ends of the thread: the one that came out of the mouth and the one that remained in the nose.
Then use the ligatures in the thread to spread the flesh, doing this until you know that the flesh has been cut by the thread ligatures. Then remove the thread and after wiping away the blood, place a wick in the nostril which has been soaked in Egyptian ointment. Do this for three days or more.
Until the ointment consumes all that remains of the flesh, then the last thing in the nose will be a lead tube for several days until it is healed. If it needs drying treatment, use that.
This is a picture of the syringe with which ointments and medicines are dropped into the nose.
It is made of silver or copper, resembling a small lamp with an open pouring spout and channel.
And if you wish, make the tube closed like a reed and the syringe's pouring spout flat.
It has a handle at the end, as you see, which is held when the fat or whatever juices or liquid substances are heated in it.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On Vegetable Warts at the Tip of the Nose.
A wart often grows at the tip of the nose, increasing in size with time until it becomes unsightly.
Therefore, it should be cut off as soon as it appears, and its entirety eradicated. Then apply either cauterization or a burning medication that acts in place of cautery to the area. If cutting it was delayed until it grows large, examine it; if it is hard, petrified, dark-colored, and insensitive.
Do not apply iron to it, for it is a cancerous tumor. I have often seen this tumor cut and the result was great suffering for the patient. If the tumor is soft to the touch and not dark-colored, and you see that cutting can be done completely. Then drain the wart and cut it without hesitation or fear, treating the area with drying and astringent agents until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Six
On the suturing of the nose, lip and ear. If the connection of one of them becomes separated due to a wound or something similar, know that when separation of connection occurs in one of these cartilages, it rarely allows for successful work except in some people.
So, when one of these things occurs, see if the wound is fresh and bleeding. Bring the lips of the wound together by suturing and then treat it until it heals. But if the separation has made both sides complete, you should flay each side to the thickness of its outer skin until it bleeds.
Then bring the lips together by suturing, tighten them and place powdered myrrh and frankincense on top. Put a plaster of naphtha ointment or other prepared ointments over it and leave it tight for two or three days, then remove it.
Change the dressing and leave it until the stitches fall out on their own, then treat it with ointment until it heals. As for the suturing technique, bring the separated connection together either with needles as we described in suturing the abdomen or with thread as we explained there.
Chapter Twenty-Seven
On the removal of nodes that appear on the lips.
It may happen to many people that small, hard tumors appear inside their lips, some resembling a carnation seed and others smaller or larger. So you should turn the lip over and cut it open at each node, then hang them from a thread.
Cut them from all sides, then fill the area after cutting with powdered borax until the bleeding stops. Gargle with vinegar and salt, and treat the areas with astringents until the wounds heal.
Chapter Twenty-Eight
On Excising Superfluous Flesh in the Gums.
Very often superfluous flesh grows on the gums which the ancients called an aphtha. It should be hung by a thread or grasped with forceps and cut at its base, then the wound is allowed to bleed freely or the blood is wiped away, after which powdered alum is applied to the spot.
Or one of the astringent dried herbs. If that flesh returns after treatment, which it often does, cut it again and cauterize it; for it will not return after cauterization.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On the Extraction of Teeth with Iron.
It may happen that rough, ugly scales collect on the surfaces of teeth, both inside and outside, and between the gums. These scales may become black, yellow, or even green, until decay sets in and affects the gums, thus spoiling the appearance of the teeth.
The patient should sit before you with his head resting in your lap. You must then extract the molars and teeth which show scales and anything resembling sand, until nothing remains. Do the same for blackness, greenness, yellowness, and so forth, until they are cleansed.
If all the deposits are removed at the first extraction, well and good; otherwise repeat the extraction on another day, and a second and third time until you achieve your purpose. Know that molars require various types of forceps, shaped in many ways, according to what is presented for your work.
This is because the forceps used to extract a molar from the inside are different from those used to extract it from the outside, and those used between molars have yet another shape. You should have several types of forceps ready at hand, all prepared:
Chapter Thirty.
On the Extraction of Teeth.
You should treat a toothache with every remedy and delay extraction as much as possible, for there is no substitute if it is removed; since it is a precious entity. Even if extraction becomes necessary, proceed cautiously.
If the patient is determined to have it extracted, you should ensure that the toothache has subsided. Patients are often deceived by pain and think it is in the correct tooth, so they have it extracted, but the pain does not go away until the diseased tooth is removed. We have seen this done by barbers many times.
If the toothache is indeed in that tooth, then you should mark around the tooth with a strong forceps to loosen the gums from all sides, and then move it with your fingers or delicate pliers, at first little by little, until it loosens.
Then, when it is loosened, grasp it firmly with large forceps, the patient's head securely between your knees so that he does not move, and then pull the tooth straight out so as not to break it. If it does not come out, take one of those instruments, insert it under it from all sides gently, and shake it as you did before.
And if the tooth is carious or decayed, you should fill that cavity with a rag and tightly plug it with the end of a thin probe so that it does not break when you pull on it with the pliers. You must carefully examine the gums around all sides.
Take care not to break it, for some of it may remain and cause the patient more suffering than his original pain. And beware of doing what ignorant barbers do in their haste to extract it without using what we have described.
They often inflict great evils on people, the least of which is that the tooth breaks and all or part of its roots remain. Or they may extract it with some of the jawbone, as we have seen many times. After extraction, rinse with a drink or vinegar and salt. If bleeding occurs from the site, which often happens, then crush some alum and apply it to the site.
Otherwise, cut it out if the borax does not help. The image of the delicate callipers with which to move a tooth first has long prongs and a short handle, thick so that they do not bend when you grasp the tooth. This is an image of the large callipers:
It should be as you see, with a thick handle so that when you grip it, it does not give way or bend. The prongs should be short. Make it of Indian iron or of well-tempered steel. The ends should be squared off, and on the end there are teeth which fit into one another so that they grasp firmly and tightly.
The prongs may also be shaped like a file, for a firm grip as well.
Chapter Thirty-One.
On the Extraction of Tooth Roots and Removal of Broken Jawbones.

It should have a short, slightly thick end and not be squared off, lest it break. If the root comes out with this, well and good; otherwise, you should resort to these other instruments, which are shown in the following image: the first is triangular at the end, and somewhat thick:
