Chapter Thirty-Two.
On spreading erupted teeth that are not like others.
If teeth erupt in an unnatural position, they become unsightly, especially if this happens in women and delicate individuals. It should be examined to see if the tooth has erupted behind another tooth.
And if it cannot be spread or moved back, extract it. If it is stuck to another tooth, cut it with this instrument, as shown in this figure:
It resembles a small beak and should be made of very sharp Indian steel.  Cutting should be done over many days due to the hardness of the tooth, so as not to disturb other teeth. If, however, it is loose and amenable to being cooled, cool it with this type of cooling instrument, as shown in this figure:
Let the entire instrument be of steel, and its engraving from it very fine, like the file with which needles are made. Cool the tooth little by little over many days gently so that the tooth is not loosened and falls out, then smooth it finally and strip it with some files.
And if a tooth has broken off some of it and it hurts the tongue when speaking, then it should also be cooled so that the roughness of the break goes away, and it becomes level and smooth so that it does not hurt the tongue nor spoil speech.
Chapter Thirty-Third.
On fixing movable teeth with silver threads or gold threads.
If the front teeth become loosened and movable from a blow or fall, and the patient cannot chew anything to eat lest they fall out, and they have been treated with astringent medicines without success.
The solution is to tighten a gold or silver thread, with gold being preferable because silver oxidizes and rots after a few days, while gold remains unchanged. The thread should be of medium fineness and thickness according to what fits between the teeth.
To illustrate the splinting, take the thread and insert its bend between the two sound teeth, then weave the ends of the thread between the movable teeth, one or more, until the weaving reaches the sound tooth on the other side.
Then repeat the weaving back to the side where you started and tighten your hand gently and skillfully so that they do not move at all, making sure the thread is tight at the roots of the teeth so it does not come loose. Then cut off the excess ends of the thread with scissors, gather them together, twist them with a small tool and hide them between the sound tooth and the movable tooth.
So that it does not hurt the tongue, then leave it tightened as long as it lasts. If it loosens or breaks, tighten it with another thread. In this way one can enjoy it for life. This is a picture of the teeth and the splinting method in two sound teeth and two movable teeth, as you see:
A single tooth or two may sometimes fall back into place and become fixed as we have described, but this is only done by a skilled craftsman. He may carve bone from the bones of an ox, fashion it into the shape of a tooth, and insert it into the space where the tooth has been lost. It is then securely fastened as we have said, and he will enjoy its use.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On cutting the frenulum which appears under the tongue to prevent speech.
This frenulum which appears under the tongue may either be natural, with which a person is born, or accidental from a wound that has healed.
The procedure is to open the patient's mouth, place his head in your lap, and then raise his tongue. Then cut the frenulum across with a scalpel so that the tongue can be released from its hold. If there is some stiffness and complexity, and if this is from a healed wound, insert a probe and split it across until the frenulum breaks and the knot dissolves.
Be careful not to make the incision too deep into the flesh, as you might cut an artery there and cause bleeding. After the cutting, rinse with rose water or vinegar and cold water. Then place a linen wick under the tongue for the patient to hold in his mouth every night.
So that it does not heal again. If bleeding occurs, apply powdered alum to the area. If the bleeding persists, cauterize the spot with a lentil-shaped iron suitable for this purpose. Then treat it with all other remedies until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Five.
On removing a frog that has developed under the tongue.
Sometimes a tumor resembling a small frog develops under the tongue, preventing the tongue from functioning normally and perhaps even growing so large as to fill the mouth. To treat it, open the patient's mouth facing the sun and examine the tumor.
If you see it [the tumor] dark in color or black, hard and the patient feels no sensation in it, do not interfere with it, for it is cancerous. But if it tends towards whiteness and has moisture, insert a probe into it and cut it with a delicate scalpel, clearing it from all sides.
If blood overwhelms you while you are working, put powdered alum on it until the bleeding stops, then return to your work until you remove it completely. Then gargle with vinegar and salt.
Then treat it with all appropriate remedies until it heals.
Chapter the Thirty-Sixth.
On the Treatment of Tonsil Tumors.
And whatever grows in the throat of other tumors may appear within the throat glands resembling those that appear externally, called tonsils, and if you treat them with what I mentioned in the division and they do not heal.
See if the tumor is hard, dark in color and with little sensation, do not interfere with it with an iron instrument. And if it is red in color and its origin is thick, do not interfere with it either with an iron instrument for fear of bleeding, but leave it until it matures, then either cauterize it or it will burst on its own.
But if it is white, round and its origin is soft, then this is what should be cut. The work is to look before working whether its hot inflammation has completely subsided or decreased somewhat.
Then seat the patient facing the sun with his head in your lap and open his mouth. Have a servant press his tongue down with an instrument, its shape is as follows: .
Make it of silver or copper, thin like a knife. When you press with it on the tongue and the tumor is revealed to your sight, take a probe and insert it into the tonsil, drawing it outward as much as possible without pulling any membranes with it.
Then cut it with an instrument whose shape resembles scissors, but its two ends are curved, and the edge of each is opposite the other and very sharp. It is made of Indian iron or Damascus steel.
If this instrument is not available, then cut it with a scalpel, which looks like this:
Sharp on one side and blunt on the other, after cutting one tonsil, cut the other in the same manner. Then after cutting, the patient gargles with cold water or vinegar and water.
If he experiences bleeding, gargle with water in which pomegranate rinds or bay leaves or similar astringents have been steeped until the bleeding stops, and then treat it until it heals. Sometimes other tumors may grow in the throat besides the tonsils, and these are cut out as described for cutting the tonsils.
I treated a woman for a tumor that had grown inside her throat, extending towards the uvula with little sensation; it was almost blocking the throat. The woman was breathing through a narrow passage and it had prevented her from eating or drinking water.
She was close to death if she had remained that way for a day or two, and the tumor had grown two branches that protruded from her nostrils. I acted quickly and inserted a probe into one of them, then pulled it, removing a suitable piece.
Then I cut it where I reached it from the nostril, and did the same with what protruded from the other nostril. Then I opened her mouth, pressed down her tongue, and inserted the probe into the same tumor and cut out some of it. There was only a slight amount of bleeding.
The woman's throat opened up, and from that moment she was able to drink water and then eat. We continued to cut away at that tumor repeatedly for a long time, the tumor regenerating what was removed; it was a long ordeal for both of us.
I treated a growth inside the throat by cauterization, and it stopped increasing; then I traveled to another place, and I do not know what God did with it afterward.
Chapter the Thirty-Seventh.
On Excising a Swelling of the Tonsils.
If a swelling descends into the tonsils and becomes white and oblong, it is called an "Amud" (pillar). If it is thick at the bottom and round, it is called an "Anba" (grape). If it is treated as described in our section on division and does not respond to treatment.
I saw a hot swelling subside and become thin, so it should be cut out. But if any part of it was gathered and round, or if it lacked length, or was blood-colored, leaden-colored, black, or insensitive, it should not be cut out.
There is danger to the patient in this, so if you see it with the characteristics of whiteness and length as mentioned, the patient should sit facing the sun and press their tongue down with the instrument previously described. Then a ligature should be inserted into the swelling.
It should be drawn downwards and cut off with one of the two instruments mentioned for excising tonsils.  Only that which exceeds the natural condition should be cut, because if you cut more, you will injure the voice and speech.
After cutting, the treatment we described for excising tonsils should be applied until it heals. If the patient refuses to have it cut out, a cauterizing method should be used, without fear or hesitation. The method of cautery is with a caustic drug; put the patient's head in your lap.
Then press their tongue down with the instrument we mentioned and take some of the caustic water, as described in the chapter on cautery, mix it into a paste, neither too thick nor too thin. Fill a hollow cavity in this instrument – and this is its shape: [Image of a surgical tool]
Its end, where the medicine is to be placed, should be concave like the bowl of a spoon. You place the instrument with the medicine on the wound itself, and the patient should lie on his side so that saliva flows from his mouth and nothing goes down his throat to harm him.
Then hold your hand with the medicine, pressing it on the wound for half an hour until you see it has turned black and the sting of the medicine has subsided. If you wish, you may take cotton wool, wrap it around the end of the rod, and soak the cotton wool in the medicine.
Insert the rod with the cotton into a tube from above until the cotton adheres to the wound. Repeat this several times until you have reached the desired cauterization. Then leave it; it will shrivel and fall off after three or four days. If you need to reapply the medicine, prepare it again.
After cauterization, wipe around the cautery with cotton soaked in butter and dry the medicine around it. Then gargle with cold water and treat externally with poultices and internally with gargles until healed. This type of treatment is safer than cutting and far less frightening.
The wound can also be treated with something gentler than cautery and cutting: treat it with fumigation in this manner. Take fennel, asafetida, marjoram, saffron, wormwood, chamomile, quince seeds, and similar herbs; gather them all or some of them in a pot and immerse them in vinegar.
Boil it, and place the pot on a wise man's brick base; there should be a hole in the middle of the lid. Mount a hollow instrument on this hole, made of silver or copper.
Insert the end with the rose into the patient's mouth so that the vapor rises to the wound through the tube until the wound softens well; then repeat this several times until it softens.  Avoid performing this treatment at the very beginning of the swelling.
For it often increases the swelling, but it should only be done when the hot tumor is subsiding. If this instrument is not available to you, take a reed and fix an eggshell at its end so that the patient's mouth does not burn.
Because the eggshell prevents the heat of the steam from burning the mouth, and this is a good treatment with its safety.
Chapter Thirty-Eight.
On extracting thorns and what becomes lodged in the throat besides them.
A bone or fishbone, or something else often becomes lodged in the throat; therefore, you should extract what is visible and seen after pressing down on the tongue with an instrument under the sun.
So that what is in the throat will become clear to you, and what has not appeared to you and remains hidden in the throat.
Or he swallows a mouthful of dry bread, or takes a piece of soft sea sponge, ties it in a string, then swallows it. When it reaches the place of the thorn, he quickly pulls the string several times.
Often the thorn or bone will stick to it and come out; if it does not come out as mentioned, then use an instrument made of lead in this shape:
It is slightly thicker than a plumb and at its end there is a bend which the patient inserts into his throat gently while raising his head upward, and he refrains from touching his larynx lest coughing should occur, and he pushes with it the bone or thorn; or the doctor introduces it with his hand.
And the patient's introducing it is better, because of his knowledge of the location of the object that is embedded, and he pushes it down or pulls his hand with the instrument up, all according to what he can manage until it comes out.
Chapter Thirty-Nine.
On the Removal of a Fishbone Lodged in the Throat.
If you have treated the fishbone as mentioned in the section on treatment with medication and it has not been successful, then look into the patient's throat in the sunlight after pressing his tongue down with the instrument that has been described to you.
If your sight falls upon the fishbone, then draw it out with a small fishing line or a delicate pair of tweezers; if you are not able to do so, then take a hollow tube and insert it into the patient's throat as near to the fishbone as possible, then insert a heated iron into the inside of the tube.
Do this several times, and the patient abstains from water all day; then he takes a large cup filled with cold water, opens his mouth into it, gargles, and does not swallow a drop of it, moving the water from time to time with his hand.
If the leech falls on the place when it feels water, if it does not come out as we described, then burn the throat with urine and with a cautery using the instrument that was described in the chapter on burning. Do this several times, for it will fall off.
The method of using incense is to take a container in which there are hot embers heated by fire, and the container is covered with a lid in which there is a hole. Then the end of the instrument is placed into that hole and incense is thrown on top. The patient places his mouth on the end of the tube and closes it so that the incense does not come out until he knows that the incense has reached his throat.
If the leech falls on the place, then if it does not fall off, incense is repeated several times and the patient endures thirst and eats salt and garlic and does not drink water; it is certain that it will come out with this treatment. This is a picture of an instrument with which to draw out a leech from the throat when sight falls upon it.
It resembles callipers as you see, except that it has this protrusion which enters the throat and its ends resemble a bird's beak with roughness like a file so that when it grasps something it does not let go.
And there is another instrument with which to press down the tongue, it is a wide ring of iron as large as the mouth above the tongue. It enters the mouth upright on its side so that the mouth remains open against the will of the patient and he cannot remove it at all until it is removed.
Describe the instrument with which to open the mouth in exactly this manner.
Chapter forty.
In it, discussion is made about cutting and opening abscesses.
There are many types of tumors, diverse according to what will be mentioned one by one in this book. They differ in their incision and opening in two ways: one is the type of tumor itself and what it contains of humors.
The second type is according to the places in the body where it occurs, for a tumor that occurs in the head is not like a tumor that occurs in the buttock, and a tumor that occurs in a fleshy place is not like a tumor that occurs in a joint; each one has a rule of treatment.
Some tumors should not be incised except after the maturation and completion of pus in them, and some should be incised while they are still immature, such as tumors that are close to the joints.
For if a tumor occurs near a joint and its matter continues until it becomes putrid, it may corrupt the ligaments or nerves of that joint, thus becoming a cause for lameness of that limb, or the tumor may be near a principal member.
For if you delay its incision until it matures, you would thereby injure that principal member; or it may be near the buttocks, and you incise it while still immature, for it is necessary to incise a tumor that is still immature and not completely ripe when it is near the buttocks so that it does not become putrid and spread into the inside of the buttocks and become dangerous.
And it may reach a point where it does not heal, and you should know the time for incising tumors that have matured completely, which is when the pain of the tumor subsides, the fever goes away, the redness and throbbing decrease, and the head of the tumor becomes hardened along with all other signs.
The incision should be made at the lowest part of the tumor if possible, so that it is easier for the pus to flow downwards, or in the thinnest and most prominent part of the tumor. The incision should run lengthwise in the body if the tumors are located on the hands or feet.
Avoid making the incision over muscles, tendons, nerves, arteries, and in general, all flat places where there is no bending; as for those that bend, let the incision go according to that place. And if the tumor is in fleshy places, it is best to leave it un incised.
Until it is fully matured as we have said, for if you incise it before that, the pus will flow from it for a long time and be foul and dirty, and perhaps its lips will become rigid and swollen, and some tumors may be incised across the body when necessary or according to what the member requires.
And one incision should be used in small tumors, and a wide incision or many incisions according to the size of the tumor in large tumors, and some tumors may need to have their skin scraped and cut if it has become like rag.
And it has reached a certain point where it died as is shown in many abscesses and fistulas, some of which are cut with a three-cornered incision, and some are cut from it in the shape of an ace of spades like the Arabic sore.
And some use a round incision and a crescent incision and the like, and if it does not have a head like smooth flat tumors, it should only be incised with a simple incision. If the tumor is large and has been accumulating for a long time and you have incised it, do not hasten to let all the pus out at that time.
But draw some of it out, then tighten the tumor to another day, then draw out some more pus, and do this repeatedly gradually until it all comes out.
Especially if the patient is weak or a pregnant woman or a young child or an old man, for the animal spirit often dissolves with the sudden exit of pus, and the patient may die without you realizing it, so be extremely cautious in this matter.
And after you have incised these tumors, you should wipe the wound and look; if the tumor's wound is small or the incision was a simple one, use a bandage of linen or soft cotton, but if the tumor is large and there are many incisions.
It is necessary to insert a thread into each incision so that they reach each other, and if some of the tumor has been cut from the skin or dried up, it should be stuffed with absorbent cotton or tow linen without moisture and tightened until the third day.
Then remove it and treat it with the appropriate ointments until it heals. If bleeding occurs while you are working, use cold water and vinegar after soaking a linen cloth in them and applying it to the bleeding site several times.
If the bleeding persists, you should use the measures, treatments and necessities that we have described in many places in this book and in the division. If it is wintertime and the tumor site is highly innervated, soak the bandages in a warm syrup and oil.
And place them on the site. If it is summer and the sites are hot, soak the bandages in water and oil or syrup and oil, all cold, until the third day as we said. Then the tumor should be dissolved and rubbed.
Use what seems appropriate for its treatment until it heals. This is what you need to know about the treatment of tumors in general. As for the detailed method, I have mentioned how to treat each tumor summarized and categorized.
Chapter Forty-One.
On the incision for tumors that appear on the scalp.
Small tumors appear on the scalp, and they are of a sac-like nature containing membranes, as if they were a chicken's pouch. There are many types; some are fatty, and others contain moisture resembling slime.
Some contain moisture resembling broth or soup, and others are hardened and calcified. All of them are safe to incise and remove unless you encounter an artery during the incision.
The operation on opening it consists first of probing it with the instrument whose form follows this, which is called a director, in order to learn its contents. If what it contains is moist, it should be opened lengthwise with a simple incision as shown:
Begin the incision from line ~B~ to line ~J~, and when the moisture has drained away, peel off the sac which contained it and cut it all away, leaving nothing of it at all, for it often recurs if anything of it remains.
Then dip cotton in soft ointment if available, or else in salt water, and fill the wound with it, leaving it until the following day, for it will consume what remains of the sac. Then reapply the cotton with Egyptian ointment a second and third time if necessary.
Until you are certain that nothing of the sac remains, and then treat the wound with ointments until it heals. But if the tumor contains fatty matter, open it as follows:
Make a firm incision, holding the edges with forceps, and peel it away from all sides. Strive to remove the peritoneum which contains it. If you encounter an artery, do as I have described above. Do the same in the tumor if it is hardened from the incision.
The treatment is the same as I have described, and the incision on the hardened tumor is easier because it has little blood and moisture. Some of these tumors in the head of some people have no moisture at all. For example, when I opened a tumor...
... in the head of an old woman, I found the tumor hard as a solid, rough stone, white and impossible to break; if someone had thrown it, it would have shattered. And none of the other tumors occurring in the head were like this except for those which occur in the heads of boys.
And when it comes to the roots of the ears, split them all with a simple incision and make its base from its underside so that the passage of matter goes down easily, then treat it with what is appropriate for its treatment.
The Forty-Second Chapter.
In the incision on pigs that are exposed in the neck, these tumors often appear in the neck and under the armpits and in the thighs and may appear throughout the body. The one that appears in the neck is usually singular, while those found elsewhere are multiple, some arising from others.
Each pig has its own peritoneum as it is in the hernias and head tumors as we have described, and there are many types of these pigs: some are petrified, some contain moisture, and some are malignant and do not respond to treatment.
I have not seen one in good condition when touched, and its appearance was close to the color of the skin. It moved in all directions and was not attached to the neck nerve or the jugular vein or any artery, nor was it sunken. It should be incised with a simple incision from above to below the body in this shape, from line ~J~ to line ~B~.
And peel it from all sides and stretch the lips of the skin with a forceps or two forceps or many if needed as we said in head tumors, and extract it little by little and be careful on the neck not to cut a vein or nerve, and let the scalpel not be too sharp.
Lest your hand increase the cut or agitate the patient and you cut what you do not need to cut, if you cut a vein or artery and it prevents you from working, put powdered alum in the wound or some astringents that stop bleeding.
Tighten the wound and leave it until the sharpness of the tumor subsides and the wound relaxes, then consider putrefaction, for the blood will stop then, so return to your work until you are done, then search with your index finger if there are any other small pigs left, cut them and clean them.
If there is a large vein at the base of the wart, it should not be cut off from its base. Instead, it should be tied tightly with a doubled thread and left until it falls off on its own without harm. Then the wound should be stuffed with clean cotton that has been soaked in Egyptian ointment and treated thereafter.
If the entire wart is cut off, the lips of the wound should be brought together and sewn up immediately after you are sure that there is no remnant at all. If you see that a remnant of the wart remains.
You should aim to cut it off at its base and foundation and use the suturing as we have mentioned. And if any of the warts contain moisture, cover them with a simple plaster where the ripening spot appears to you and place the plaster below as we said.
Then after the plaster, use a bandage with Egyptian ointment or similar to consume what remains of the corruption until you see that the wound has become clean and treat it with ointments that promote flesh growth so that it heals.
Chapter Forty-Third.
On the incision of the larynx for a tumor occurring inside the throat.
The ancients mentioned this incision in the larynx and I have not seen anyone in our country perform it, and this is according to their words. As for those with angina, they should avoid an incision in the larynx as they will not benefit from it.
This is because all the veins and lungs will be diseased, but for those with a hot tumor in the mouth or throat or tonsils, if there is no cause in the trachea, it is necessary to use an incision in the larynx to escape the disaster that comes from choking.
If we want to do this, we should cut the larynx under three or four small incisions in width between two circles as much as the incision is in the peritoneum and not in the cartilage. This location is suitable for the incision.
Because it is devoid of flesh and blood vessels are distant from it, if the operator is timid he should stretch the skin of the throat with a string and then cut the skin until he reaches the trachea, avoiding blood vessels if he sees any.
Then he should cut the peritoneum as we have described, and infer the cutting of the trachea from the phlegm that comes out of it along with its bubbling and cessation of voice, leaving the wound open for a time. If the time in which he feared choking passes, he should gather the lips of the wound from the skin.
And he should suture it alone without cartilage, then use medicines that promote flesh growth until it heals. The author of this book said: the interpretation of this whole statement which we have mentioned is only if they see the patient's throat obstructed by one of these tumors which we have mentioned.
And if the patient is on the verge of death and intends to die, they should proceed to cut the larynx so that the patient can breathe on the wound for a while and be saved from death, but they are commanded to leave the wound open until the course of the disease is completed, and its course is about three days.
Then they are commanded to suture the wound and treat it until it heals. I myself have witnessed a servant girl who took a knife and ran it across her throat, cutting some of the trachea. I was called to treat her and found her dying like a slaughtered animal. So I exposed the wound.
I found that the blood coming from the wound was slight, so I was certain that she had not cut an artery or a vein, and the air was escaping from the wound. So, I sutured the wound and treated it until it healed.  Nothing else happened to the servant girl except for a hoarseness in her voice.
And she returned after a few days to her best condition. Therefore, we say that cutting the larynx is not dangerous.
Chapter forty-fourth.
On the incision for the tumor that appears in the tonsil from outside, which is called elephantiasis of the tonsil.
This tumor, which is called elephantiasis of the tonsil, is a very large tumor the color of the body and it is common in women and it is of two types.
Either it is natural or accidental. As for the natural, there is no remedy for it, and as for the accidental, it is of two types, one resembling fatty tumors, and the other resembling a tumor that results from the complication of an artery and its incision is dangerous. It should not be exposed to the iron at all except for what is small from it, if you probe and examine it with a speculum and find that it resembles a fatty tumor and is not attached to any of the veins, then cut it as you would cut a fatty tumor. And remove it with whatever sac it contains, if it is in a sac, otherwise extract all of it, and then treat the area as appropriate.
Chapter forty-fifth.
On the incision for types of tumors.
Tumors are of many types, and I have mentioned all their types in the division. It is necessary to tell here the difference between a tumor and an abscess, as it is problematic. I say that an abscess has heat, fever, and severe pains until the boiling of the pus subsides. And the putrefaction continues, at which time the fever and sharpness subside. A tumor is not accompanied by heat, fever, or pain. It contains a peritoneal sac, which is its own specific envelope, and it appears the color of the body. Its beginning is like a lentil seed, and it becomes like a melon, larger or smaller.
It is of two types: either fatty or containing moisture. The colors of the moisture are many, as I mentioned in the division. If you intend to treat a tumor, first probe and examine it with an instrument called a speculum, whose image will come later in the chapter that follows this one.
Determine the size of the tumor, then remove the probe and look at what comes out after it. If a liquid of any color emerges, make a simple incision as I have described for all other tumors. If no liquid emerges after the probe, know that it is fatty.
Make a firm incision on it in this manner as I have taught you, and hang it with forceps. Carefully peel the skin from every side and preserve the sac if possible so that it comes out intact with the contents.
If the sac is torn during the procedure and you are unable to remove it intact, which often happens, remove it piece by piece until nothing remains. For if any part of it stays behind, whether little or much, the contents will more likely return to its former state.
If you are overpowered and a little remains, severely cauterize the wound when finished with some sharp caustic agents. Tighten the wound and place something soothing on top of it to calm the hot tumor. Treat it with all other remedies until it heals. If the contents is large, cut its edges and treat them with what promotes healing.
If a throbbing or non-throbbing vein is encountered and bleeding occurs, immediately cauterize the area with powdered litharge and leave it tightened for two or three days until the wound becomes infected and the blood flow subsides. Then return to removing what remains of the contents.

Chapter forty-seventh.
In the treatment of men's breasts.
Which resembles women's breasts, sometimes the breast of some people swells upon reaching puberty until it resembles a woman's breast, remaining warm and ugly. So whoever hates that, it should be cut with a crescent-shaped incision like this: .
From line ~b~ to line ~c~. Then peel off all the fat, then fill the wound with salty medicine, then bring the lips of the wound together with stitches and treat it until it heals. If the breast leans downward and relaxes to its bone as it happens to women.
It should be cut in its upper sides with two incisions resembling the shape of crescents, each connecting to the other at their ends so that the larger line surrounds the smaller one like this: . From line ~b~ to line ~c~, then peel the skin between the incisions, remove the fat, and use what we mentioned of stitching and necessities, and what it needs for treatment until it heals.
If you do not remove the necessary portion due to the patient's anxiety or bleeding, then you should pack the wound with cotton soaked in an escharotic ointment and leave it until it consumes what remains of the fat, then treat it until it heals.
Chapter Forty-Eight.
On tumors that develop under the armpit.
These tumors that appear under the armpit are of a porcine type, solid and containing a sac; some contain moisture. If it contains moisture, then a crescent-shaped incision should be made as follows.
From line "~b~" to line "~j~". Once the tumor has emptied all its contents, pack it with dry cotton and leave it until the next day; then remove the cotton.
Treat it with the appropriate ointment until it heals. If the tumor is of a porcine nodule type, incise it as we mentioned for incisions on pigs.  If the wound continues to discharge pus, then apply cauterization as we described previously.
Chapter Forty-Nine.
On incising a tumor that presents near an artery or vein, which is called an aneurysm.
If an artery is wounded and the skin above it heals, a tumor often develops; similarly, a vein may swell and develop a tumor.
The signs that indicate whether the tumor and swelling are from an artery or a vein: if it is from an artery, the tumor will be rectangular and located deep within the body; and when you press on the tumor with your finger, it will feel as though it has a pulse.
A tumor from a vein will be round and located on the surface of the body. Incision on these tumors is dangerous, especially those in the armpit, groin, neck, and many other parts of the body, as they are often very large; therefore, treatment with iron should be avoided.
And whatever of it is also in the extremities or in the head, it should be avoided. Whatever of it is an aneurysm of the artery, make a long incision in its skin and then open the incision with forceps, then dissect out the artery and free it from its adhesions.
Until it is exposed, then insert a needle under it and pass it through to the other side and tighten the artery with a double thread in two places as you were shown in the section on the two arteries that are in the fingers, then scoop out the spot between the ligatures until all the blood in it comes out.
And the swelling will resolve and then use treatment that produces pus so that the ligatures fall off, then treat it with appropriate ointments until it heals.
But if the swelling is from a vein incision, then you should grasp as much of the swelling with the skin as possible with your hand, then insert a needle under the spot that you are grasping with your hand and pass it through with a double thread so that it comes out on the other side. Then tie the swelling well as I described to you tying the neck from both sides at the ends of the thread, and if you fear that the threads may slip, insert another needle with a second thread under the entire swelling where the first needle intersects.
Tighten your threads in four places, then make an incision in the middle of the swelling so that when what is inside comes out, cut off the excess skin and leave what was tied, then apply a dressing soaked in syrup and oil. Then use treatment with twisting and ointments until it heals.
Chapter Fifty.
On the swelling that occurs from nerve torsion.
Just as swelling occurs in the artery and vein, so too does it occur in the nerve if there is a blow to it or excessive exertion or similar causes, and this is most common in the wrists and heels.
And in every place where the joints move, it is a soft tumor whose color resembles that of the rest of the body and which in most cases is painless. And if it is pressed hard, the patient feels a numbness similar to coldness, and the tumor is not located deep within the body.
It is under the skin and moves in every direction, not going forward or backward. And whatever part of it is located in the joints should not be treated with iron, for it may cause suppuration. And whatever part of it is located on the head or forehead.
Incise the skin with a scalpel, then if the tumor is small, grasp it with forceps and cut it from its base. And if it is large, hang it on a string and scrape it, then remove it and close the wound with stitches, treating it until it heals.
Chapter Fifty-One.
On Cutting Warts that Appear on the Abdomen.
It often happens that some people have warts on their abdomens and throughout their bodies, called fungal warts because they resemble mushrooms. Their origin is thin, and their head is thick, sometimes with split lips. They can be small or very large.
I once saw a man with two warts on his abdomen that looked exactly like mushrooms, with a thin white stalk sometimes splitting at the lips, and moisture constantly flowing from them. I cut them off and found one weighed about eighteen ounces and the other about six ounces.
To cut them, first observe if the patient is moist and if the warts are white, damp, thin-stalked. Cut it with a wide scalpel and have your cautery ready for there is often profuse bleeding when they are removed.
If bleeding becomes excessive, cauterize the wound immediately. If you find the patient cowardly and afraid of being cut with iron, take a strong lead thread and tie it tightly around the wart described above, leaving it for two days. Then increase the tension on the lead thread, continuing to tighten it.
Whenever a tumor is affected until it is severed and falls away from itself without pain, if the tumor is thick at its base there is some fear and risk in cutting it, especially if it is in the abdomen; but it may be possible to cut half of it or part of it.
Then cauterize it so that it does not return, and then treat it until the wound is healed. Beware of cutting a tumor that is dark in color, insensitive, and looks malignant for it is a cancerous tumor, and I will mention cancer after this.
Chapter Fifty-Two.
On the treatment of a scrotal hernia.
A scrotal hernia can be caused by many things, either from a rupture of the peritoneum on the abdomen, through which fat or intestine protrudes as is seen in other hernias, or from blood that wells up from a vein or artery as previously mentioned, or from gas that collects within it.
If it is due to a rupture of the peritoneum and protrusion of fat, then the tumor will be a color similar to that of the body, soft, and painless; its appearance will be different. If it is due to the protrusion of intestine, then its appearance will be even more different than what we have described. If you press it with your fingers, it will disappear and then return; there may also be gurgling sounds. It increases greatly when entering a bath or during strenuous exertion. If it is due to moisture, it will be soft.
It does not disappear when pressed with your hand, and it neither increases nor decreases. If it is due to blood, along with these signs, the tumor will appear darkened. If it is due to growing flesh, then the tumor will be hard, fixed in size.
If it is due to gas, then touching it will feel soft.  The course of action in this case is to look and see if the scrotal hernia is due to arterial or venous blood, or gas; if so, you should refrain from treating it because there is danger and risk involved, as I have already informed you in the chapter where I mentioned tumors that occur from arteries and veins.
If the tumor is before the intestine or the bladder, it should be ordered that the patient hold himself and stand straight, then mark all around the tumor with ink. Then order him to lie on his back between your hands, and cut around the tumor with a wide scalpel on the spot marked with ink. Then extend the middle of the tumor upwards with a large snare, and tie the incision site with a strong thread or silk ligature tightly.
The ligature should be a knot, then open the middle of the drawn tumor above the ligature and insert your second finger. Search for the intestine; if you find it has been caught by the ligature, loosen the knot and push the intestine back into the abdomen. If you find a bladder, extend it with a snare.
Cut off its excess, and if you encounter an artery or vein, tie it firmly and return to your work. Take two needles and insert strong threads into them, enter the needles into the incision you made around the tumor, crossing each other; then tighten the tumor in four places on the needles.
If you wish, remove the needles and leave the spot until the drawn flesh decays and falls off by itself, or cut it if it becomes thin and decayed. Then treat it with appropriate ointments until it heals. If the tumor is fleshy and grows in it…
or from moisture, you should pierce the tumor as we said and remove the blood or moisture you find in it, then treat it with something that heals the area.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On the Treatment of Cancer.
We have mentioned in the division types of cancer and how to treat it with medicine, and warned against treating it with iron lest it ulcerate, and we mentioned cancer arising in the uterus and warned against treating it.
The ancients mentioned that whenever cancer is in a place from which it can be entirely removed, such as cancer which is found in the breast or thigh, and similar limbs capable of having it completely excised, especially if it is beginning and small; but if it has advanced and become large, it should not be approached.
I have not been able to cure anyone from it, nor have I seen anyone before me who has reached that goal.
Then set the tumor upright so that it can be worked on, then place the pincers suitable for it in the cancerous growth and grip it firmly from all sides with the skin being thoroughly examined so that nothing of its roots remains, and leave the blood to flow and do not cut it quickly.
Rather press the places and express all the thick blood with your hand or as much as you can with instruments, if, during your work, a great hemorrhage occurs from cutting an artery or vein, then ligature the vein until the bleeding stops and then treat it with all appropriate treatment until it heals.
Chapter Forty-Five.
On the Treatment of Fistula.
We have previously informed you of the types of dropsy and how the gathering of water occurs, as well as the signs of each type and its treatment with medicine; the type that is treated with iron is the sharp variety alone and the blunted variety and the fleshy variety are not treated with iron at all.
If that fails, and you have treated this sharp type of fistula with medicine and it has not been successful, then see if the patient is excessively weak or if he has another disease besides the fistula, such as…
coughing or diarrhea, and the like, then do not treat him with iron, for it is dangerous. If you see that the patient is strong and has no other disease except fistula alone, and he is neither a boy nor an old man, then proceed as follows: have the patient stand upright before you. and have a servant behind him press his belly with his hands and push the water downwards towards the groin, then take a pointed bistoury of this form:
limited on two sides, long and pointed at the end like a bistoury except that it has a slight bend so as not to go astray during the operation into the intestines and harm them, then observe if the fistula originated from the side of the intestines.
The incision should be made three fingerbreadths below the groin, in a line with it, above the pubis. If the hernia arose from liver disease, make your incision on the left side of the groin, three fingerbreadths away. If it arose from spleen disease, make the incision on the right side, three fingerbreadths away.
The incision should also not be made on the side the patient wishes to lie on so that pus does not flow into that weak spot. Then pierce the entire skin with the instrument and insert the instrument into the incision, raising your hand.
With the scalpel, separate the skin from the peritoneum as if you were peeling it off, a distance of about a fingernail or so. Then pierce the peritoneum until the scalpel reaches an empty space, which is where the fluid is located. Withdraw it and insert the instrument pictured here into the opening.
It is like a tube made of thin reed, fashioned from silver or copper or smooth polished boxwood, with a small hole at the bottom and three holes along its sides, two on one side and one on the other, as you see.
Sometimes its end is fashioned into a sharp point like a writing quill, in this manner:
There is a ring at its upper end. When the instrument reaches the fluid, it descends from its hour to the instrument and drains a moderate amount of fluid because if you drain more than necessary in a given time, the patient may die from dissolution of his animal spirit.
Or he may suffer a fainting spell near death, but drain it according to his strength and what indicates this to you are the patient's pulse and complexion. Then remove the instrument and retain the fluid because it is retained by its hour.
This is due to the skin holding the hole in the peritoneum which you were instructed to close in that manner, then reinsert the instrument another day if you think the patient can tolerate it.
And a small quantity also comes out of the water, you do this for several days while on your neck and preserving from error until there is nothing left but a little water; if you are afraid for the patient and leave a lot of water, then treat him by burying him in hot sand.
And a lot of sweating in the bath and the sun and patience with thirst and treatment with drying medicines until he is cured, and it is cauterized on the stomach, liver and spleen after drawing out the water as previously described.

As for the one who has a narrow hole, it is treated with lead as we said for many days until it widens, and those who have the hole in the wrong place - some are born with the hole at the end of the urethra and cannot urinate forward.

Chapter fifty-six.
In the rupture which occurs in the scrotum, testicle, blackness and corruption and adhesion of the scrotum to the testicle.
This rupture often occurs in the urethra; it is a malignant fleshy growth, which may be benign or malignant.  The benign type should be suspended with a fine ligature.
Cut it until it is entirely cleansed, then apply cotton wool soaked in Egyptian ointment and subsequently treat it with palm ointment until it heals. If the rupture is malignant and dark in color, cauterization should be used after cutting and cleansing it.
