Chapter seventy-eight.
On the expulsion of the placenta.
If the placenta is retained after childbirth, you should order the patient to hold her breath and then cough it up with a speculum while pressing your hand over her mouth and nostrils. If it comes out this way, well and good; otherwise, take a measure.
Make a hole in the lid and put in it herbs that are open for the mouth of the womb, such as fennel, chamomile, anise, chamomile, lavender, and mint; all these drugs or some of them. Immerse them in water and put them on the fire. Then place a silver tube on the hole in the lid of the vessel and the other end in the mouth of the womb.
Hold it until the vapor reaches the bottom of the womb, then sneeze as we said; for the placenta will come out quickly. If it remains after this treatment and does not come out, order the midwife to dip her left hand in sheep fat or the saliva of a dog.
Then insert it into the vagina and search for the placenta; if she finds it, grasp it and pull it out little by little until it comes out. If it is stuck deep in the womb, insert the hand as we have described until she finds the placenta; then draw it out gently and with compassion.
So that the womb does not fall out when pulled hard, but it should be moved gently to the right and left sides, then increase the amount of pulling; for it will respond then and get rid of adhesion. If the mouth of the womb is closed, we have described the treatment by sneezing and the treatment with the vessel and herbs.
If it does not come out with all that we have described, beware of violence in pulling again, but you should tie what came out from the womb to the thigh of the woman and then inject her with the four-fold ointment; for it will soften it after a few days.
It will dissolve and come out, but if it rots, a bad smell rises from it to the stomach and head, and this harms the patient; so you should use the appropriate fumigation.
Therefore, since some of the ancients tried fumigation with aloeswood and dried figs, a picture of the instrument used by women to fumigate themselves when they have menstrual retention and the placenta.
It is made of lead shaped like a funnel or of copper, the thin end is placed in the nostril and the wide end on the fire and the incense is put on the embers, hold it until that incense goes and replace it with another.
Chapter Ninety-Seventh.
In the Treatment of the Unperforated Fistula.
Many boys may be born with their fistulas unperforated, often closed by a thin membrane. Therefore, the midwife should pierce this membrane with her finger; otherwise, let it be incised with a sharp bistoury, taking care not to touch the muscle.
Then place a wick soaked in wine and oil on it, then treat it with ointments until it heals. If you fear that it will close again, insert a lead tube into the orifice for many days, removing it when the child desires to defecate.
It may also happen that the fistula is closed by a retracted wound or a tumor, which should be incised and then treated as we have described, with dressing and packing.
Chapter Eighty.
On the Treatment of Fistulas Occurring in the Lower Regions.
Fistulas occurring in the lower regions are a complication and thickening that occurs near the anus, either externally or in the surrounding space, from one side or another; they may be single or multiple.
When this complication suppurates, a watery, white fluid or thin pus discharges from it. Some of these fistulas may extend to the rectum and intestines; others are not penetrating. Those that are penetrating can be recognized by the passage of feces and flatus through them when the patient defecates.
Sometimes worms emerge from them. Fistulas in the surrounding space may penetrate to the bladder and the urethra; others may penetrate to the hip joint and the coccyx. A fistula penetrating to the anus is also known, but not all fistulas are penetrating.
Insert your index finger into the anus and insert a fine probe into the nasor from copper or iron if there is no bend in the nasor, but if there is a bend, insert a fine lead probe or a horsehair.
Until you feel the probe or hair in your finger, if you do not feel it at all and nothing of feces, wind, or worms appears from the hole as we have said, then know that it is impassable, so hasten to treatment.
And if the nasor is passable to the bladder or to the urinary tract, its indication is the urine coming out from it and its refusal to be united with the place by medicines, but if it is passable to the thigh joint or the coccyx, its sign is the probe reaching there.
If there is no bend to the bone and pain exists in a certain direction and pus constantly comes out of it, and treatment does not work in it and it does not unite with ointment, these passable nasors are all incurable.
And its treatment is a futile effort for whoever undertakes it from ignorant physicians, but as for the one that is impassable and not chronic, healing is expected with iron as I will describe and what I have tried, which is to lay the patient down on their back between your hands.
Extend their legs upwards and their thighs sloping towards their stomach, then insert a copper or lead probe if there is a bend in the nasor until you know where the probe ends; if the patient feels it towards the anus, then you should insert your index finger into the anus.
If you feel the probe in your finger having penetrated by itself, exposed without feeling any fascia or flesh between your finger and it, then know for sure that it is passable; so do not labor on it, as there is no healing from it as we have said.
They have said that it heals in some people rarely, and of the treatment which is hoped for its benefit is to heat a thin iron according to the width of the nostril as previously mentioned, and insert it heated into the nostril until it reaches near the septum, then return it two or three times.
Until you know that all of that excess, loose flesh which resembles a bird's feather pipe has been burned, then treat it with a bandage soaked in butter so that the burned flesh comes out, and then treat it with cooked ointments, for if he heals.
Otherwise, it will not heal by any other treatment ever, and if you insert the probe and it does not reach your finger at the septum and there is a thick veil of flesh or peritoneum between it and the probe, and you see the nostril as follows on the surface of the skin, then at that time cut the skin from the beginning of the nostril. And you pass with the incision while the probe is in the nostril until you reach with the incision where the end of the probe has reached, and then the probe is released and falls, and then clean out that excess flesh which resembles a bird's feather pipe so that nothing remains of it.
Then treat it with cooked ointments until it heals, and if the blood overpowers you and stands between you and your work in cutting that flesh, then the best thing to do and the quickest benefit is to cauterize it with fire or a caustic drug because cauterization by fire combines two good states: burning that excess flesh.
And cutting the blood and drying the moisture, then treat it with a bandage soaked in butter or powdered sulfur mixed with oil until the place festers and that burned flesh comes out in the pus, then at that time treat the wound with cooked ointments that grow hard flesh.
And these are the medicines which have astringent and drying properties, for if it closes up it heals and you need not fear a return, but if the nostril has reached the depth of the septum and is far from the surface of the body, then insert your finger into the septum and explore with it.
If you feel the probe and there is a veil of peritoneum or flesh between you and it, and it is close to penetration, then the work in it is only on the path of hope and desire. This is because you should use one of three methods: either cauterize it as we have said.
Or you may cut it until you reach its bottom so that you can insert the probe and treat it from nearby, but do not overdo the incision lest you cut the muscle surrounding the anus, which would cause the patient to have involuntary bowel movements. Then treat him as we have mentioned; perhaps he will be cured as we have said.
Or you may penetrate that veil and puncture it after cutting it near the anus as we have said, either with a probe or with another sharp-pointed instrument. Then clean out all the adhering flesh in the incision as thoroughly as possible.
Then suture the entire wound, along with the incision and the upper opening, until it is closed, leaving the fistula open inside the anus. This will be easier for the patient, and the fistula may even heal in this condition.
And if you insert the probe into the fistula and it is located on a side of the anus, toward the body surface and the skin around the anus, then take a probe with a perforated end like a shoemaker's awl in this manner:
And insert into it a twisted thread of five strands or so, then insert the probe with the thread into the fistula until it reaches its bottom. If it is perforated in the edge of the anus from the inside nearby...
Then pull the thread out of that hole by inserting your finger into the anus and pulling out the end of the thread. Gather both ends together and tie them tightly, leaving it for a day or two.  Whenever the thread cuts into the flesh, tighten it well so that the flesh between the two ends will be severed and fall off. Then treat the wound until it heals and is cured.
If the nostril is not perforated, perforate it as best you can, but if it is deep, do not do so for fear of cutting the muscle. Then proceed with it as we have mentioned until it heals.
The image shows a pointed scalpel with which to incise the nostrils. One edge is extremely sharp, and the other edge is not sharp so as not to cut what is unnecessary as you see.
Chapter Eighty-One.
On the treatment of hemorrhoids that bleed and their excision, as well as the treatment of fissures.
Hemorrhoids are of two types: either they are inside the anus, resembling red balloons or grapes, and come in small and large sizes, and blood always flows from them.
They can be single or multiple, and located outside the anus at its edges. However, those that are outside the anus are usually less moist and produce a yellowish fluid or a small amount of continuous blood flow. They are the color of the body.
The treatment for those located inside the anus is to order the patient to defecate and strain until the anus protrudes and the hemorrhoids are visible to you. Then quickly grasp them with forceps or your fingernail and cut them at their base. If the forceps do not hold them due to moisture and relaxation,
grab them with a rough cloth or a piece of toasted bread, or pull them with your fingers and then cut them. After cutting, sprinkle some sharp dust on them to act as cautery, or cauterize them as previously described in the chapter on cautery.
Then treat it with all other treatments until it heals. If the anus does not protrude, inject the patient with an injection containing a mild irritant to wash out what is inside and encourage it to protrude quickly when the patient strains. The treatment of hemorrhoids outside the anus is simple and easy.
Take it with your fingernail or hang it on a thread and cut it, then treat it as we mentioned until it heals. And whoever dislikes cutting with iron should use its core in this manner: take a twisted thread and put it in a needle.
Then draw the wart upwards and pass it through the needle at its origin from the other side, and wind both ends of the thread below the needle while it is obstructing and tighten the wart tightly, then tie the thread and remove the needle. Do this to all the warts and leave one without coring it.
So that pus drains from it, then place a rag soaked in rose oil or cerate on the seat and put it on the seat, and order the patient to rest, then leave him until the warts fall off. And when the warts fall off, treat them with ointments and all other treatments until they heal.
As for the treatment of anal fissures, it often arises from dryness and constipation. If it persists and medicine does not work, it should be scraped with a scalpel blade or your fingernail until it becomes moist and the upper scab that prevents it from healing is removed.
Then treat it until it heals properly. If it does not heal, repeat the scraping more intensely than the first time until it bleeds and sheds, then treat it, for it will be cured.
Chapter Eighty-Two.
On the Treatment of Inverted and Non-Inverted Warts, Dry Warts, and Plantar Warts.
A wart is a round nodule the color of the body, resembling a nail head, found throughout the body, especially on the soles of the feet and toes. It causes pain when walking.
It should be incised around the wart, grasped with forceps or a thread and removed from its base, then treated. If you wish, cauterize it as previously mentioned in its chapter. As for dry warts, each one is a small, rough, adherent, round protuberance on the surface of the body.
It often occurs in the hands of boys and its treatment is to lengthen the wart and cut it or cauterize it with silk thread or a hair until it falls off. If you wish, cauterize it as previously mentioned with fire or a caustic drug.
Inverted and non-inverted warts may also be removed with the instrument I have mentioned in the treatment of moles. Moles are also small, thick, adherent bumps on the surface of the body that go deep down; if they are affected by cold, one feels a burning sensation like that of an ant bite.
It occurs throughout the body, and most commonly on the hands. Its treatment is to take a tube made from a goose feather or a strong eagle feather and place it around the mole so that the tube forms a circle around it; then turn your hand with the tube until you cut what is around the mole.
You make it go deep into the flesh and the mole will detach from its base; if you wish, you can do this with a copper or iron tube in the same manner.
The top of the tube should be constricted and twisted to make it easy for the fingers to hold and turn it. If you wish, you can cauterize the mole as previously described in the section on cauterizing warts.
Chapter Seventy-Third.
On Instruments Used for Injections in Cases of Hemorrhoids, Diarrhea, and Colic.
An injector may be made of silver, porcelain, or beaten and hammered copper. Small and large versions of this instrument may be made depending on the user. Those used to treat young boys are small, and for those whose hemorrhoids are narrow or painful, the injectors should be very fine.  Image of a large injector:
The upper funnel where the syringe is attached is wide, and it has a collar where the syringe is fastened as you see. Its lower end, which enters the rectum, is constricted and very smooth; there are two holes on one side near the tip and one hole on the other side.
The opening of the wound should be the width of the penis or a little wider, and the tube into which you put the medicines should be made from an animal bladder or sheepskin, shaped like a small dish with a diameter of one span.
It should be pierced with many holes, spaced evenly around it, each hole the width of a finger. Then a tightly spun thread made from two strands of ten threads or so is passed through these holes and used to gather up the tube like a small dish. Once medicine is put in, the top of the injector should be firmly squeezed between two stones and then injected. This is a diagram of the tube and the opening.
To inject it, fill the bladder or tube with warmed medicine as much as the patient can tolerate. Be careful that it is not too hot or too cold. Then the patient should lie on his back and raise his legs upward.
Place a cushion or blanket underneath him, then grease the tip of the injector with ointment, egg white, fenugreek saliva or something similar. Then gently insert it into the anus. A servant should hold the tube with both hands and squeeze firmly until all of the contents are emptied into the intestine.
Then remove the injector and have the patient raise his legs against the wall. If the medicine urges him to defecate, he should hold it in as long as possible. If he sleeps with it all night, it will be most effective. If injecting for diarrhea, hasten its expulsion by repeating the injection until he is cured.
If there are hemorrhoids or painful tumors in the anus, use a very gentle and smooth injector, unlike those used for children. This is a diagram of a gentle injector with its skin stretched tight:
Chapter Eighty-Four.
On Surgical Procedures.
I previously mentioned the treatment of cysts in the section on cauterization, but I will now describe their complete treatment by hand and medicine. Surgical procedures vary depending on what is being operated on and where the wound is located.
For surgical wounds are numerous, such as a blow from a stone or a cut from a sword or knife or a stab with a spear, stick, arrow, and the like of many things.
As for surgical wounds according to the locations on the body, such as a wound that occurs on the head or neck or chest or abdomen or liver and so forth of the organs.
And I will describe the treatment of some wounds to make them a measure and a law for all other wounds, and I begin with simple head wounds, especially since the complex ones will be mentioned at the beginning of the third chapter of this book.
I say that if a simple wound occurs on the head and there is no bone fracture, then if it was from a blow of a stone or something like it and the skin had only been split and the wound was large, and I feared for the patient the occurrence of a hot swelling, then I hastened and dissected the scalp to its place. Do not delay this and let out blood for him to the extent of his strength, unless something prevents him from being bled, especially if the blood coming out of the wound is slight, let the bleeding be done opposite the injured side.
Warn the patient against filling himself up, drinking and coarse foods, for if something prevents him from being bled, let him reduce his food and not approach meats or drinks, and bear on the wound if a hot swelling occurs, cotton soaked in rose oil alone or with a drink that has astringent properties.
And if you are confident that there will be no hot swelling, then bear on the wound if it is soft with its blood and has not been changed by the air I will describe to you shortly, but if it has been changed by the air, then bear on it some ointments.
Until it produces pus, then treat it until it heals, if the wound was large and from a sword cut or something like it, and its lips did not come together with sutures, then bring them together by stitching as I describe in stitching abdominal wounds.
If the skin has been stripped from the bone and hangs, and its adherence is only slight, cut it off. Then treat the wound with medications that promote the formation of firm flesh in place of the skin, and instruct the patient to eat strengthening foods such as heads and trotters, etc.
If putrefaction occurs in the skin and it does not adhere to the bone, cut away all the putrefied flesh, wash it, and then treat it. If there is an artery or vein bleeding from these wounds and the bleeding does not stop with medication, search for the artery.
If you find it, do not amputate it unless necessary; cut it with a scalpel and ligate it. If needed and the aforementioned methods are ineffective, cauterize it until the bleeding stops. If these wounds are small and simple, their treatment is easy: leave them covered with this poultice while they are bloody.
Before the air changes it, the poultice is made as follows: take one part frankincense, two parts mastic, and three parts slaked lime or unslaked lime; grind all of them, sift them, and fill the wound with it. Bandage it tightly so that it adheres well.
It will coagulate with the blood, then bandage it tightly with strips of cloth and leave it undisturbed as long as there is no swelling; for the medication will not separate from it until it heals. Lime alone may be used if frankincense and mastic are unavailable.
This same procedure can also be done with a dressing on many large wounds if it is applied skillfully and tightly bandaged, as long as the wounds are fresh and bloody and have not been changed by the air. However, if they have been somewhat altered by the air, especially in summertime,
then applying this poultice is ineffective; treat it instead with some stimulating ointments or apply a gruel of barley flour made with water and honey until it produces pus, then treat it further with all appropriate remedies until it heals.
If a fracture occurs with the wound, and it is slight, draw it together with a ligature. The treatment of head fractures has already been mentioned, in the chapter on neck surgery. If the wound is in the neck, its treatment differs not from that of a simple head wound.
If a nerve or artery in the neck has been severed, and it is a nerve, there is no remedy except to avoid treating it with anything that constricts severely, such as ligatures or bandages, for these injure and inflame the nerve.
Nothing cold should be applied to the wound at all, for the essence of the nerve is cold, and its connection with the brain, which is the noblest of organs, would be harmed. Treatment should consist of soft remedies, such as rosewater washed with fresh water repeatedly moistened with oil or violet ointment.
If it is also washed with fresh water and all other medicinal stones in this manner, and moist ointments are applied, but if the wound is large, use sutures or bring its lips together with bandages, then treat it until it heals.
If the wound has depth and a collection of pus has formed in its lower part, lance it at the lowest point. If an artery in the wound has been severed and you see its blood flowing, amputate it, ligate it, or cauterize it if necessary.
If the wound has severed some or all of the cartilages of the larynx, but the vocal cords are intact, bring the edges of the skin together with sutures over the trachea. Do not touch the larynx itself but restore it to its natural shape and then bind it tightly, leaving it for several days.
Then treat it with ointments that constrict and dry it until it heals. If the wound has affected the bone, examine it. If there are bone fragments, draw them all out or as many as possible. Leave those you cannot reach until the wound has granulated and healed, making their removal easier.
If the wound is fresh, sprinkle over it the powder we have described. If a bone becomes putrid and necrotic in the wound, treat it with what will be mentioned in its chapter, in thoracic surgery and that between the shoulders.
If it is a stab wound from a spear or knife and you see a hollow, look to see if air comes out of it when the patient breathes. If so, know that it is a mortal wound. But if there is no hollow and it is fresh with blood, do not put powder in it at first, nor tighten it lest blood be retained in its cavity.
For if it reaches the heart, it will kill the patient. But put an attractive ointment in it and apply it twice during the day. If you do not have ointment available, put a worn cotton ball into the wound to absorb the moisture that comes out of it, and let the patient sleep on his wound.
So that what gathers in it may solidify. And if the wound has been three days or more and the patient has not experienced tetanus, a bad palpitation, or shortness of breath, and you see that all his other conditions are good, know that the wound is healthy. Treat it with bandaging and all other treatments until it heals.
But if pus forms and discharges continuously, know that it has become necrotic. Treat it as described in its chapter. Let the patient sleep on his wound so that what gathers in it may solidify. And if the wound is on the chest or back, treat it with stitching as previously mentioned.
Whether it is large and requires stitching or small and can be treated with powder, if it has affected the bone and pieces have broken off, search the wound and immediately remove those pieces if they are clean. If they are not clean, leave them until...
the wound becomes putrid, for then it is easier to remove them. As for all other acute wounds that occur in the rest of the organs, their treatment follows the same rules as those we have mentioned.
Chapter Eighty-Fifth.
On abdominal surgery, the protrusion of intestines and their suturing.
The rupture which appears in the abdomen may be large or small or medium, the large one from which intestines or several intestines may protrude, their introduction and suturing being more difficult and harder.
And the small rupture may also be difficult in another way, it being necessary that its return should be more difficult, and if the intestines are not promptly returned to their place they swell and thicken, making their introduction difficult; therefore, the best ruptures are those of medium size, since the return of the intestines is not as difficult with them as it is with these two types.
Know that the suturing of the abdomen is done in four ways; two types of sutures are suitable for suturing the abdomen and for suturing all other surgical wounds, and the other two types are general for all surgical procedures, although they are more specific for suturing the abdomen.
And I will describe them to you one by one with explanation, saying that if the wound is small and some intestines protrude from it and their return is difficult, this is due to one of two things: either the smallness of the rupture as we said, or the intestines having been swollen before by cold air.
If so, it should be heated by dipping a sponge or cloth in lukewarm water alone, or which may have been cooked in it saffron and roses and sprinkled on the intestines until the swelling subsides.
A drink containing astringents may also be used, and it is better than water alone in resolving swelling. If you see that the swelling has subsided, moisten the intestines with water which has been cooked with henna or sesame; this will make its entry easier.
If it is still impossible for it to return after this treatment, then make a small incision in the rupture with the instrument used for making fistulas. The first treatment, if successful, is better than an incision; however, one resorts to an incision only when necessary and all other methods have failed. This is the image of the instrument:
One of its sides is curved and limited, the other is not limited. The thin end should not be as fine as a lancet, but rather slightly blunt; it is an instrument resembling a small spatula, as you see. If the wound widens and the intestines protrude, they should be returned to their natural form.
Its proper place, if possible, is best. But if the tear is wide and in the lower abdomen, the patient should lie on his back with his legs raised higher than his head. If it is in the upper abdomen, his head and chest should be raised higher than his lower body.
Similarly, if the tear is on one side of the abdomen, always aim to have the side where the surgery is being performed raised higher than the other side. This should be done for both major and moderate surgeries.
For minor surgeries, position the patient as best you can. Then have a gentle assistant hold the entire tear with his hands, bringing the edges together and revealing a small portion at a time to the person suturing.
This is the general description of a single stitch: take one or more needles, depending on the width of the wound, and leave a distance from the edge of the tear equal to the thickness of a fingernail. Insert one needle without thread into the edges of the skin. Insert it into the peritoneal edges beneath the skin from the inside, so that the needle passes through to the other side. This brings together both the edges of the skin and the edges of the peritoneum, creating four layers. Then tighten a doubled thread around the needle several times on both sides until the wound edges meet firmly.
Leave a distance equal to the thickness of a finger and insert another needle, then secure it with the thread as you did with the first needle. Continue in this manner with as many needles as needed until the entire wound is closed. Use needles that are of medium thickness.
Because very fine needles quickly cut flesh and thick ones have difficulty entering the skin, it is necessary for them to be of medium thinness and thickness.  It should not be inserted into the edge of the skin near the wound.
So that the flesh is not quickly cut and the wound does not open before it heals, nor should it be too far apart in suturing so that the wound cannot heal. Then, cut the ends of the needles so they do not hurt the patient when he sleeps and make for him pads of linen cloth from each side to hold the ends of the needles.
Leave them until you know that the wound has healed. This type of suturing with needles is thus best for minor surgeries because it may suffice in its stitching with one or two needles or so.
As for the description of suturing for a second intention wound, it is to gather together by stitching the four edges, meaning the edges of the skin and the edges of the peritoneum at once with a needle containing a twisted thread moderate in thinness and thickness; then, if this needle passes through those four edges, return the needle from the side where you began.
Let the thread fall entangled from above the wound so that the stitching is according to the stitching of a sack which is tightened with it and place between each stitch and stitch a distance equal to the width of a little finger. This type of stitching is known to all people.
With this stitching, I operated on a man who had a wound in his abdomen inflicted by a knife. The rupture of the surgery was more than a handbreadth and he had eviscerated nearly two handbreadths of the middle intestine and the rupture was in the middle of the abdomen.
I returned him after his intestines had been outside the wound for twenty-four hours. The wound healed in about fifteen days, and I treated him until he recovered and lived for many years afterwards, acting normally in all of his affairs. The physicians had judged that he would not recover at all.
It is amazing that I did not treat him with ointment because I was in a place where there were no medicines. So I would put cotton on the wound twice a day and commit to washing it with honey water until it healed.
As for one of the special types of suturing, I mention it according to the words of Galen, which is to begin the suture from the skin and insert the needle from outside to inside. And when the needle passes through the skin and into the muscle going straight along the entire length of the abdomen.
I left the edge of the peritoneum on the side where I inserted the needle and passed the needle through its other edge from inside to outside in the other edge of the suture. And when I pass it, I pass it again in this same edge from the suture from outside to inside.
And leave the edge of the peritoneum on this side and pass the needle through its other edge from inside to outside and pass it as you pass it in the peritoneum to the edge of the suture that is in its direction until it passes through completely.
Then begin again from this same side and line it with the edge from the peritoneum on the other side, and bring the needle out of the skin near it, then return it to that skin and line the edge of the peritoneum on the other side with this edge from the suture.
And bring it out of the skin from its side, and do that again and again until you sew the entire surgery according to that example. The description of this suture is that it sews a double suture like fur.
By sewing the peritoneum once from one side with a skin edge and leaving it once from the other side until it is emptied. As for the second type of special suture that he also mentioned, this is his word verbatim.
And it is to sew it according to the example of how some healers sew it, by bringing together each part with its counterpart naturally. They bring together an edge of the peritoneum with its other edge and an edge of the suture with its other edge. This is as I describe to you.
The needle should be inserted into the margin of the peritoneum closest to you from outside and passed into it alone, leaving the peritoneal margins. Then the needle is returned and passed from outside to inside in both peritoneal margins, and then returned again.
And it is passed from inside to outside in the other margin of the peritoneum on the opposite side. This type of stitching is better than the common easy stitching, which is the stitching mentioned before. This is the text of Galen. Some have said that abdominal suturing may be done with two needles. This is done by threading a single thread into both needles and beginning by inserting one needle from your side and passing it to the other side, then inserting the other needle from the opposite side and passing it toward you according to the stitching of the saddle makers.
Know that if a rupture is in the middle of the abdomen, its stitching is more difficult than in any other place in the abdomen. As for treating the wound, its rule in treatment is like that of all other surgeries. That is, if you grasp the wound soft with its blood before the air changes it and return the intestines, line them up, and tighten them, then apply a poultice of saltpeter on it.
If the wound has been changed by air, apply some of the ointments that you prepare until it suppurates and the stitches fall out, and then the peritoneum and omentum unite. Then treat it like all other surgeries until it is healed.
If you do not have medications available, apply soft cotton as soon as pus begins to form and change it twice a day as I have taught you until it is healed. You will not need any other treatment in most cases if the surgeries are simple. If you fear that the wound may involve the major organs,
you should soak soft wool in lukewarm oil or rose ointment and place it around the areas between the groin and armpit. If he feels pain or foulness in his intestines, which often occurs, inject him with a lukewarm black astringent beverage.
Especially if the gangrene has reached the intestine and become a penetrating wound into it, know that what was from the intestines thick is easier to heal and what was thin is more difficult to heal.
As for the intestine known as the fasting one, it does not accept healing from any surgery that befalls it at all, due to the abundance of its large veins and the thinness of its body and its proximity to the nature of nerves. As for if what protruded from the wound was pus and you found it soft, return it according to how you returned the intestine itself.
If a period has passed and it has become green or black, you should tighten it with a thread above the place that has blackened from it so that bleeding does not occur, for there are veins and arteries in the pus; then cut what is below that ligature.
Make the two ends of the thread hanging from below the surgery outside of it to make it easy for you to pull and remove them when the pus falls out and the wound suppurates. As for mentioning the wound that occurs in the intestine, if a tear occurs in the intestine and it is small.
It may be possible to splint it in some people, for I once saw a man who had been wounded in his abdomen by a spear thrust and the wound was to the right of the stomach. I probed the wound and it became straight, from which feces and gas were exiting. So I treated him as if I did not hope for his recovery. I continued to treat him gently until he recovered and the area healed together. When I saw that the area had joined together, I feared for the patient that an accident might happen in his abdomen, but nothing bad happened to him at all, and he remained in the best of health. It is true, he eats and drinks, has intercourse, enters the bath, and relaxes in his service. Some experienced people have mentioned that whenever a tear occurs in the intestine and it is small, it should be sutured in this manner.
Take large ants with heads, then bring together the edges of the wound and place one ant on it with its mouth open. When it seizes hold and tightens its jaws, cut off its head; it will adhere firmly and not come undone. Then place another ant near the first.
Continue to do this with several ants, according to the size of the wound. Then return the wound to its place and sew it up. These heads will remain stuck in the intestine until it heals and is well. This method will not cause any harm to the patient at all. It is also possible to suture the intestine with fine thread as well.
To draw the animal glue out of its container, take one end of the thread from the container and pull it tight. Then tie a fine, spun linen thread to that end, and thread this into a needle. The linen thread, with the animal glue on it, is then used to suture the intestine. It is then returned to the abdominal cavity. This type of suturing with ants and animal glue is merely a matter of hope and experimentation.
If the tear is large and wide, especially if it is in one of the thin intestines, there is no remedy and it will not heal at all.
Chapter Eighty-Sixth.
On the Treatment of Colds and Catarrh.
Know that any wound or swelling, if it becomes chronic and does not heal but instead develops into an ulcer that constantly discharges pus, is called a "naasoor" in general terms, wherever it may be located. We call it a "cold sore."
It is, in fact, a hardened mass of white matter, without pain, that has a cavity like the quill of a bird's feather.
That is why some people call it a "feather." Sometimes it is moist and constantly discharges pus. At other times, the moisture may stop. This moisture can be abundant or scarce, thick or thin.
And I presented in the division when mentioning surgeries that every ulcer that does not heal and flesh does not grow in it is due to one of nine causes, one of which is either a lack of blood in the body or its bad quality, or because there is hard flesh inside it and on its lips, preventing the growth of good flesh. Or it is much afflicted with pus and filth, or because the ulcer itself is putrid and the material supplying it is of poor quality, or because the medicine is not appropriate for its treatment, or because a corruption has occurred in the town of the plague type, or because there is a peculiarity in the town.
Or it is much afflicted with pus and filth, or because the ulcer itself is putrid and the material supplying it is of poor quality, or because the medicine is not appropriate for its treatment, or because a corruption has occurred in the town of the plague type, or because there is a peculiarity in the town. As was shown in the city of Saragossa, where the ripening of diseases is difficult and the healing of tumors is slow by nature, or because there is one bone or several bones in it, and I have explained all these causes, their signs, and their treatments with medicine in the division.
And I should inform you here about the treatment of fistulas and colds that occur through manual work, know that fistulas can occur in all parts of the body, some of which end in large veins, arteries, or nerves.
Or to the peritoneum, or to the intestine, or to the bladder, or to a rib from the ribs, or to a vertebra from the vertebrae of the back, or to a joint from the complex joints, composed in a complex manner such as the hand and foot, and some of which lead to a nearby major organ and so on.
So, any of these fistulas on this description are of the difficult diseases that do not accept treatment and working on them is trouble and ignorance. If any of these fistulas have not reached the places I mentioned, then hope and ambition for its treatment are warranted.
And I wanted you to reach that knowledge, so take a speculum of copper or iron if the fistula passes straight and search it with it. If there is a bend in the fistula, then search it with a lead speculum, as lead softens its body.
If the fistula, upon entering and turning towards the bend, has many openings and you cannot detect them with a probe, inject fluid into one of its mouths. The moisture which you inject into it will travel towards the other mouths and flow out.
Then thoroughly investigate in any way possible to determine whether there is bone or nerve, or whether the fistula is deep or shallow, or whether it is a single fistula with many openings. Be sure to ascertain all of this to the best of your ability, through careful questioning and by observing the pain when you press your hand on the area.
And similarly, consider the signs and causes of the tumor. Once you have a clear understanding of all these factors, proceed with treatment confidently.
If the tumor is apparent and close, or in a healthy place far from a joint, nerve, artery, vein, or any of the places mentioned to you, then cut open the tumor as previously described and remove from it all adhesions, corrupt flesh, and excess flesh.
And remove anything that grows in its mouth, such as flesh or a pustule, and treat it until it heals. If the tumor is deep-seated and on a straight course, then you should cut it open as deeply as possible and then cleanse it of all its corrupt flesh.
Then use twisted lint saturated with sharp medicines and insert it into the bottom of the tumor which you can reach with the iron. Do this several times so that the sharp medicine will consume all that remains in the bottom of the tumor from corruption.
Then force it to heal with ointments that promote healthy flesh growth. If it does not heal in this way, then remove it as previously described. If the cause of the tumor is bone and you have confirmed this, then examine it and cut it open as I told you unless something prevents you from doing so.
If a vein, nerve or major organ is exposed as we said, and the bone is revealed with some corruption and blackness, then scrape it until the corruption is gone and treat it with what promotes flesh growth until it heals. If it does not heal and pus continues to form, as it did before.
Know that you have not reached the complete removal of corruption. Expose everything on it again and thoroughly scrape and clean it with your utmost effort. Then force it to heal. If it heals, good; if not, know that this corruption is deep within a cavity. Expose everything again.
Thoroughly scrape and clean it with your utmost effort. If the corrupt bone is small and you are able to extract it, then extract it with delicate forceps suitable for this purpose.
If there are many bones, draw out all of them and do not leave anything behind. If any ligaments obstruct you, and cutting them presents no danger, cut those ligaments and the flesh, if there is any, and free the bones.
If you cannot draw out the bones at once, cover the wound with something that will cause it to fester and leave it for several days until the flesh and ligaments around those bones become putrid. Preserve the wound so that the incision you made does not close up.
Rather, if you fear that, place upon it cotton wool soaked in powdered sulfur mixed with oil or cotton wool soaked in Egyptian ointment or one of the green ointments. For if those ligaments fester and the bones are softened, it will be easy to draw them out.
But if it is one large bone, such as the shinbone or the thighbone and so forth, and only its surface has decayed, scrape it deeply until that blackness and decay are gone, then bind up the wound. But if a large part of it has decayed and the decay has reached the marrow of the bone, it must be removed.
Cut away the whole bone to where the decay ends; then treat it so that it heals and fuses together. I will tell you about a young man who had an injury in his leg, as an example and help for your treatment: this young man was of mature age.
He was about thirty years old and he had pain in his leg, caused by something moving inside his body until it reached the leg and a large tumor developed, with no external cause. Time passed and doctors made mistakes so that the tumor opened up.
A great deal of material flowed out and he was treated badly so that the leg became ulcerated, with many openings all discharging pus and bodily fluids. A group of doctors treated him for about two years, but there wasn't a skilled surgeon among them.
When I reached him, I saw his leg and the humors flowing from those openings in a great stream, and the man had become emaciated and his color yellowed. I then inserted a probe into one of those openings and it led to the bone, and then I explored all the openings.
I found that some of them led to others from all sides of the leg, so I proceeded and made an incision along one of those openings until I exposed some bone, which I found decayed, eaten away, blackened, putrefying, and pierced through until the hole reached the marrow. Then I spread open what had been revealed.
I was able to remove the decayed bone and I believed that there was nothing in the bone except for that decay which I had cut out and spread, as though I had eradicated it. Then I dressed the wound with astringent medications for a long period, but it did not heal. I then returned and exposed the bone again, above the first exposure.
I found the decay adhering to the bone, so I spread open what appeared of that decay as well, and then I tried to dress it, but it did not heal or join together. I exposed it again, and I continued to cut away pieces of the bone, attempting to bind them, but they would not heal until I had removed about a handbreadth's worth of bone.
I removed it with its marrow and then dressed it with medications, which healed quickly. This repetition of work and incision was necessary because of the weakness of the patient, his poor constitution, and my fear for his life, as he would frequently suffer a faint spell from excessive vomiting. He recovered completely.
Flesh grew solidly in place of the bone, and his condition improved. His strength returned, and he was able to move about without any harm. If a bone in any part of the body has been broken...
it should be spread in this manner: take a bandage and tie it to the end of the broken bone, ordering someone to pull it upwards. Then make another bandage of thicker wool than the first one.
Then bind it upon the flesh beneath the bone and extend its end so that the flesh is drawn downwards, while you expose the flesh from the location which you wish to spread lest the saw injure the flesh; and place a piece of wood or board firmly underneath the bone from below.
For if you do this, the saw will not be prevented from cutting the corrupted bone, and the sawing should be above the place of corruption a little so that there is no corruption in the cavity of the bone, and it does not appear outwardly to the sense, lest you are forced to saw it again.
But if the bone is corrupted and is not protruding but joined together, and the corruption is in its middle or some part of it, then expose the flesh from all its sides, then place the board underneath and saw it from the first side where the corruption is.
Then when you finish sawing it, saw it from the other side, and let the sawing be a little distance from the corruption as we said. But if the corruption is in a joint, cut the corrupted joint itself and saw the bone where it joins on the other side.
But if the corruption is in connection with two joints, there is no artifice except excision. But if the corruption is in a hand metacarpus or a foot metacarpus, the matter in treating it is very difficult, but you should pare away the corruption however it appears to you, and excise and purify it in any way that you are able.
And by any artifice that becomes possible for you, as long as a vein or nerve does not obstruct you, and know that saws and cutters for cutting these bones are many according to the position, setting, thickness, thinness, size, and smallness of the bones, as well as their solidity and sponginess. Therefore, you should prepare for each type of work a tool corresponding to that work.
And know that the operations themselves may guide you to the type of instrument that you need if you have long practice and knowledge in the arts of this craft, for he who is skilled in his craft and has witnessed various types of diseases. He may deduce for himself the instruments suitable for each disease and I have illustrated for you at the end of this chapter several instruments.

Chapter seventy-eighth.
On amputating limbs and sawing bones.
Limbs may become gangrenous either from an external cause or an internal one, and if you treat that gangrene with medication and the treatment is unsuccessful and you see the gangrene advancing in the limb unimpeded.
It is necessary to cut off that member where the corruption has reached so that the patient may be saved from death or from a great affliction which is greater than the loss of the member, and a sign of this happening to him is that the member becomes black until you think that fire has burned it.
Or it putrefies after the blackness until that putrefaction strives towards what is next to that member and takes hold of the whole body, so hasten to cut it off; likewise if the cause of the corruption is from the sting of certain creatures such as the sea scorpion or viper or centipede and the like.
If the corruption or sting is in the tip of a finger, cut off the finger and do not delay for the corruption to strive towards the hand; likewise if it has seized the hand, cut it off from the base of the wrist and do not delay for the corruption to strive until it seizes the forearm.
Likewise, if it seizes the forearm, cut off the arm at the elbow in the joint itself; if you see that the corruption has spread towards the shoulder, do not cut off the shoulder at all for in that there is the death of the patient and use other treatment according to one's ability.
And you do the same with the leg, if the corruption has seized a toe, cut off the toe at one of its joints; and if it has seized the instep, cut off the entire foot; and if it has ascended to the knee, cut off the thigh at the knee joint.
And if the corruption has reached above the knee there is no remedy except to leave it and abandon the patient to death; the method of cutting off or excising a member is to tie a ligature below the place you wish to cut and another ligature above the place.
One attendant extends one ligature downwards and another attendant extends the upper ligature upwards; then you yourself pare the flesh between the two ligatures with a wide spatula until all the flesh is exposed, then cut or excise it; and you should place pieces of linen from all directions.
So that the saw does not touch the correct place and the patient suffers extra pain and a hot swelling, if bleeding occurs during your work, quickly staunch the place or apply some astringents to it, then return to your treatment until you finish.
Then bind the injured limb with a bandage suitable for it and treat it until it heals, and I will tell you an example of a man who had this exact ailment in his leg. It happened that there was blackness in his leg along with a burning sensation like fire.
This corruption first occurred in his toe, until it seized the whole foot. The man cut off the foot from himself when he saw the corruption advancing in the limb with the intensity of pain and burning he was experiencing.
After a long time had passed, this same corruption appeared in the index finger of his hand. He came to me and I repelled that evil with the remedies I applied to his hand after cleansing his body. But the evil did not relent and continued to advance in the other finger.
The corruption seized his hand, and he urged me to amputate it, but I refused him hoping to repel that evil and fearing for him upon amputating his hand death because the man's strength was failing. When he despaired of me, he went back to his town.
News reached me that he amputated his entire hand and was cured. I only told this story to be helpful in cases of this type of disease, and to serve as a guide to act upon.
Chapter eighty-eight.
On the treatment of abscesses and how to inject them with medicine.
If a swelling occurs in some fleshy organs and lasts for a while until it gathers pus, then bursts or opens and all the pus inside it comes out, leaving the place empty like a vessel...
Skin that is like cloth, thin and not severely damaged so as to affect the bone, nerve, or ligament, deserves to be called a hidden place, not a protector, unless its duration lengthens until corruption affects one of these organs.
Then it is called a protector or a cicatrix, and its treatment is to cut all that skin as it runs, especially if it has become thin and like cloth and you are sure that it will not adhere to the place because of its corruption.
If you hope that the skin will adhere and its corruption has not reached that extent and there is thickness of flesh in the skin, then treat it with injection. That is, look at the hidden place; if it is large and the pus emanating from it has a foul smell, inject it with Egyptian medicine.
This is vinegar, oil, honey, and ginger in equal parts collected in a vessel and cooked over fire until the medicine thickens and comes to the consistency of honey. Then take what you need and thin it with water and honey, and inject it into the hidden place, tighten its opening, and leave the medicine inside for two hours.
Then press it out; do this for several days until the hidden place is cleansed and the stench goes away. It may also be injected with ash water if this medicine is not available to you. This is done by taking the ash of vine wood or oak wood and pouring water over it.
Then strain it and inject it until you are sure that the hidden place has been washed. If the patient cannot tolerate Egyptian medicine or ash water, inject him with water and honey mixed with a little powdered ginger.
Or inject him with honey and drink mixed together, for honey cleanses and washes, while the drink adheres to the hidden place, especially if the drink has astringent and drying properties. If you do this several times and are sure that the corruption of the hidden place has gone, then inject it with something that promotes flesh growth.
Such as taking some of the palm marrow and dissolving it in rose oil and astringent syrup, or injecting it with some of the other medicines and ointments whose properties we have established in the treatise on ointments, if the opening of the fistula is narrow so that the injector cannot enter.
Then widen it a little with an iron instrument, or insert a wick soaked in Egyptian ointment or the ointment called "sericon" until it widens. Likewise, if the opening is also wide, bring its edges together with stitches and leave an opening large enough for the injector to enter without difficulty.
And if the opening from which the pus flows is elevated, cut it at its lowest point so that the pus drains downwards, for if pus becomes trapped in the depths of the fistula, it will prevent the flesh from growing there.
If you cannot cut the fistula downwards as you wish, then position the limb so that the pus flows easily, according to what is possible for you; make sure nothing at all becomes trapped inside.
As for the poultices applied externally to the fistula and how they are applied, take one of the appropriate medicinal pastes and spread it on a linen cloth, making sure it is large enough to cover the entire fistula. Then, cut a hole in the cloth opposite the opening of the fistula, making it slightly wider than the fistula itself. Spread some soft ointment on another piece of cloth the size of the hole and place it over the first cloth, leaving a space between the ointment and the fistula's opening to allow pus to drain freely. Do not remove the large cloth until later.
For several days, and the one covering the opening of the fistula should be removed at all times so that any pus that has accumulated can drain out. This will allow you to determine whether the discharge is copious or scant, mature or immature.
Also, examine the site of the abscess itself to see if its owner feels pain there or not, and whether there is swelling or not. Then, if your treatment continues in this way and you see that the material coming out is slight at the mouth of the abscess.
Know that the abscess may have adhered or nearly adhered, so then dissolve it the next day or on the third day. And when you dissolve the wound, replace the cloth with another and renew the ointment. If thin pus comes out of the abscess after a long time, do not despair of its healing.
For its healing is near because some people have a slow growth of flesh in their wounds and others the opposite. If pus that is not ripe comes out of the abscess after many days, know that the abscess has not adhered. If the healing of the abscess is delayed.
And if its matter lasts, increase the drying medications, and let them be in their composition moist and in their strength dry, such as palm ointment if it has been increased with an excess of colophony. And the most effective treatment is to take palm ointment.
Dissolve it with rose oil and sprinkle aged, moderately thick syrup on it, then knead it in and use it. You may also do something like this if you do not have palm ointment available: honey, if you cook it until it becomes viscous, and use it. Or take myrrh, patience herb, and cinnamon, crush them all, and sprinkle them on the honey while it is over the fire. Then apply it to a cloth and tighten it over the abscess, or coat it with cooked honey until it thickens, sprinkle medications on top of it, and place the cloth over it and tighten it.
Long angelica, the root of asphodel, common asarabacca, and centaury may also be used, either singly or in combination. Crush them, sift them, and sprinkle them on the honey you have described, and use it. If the matter of the abscess lasts and does not heal with this treatment, know that there is corruption within it.
And if it affects the bones, or any of the solid nerves, or the ligaments, treat it as we have previously described for dislocations.
Chapter Ninety-Eight.
On the treatment of ingrown nails, diseased nails, amputation of supernumerary fingers, and separation of conjoined fingers.
An ingrown nail is a large amount of flesh that grows under the toenail of the big toe or foot, and perhaps in all the other fingers. If it is neglected for a long time, it swells and becomes a hot tumor.
Or it may become putrid and rotten until it eats away at the base of the nail, and perhaps destroys it altogether.  The decay may even reach the bone, making it smell foul, and the tip of the finger becomes swollen with a livid color. If you treat it as described in the division and your treatment is unsuccessful...
...then you should cut away all the excess flesh that grows from under the nail with an iron instrument, and then cauterize the wound afterwards; for cautery is very beneficial in this case. But if the bone is sound and the nail itself is also sound...
...and the angle containing the nail has pressed the flesh inward, causing it to fester and become inflamed, then you should place a fine splint under the angle of the ingrowing nail, lifting it up, and carefully cut away that flesh.
Then apply caustic medicines to the remaining flesh until it is all gone, and treat it with ointments until it heals. But if the decay has affected the bone, then you should cut out that bone and remove it, for it will not heal as long as there is a decayed bone present at all.
If you see gangrene and decay advancing in the finger, then amputate it at one of the joints, as previously mentioned, and treat it until it heals. If a nail receives a blow or bruise and there is severe pain, you should first lance the affected area.
Then the nail is split with a sharp, obliquely angled knife from above downwards, and care must be taken not to reach the flesh beneath the nail with the incision, for this would cause the patient severe pain and lead to the growth of excessive flesh in that place. The area is then treated with something to relieve the pain.
As for the extra finger that sometimes occurs on some people's hands, it may be all fleshy, or it may have bones in some parts, and it may even have a nail. The growth of some of them may occur at the base of the joint of some fingers.
Or its growth may be in some parts of the finger, and that which grows in some parts of the finger does not move, while that which grows at the joint of the finger may move.  Any fleshy part of it can be easily cut off by cutting it at its base with a wide knife.
But the treatment of that which grows at the base of the joint is difficult, so refrain from cutting it. As for that which grows on the finger in one of the phalanges, its flesh should first be cut in a circular manner down to the bone, then the bone should be spread with one of those saws suitable for that purpose.
Then treat it until it heals. As for the adhesion that sometimes occurs between the fingers, this often happens and may be congenital or occur after a wound or burn, or something similar. It should then be split.
So that the fingers return to their natural state, then place between them a wick or a rag soaked in rose oil so that they do not adhere quickly and separate them, or put a thin lead plate between them until the area heals properly.
Similarly, if adhesion occurs between some fingers and the palm, split that adhesion according to what is appropriate and restores the shape of the limb.
Chapter Ninety.
On the Sectioning of Arteries.
Arteries are twisted, thick veins filled with black humor which occur in most of the body's organs and are most common in the legs, especially in weavers, carriers, and porters. It is necessary that you first use a strong cupping to remove the black bile from the body several times.
Then, phlebotomize the patient with a lancet. As for treating it with iron, it is done in two ways: one is to cut and remove the black blood, and the other is to cauterize the vein and remove it entirely. As for cutting, proceed as follows: first, bathe the leg with hot water.
Until the thick, turbid blood dissolves, then bind the patient's leg from above his thigh to below his knee with a bandage. Then cut the vein in one place or two places or three wide incisions.
Then draw out the black blood with your hand from below the leg upwards and from above downwards until a sufficient quantity of blood has been released, or as much as the patient can tolerate. Then bind it up and order him to avoid foods that produce black bile. Repeat vomiting and phlebotomy.
When the veins are full and this harms the patient, but as for cauterizing it, proceed as follows: shave the patient's leg if he has a lot of hair. Then take him into the bath and bathe his leg in hot water until it reddens and sweats or have him engage in strenuous exercise.
If a bathhouse is not available to heat the limb, then cut the skin opposite the vein lengthwise, either at its end near the knee or below it near the heel. Then open the skin with forceps and excise the vein from all sides until it is exposed to touch.
When it appears, you see it reddish-purple; and when it is freed from the skin, you also see it as if it were a tendon. Then insert a ligature under it until, when it rises and comes out of the skin, hang it with a smooth blind ligature. Then make another incision near that incision, the length of three fingers.
Then strip the skin from the vein until it appears, then raise it with a ligature as you did and hang it with another ligature as you did first, then make another incision or many incisions if needed.
Then pull and cut it at the end of the incision at the heel, then draw and pull it until it comes out of the second incision; then draw it up to the incision above it, and do this until you draw it from the third incision, the highest of all incisions, until it all comes out and then cut it.
If pulling and drawing does not suffice, insert a strong doubled needle with thread and tie it, then draw it; insert a ligature under it and twist it with your hand in all directions until it comes out; be careful that it does not break, for if it breaks, drawing it will become very difficult for you.
A complication will enter the patient; and if you draw it all out, place wool soaked in syrup and rose oil or oil on the surgical sites and treat it until it heals. If the varicose vein is convoluted with twists to various sides.
And it was not on a straight line as we said, you should make incisions on it at each side of its convolutions and twists; then hang it with ligatures until you draw it all out. Be careful when making the incisions that you do not cut or injure the vein, for drawing will become difficult for you if you are careless.
Image of the incision with which the varicose vein is dissected.
Image of the blind snare.
It does not have a barb like other snares and is not sharp at the end so as not to injure the vessel. It is thick and curved, smooth because if it were thin, it would cut the vessel with its curvature. But it should have the advantage of thickness as we have said.
Chapter Ninety-One.
On the ligature of the median vein.
This vein arises in the legs in hot countries such as Hejaz and the lands of the Arabs, and in hot, barren countries with little vegetation, and it may arise in other parts of the body besides the legs. It is caused by putrefaction that occurs under the skin.
As it occurs within living bodies, worms, the pus of abscesses, and worms that develop between the skin and flesh, a sign of the beginning of this vein is that intense inflammation occurs in the leg, followed by the area suppurating, and then the vein begins to emerge from that suppurated spot.
As if it were the root of a plant or animal, and if its end appears, it should be wrapped around a small piece of lead weighing from one to two dirhams, tied securely, and left hanging from the leg.
Every time something emerges from it to the outside, wrap it in the lead and tie it. If it grows very long, cut off some of it and wrap the remainder; do not cut it from its origin before it has all emerged because if you cut it, it will contract and retract into the flesh, causing a tumor and putrefaction at the site and a foul ulcer.
Therefore, it should be drawn out and gently pulled until it all comes out and nothing remains in the body. It may emerge from this vein in some people to a length of five or ten cubits.
I have been told that it emerged from a man to a length of twenty cubits. If it breaks off while you are treating it, insert a probe into the hole and pack it tightly with a long bandage along the body so that everything inside it is emptied. Then try to cleanse the area with medicines for several days.
Then treat it with the treatment of tumors, and this vein may have many branches, especially if it appears in the joint of the leg or in the leg itself, so it will have many mouths and a branch will emerge from each mouth. Treat it as we mentioned in the division and what precedes.
The Second and Ninety-Second Chapter.
In the incision for the worm that is generated under the gluteal fold and is called the cow's disease.
This disease is called in some of our countries cow's disease because it often appears in cows and it is a small single worm that is generated between the skin and flesh and crawls throughout the body ascending and descending.
It becomes apparent to the senses through its crawling from one organ to another until it pierces wherever it has pierced in the skin, making a place and coming out, forming it from putrefaction of some humors, as it appears with worms and animals and pumpkin seeds in the abdomen.
And it is expected from its harm that if it crawls in the body and rises to the head and reaches the eye, it may open inside and come out, thus ruining the eye; this often occurs. If you want to treat it and remove it, it will only be when it is crawling and appearing to the senses.
You should tighten what is above and below it with a strong bandage, then make an incision on it and remove it; if it has sunk into the flesh and you cannot find it, apply heat to the spot with fire until it is burned. The greatest harm that is expected from it is ruining the eye as we have said.
If you see that it has become in the head near the eye, tighten under it on the forehead with a strong bandage, then make an incision on it and remove it. The patient should observe cleansing their body with laxatives for foul and unhealthy humors and abstain from foods that produce putrefaction.
Chapter Seventy-Third.
On incision for the ailment known as nafr.
This ailment which is called nafr in our country is a pain that occurs in some organs and then moves from one organ to another, and I have seen it as I will describe to you.
I was summoned to a sick woman in some distant lands, and when I uncovered her arm, I saw a slight swelling in the vein of the forearm. After an hour, I observed that swelling creeping along the vein like a worm ascending quickly to her elbow as fast as quicksilver.
If it moved from one place to another, the pain would cease from that location and settle in the elbow. Then after an hour, it flowed throughout her entire body until it reached the other arm. She then told me that it revolved around her whole body as she had witnessed.
I was astonished by the speed of its transfer from one organ to another, and I had never before seen this ailment with my own eyes in this manner; although I had heard of groups of people experiencing pain that moved from one organ to another, I had never witnessed it myself as I did with this woman.
I concluded that this must be because the woman was from the desert, with a dry physique and visible veins, which allowed this traveling sensation to be perceptible. It would not appear in this way for those who are well-nourished or have moist bodies with concealed veins.
Therefore, if you wish to treat it and the patient feels pain, and it appears to you as we have described, tighten a bandage above and below it with urgency and make an incision over it so that the trapped wind escapes and the area can heal.
However, if you do not see it with your own eyes, treat it by rubbing the body and using remedies that dispel winds and scatter them, such as black cumin seeds, fenugreek, and similar medications.
Chapter Seventy-Fourth.
On the Extraction of Arrows.
Arrows may differ according to their types and according to the places where they are located in the body. As for their difference according to their types, some are large and some are small, some are hollow glass and some are solid glass, and some have three corners and four corners.
Some have tongues and some have splinters. As for those that are according to the organs in which they are located, they are of two types: either the organs are major hollow organs such as the brain, heart, liver, lungs, kidneys, intestines, bladder, and the like. So, when an arrow falls into one of these organs and the signs of death that I will describe to you appear, then you should avoid removing that arrow from it, for death befalls its owner in most cases. And when these bad signs do not appear to you, and the arrow has not been concealed in the depths of the organ, take it out and treat the wound. And among the signs of the brain, if an arrow hits it and pierces the bone and wounds the dura mater that is on the brain, then a severe headache, pain and dizziness will result from that.
And when these bad signs do not appear to you, and the arrow has not been concealed in the depths of the organ, take it out and treat the wound. And among the signs of the brain, if an arrow hits it and pierces the bone and wounds the dura mater that is on the brain, then a severe headache, pain and dizziness will result from that. And redness in the eyes, redness of the tongue, convulsions, confusion of mind, vomiting sometimes, and perhaps blood comes out of the nostrils or ears, and perhaps speech is cut off and the voice goes away, and moisture like porridge comes out from the place of the wound and flows from it like meat water.
If these signs appear, refrain from treating the patient and removing the arrow if it has not come out. As for the signs of the arrow if it hits the heart and was close to the left breast, and he felt as if it had been embedded in something solid, not something empty.
And perhaps the arrow had a movement resembling the movement of the pulse, and black blood would flow from the wound, followed by coldness in the extremities, cold sweat, and fainting. Know that death is inevitable. And a sign of an arrow if it injures the lung is the outflow of frothy blood from the wound.
The vessels following the neck swell, and the color of the patient changes, and he breathes with a loud respiration and desires to inhale cold air. If the arrow has struck the diaphragm in the chest, it will be close to the small ribs and respiration will be great with severe pain.
He sighs and all the limbs of the shoulders are moved, and if the arrow has struck the liver, severe pain follows and blood resembling the liver in redness issues from the wound. If the arrow has struck the stomach, perhaps something undigested will issue from the wound with the food.
Its sign is apparent, and if the arrow has struck the abdomen and become fixed in it, and some excrement issues from the wound or pus or an intestine that has been perforated, there is no hope for his treatment nor for extracting the arrow. If the arrow has struck the bladder and urine emerges.
And something protrudes from it outwardly and the pain intensifies upon the patient, then know that he is doomed. But as for all other organs such as the face, eye, throat, shoulder, upper arm, vertebrae of the back, collarbone, thigh, leg, and similar organs, they have been spared in most cases.
Provided the arrow has not encountered an artery or nerve, and the arrow is not poisoned, I will tell you of some things that I have witnessed concerning these arrows so that you may deduce your treatment from them. A certain arrow had struck a man in the fleshy part of his eye at the root of the nose.
I extracted it for him from the other side beneath the cartilage of the ear, and he recovered without any harm occurring to his eye. I also extracted another arrow for a Jew which had struck him in the cartilage of his eye beneath the lower eyelid; the arrow had become obscured, and I could only grasp its small point which adhered to the shaft.
It was a large arrow, one of the hard composite arrows with a square iron head that was smooth and had no ears. The Jew recovered without any ill effect occurring to his eye. I also extracted another arrow from the throat of a Christian; the arrow was Arabic.
He had two ears which were split and a spear had gone deep into his throat, so I worked carefully until I removed it and the Christian recovered.  I also removed another spear from a man who had been pierced in the belly, and we thought he would die.
When thirty days or so had passed and his condition did not change, I split the arrow and worked on it until I removed it and he recovered without any ill effects. I saw a man who had been pierced by an arrow in his back, and the wound had closed around it.
Seven years later the arrow came out at the base of his thigh. I saw a woman who had been pierced by an arrow in her belly, and the wound had closed around it. The arrow remained, and her condition did not change; she felt no ill effects from it in any of her natural actions.
I saw another man who had been pierced by an arrow in his face, and the wound had closed around it. He experienced little pain. There are many cases like this. I removed an arrow from one of the sultan's officers; it was lodged in the middle of his nose, tilted slightly to the right.
The arrow was completely buried. I was called to treat him three days after the arrow wound occurred, and I found the wound very narrow. I widened it with a thin probe and felt nothing. He felt a pain and ache under his right ear from the right incision.
I thought that pain might be from the tip of the arrow, so I dressed the area with a poultice that had drawing and ripening properties hoping that the area would swell and the mark of the arrow would appear. I cut into him again, but nothing happened in that spot to indicate that the arrow had reached it.
I continued the poultice for many days, but nothing happened. The wound closed during that time and the patient remained troubled by its removal for a long period until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose and told me. I then applied a harsh, caustic medicine to the wound for many days.
Until it opened and I probed it, feeling the fine point of the arrow stuck in the wood. Then I further widened the wound with that sharp medicine until the point of the arrow appeared clearly to us and I went along with it for a period of about four months.
Then, when the wound widened and I was able to enter the forceps into it, I pulled and moved it, but it did not respond by coming out. So I continued to soften and maneuver it with various instruments until one day I grasped it firmly with all its limbs, as shown in the figure at the end of this chapter.
And physicians used to rule that the cartilage of his nose could not be joined, but I joined it and the wound healed and the patient recovered completely without anything hurting him at all. And I tell you how I remove some arrows so that this may serve as a measure and guide for what I have not mentioned.
For the details of these techniques cannot be grasped by description nor encompassed in a book, but rather the skillful craftsman measures the little against the much and what is present against what is absent, and infers a new operation and a new instrument when faced with strange emergencies.
If we descend from this craft, I say that arrows are only extracted from the organs in which they have lodged in two ways: either by pulling from the place where they entered or from the opposite side. Those that are removed from where they entered.
Either the arrow is prominent in a fleshy place, so it is pulled out. If it does not respond by coming out at the time it occurred, you should leave it for a few days until the flesh around it decays, making it easier to pull and remove. Likewise, if it is lodged in bone.
And if it does not respond by coming out, leave it for a few days as well and try pulling and moving it every day, for it will come out. If it still does not come out after several days, you should pierce around the arrow in the same bone from all sides with a fine awl until you widen the space for the arrow, then pull and remove it.
If the arrow is fixed in the bone of the head and has penetrated one of the ventricles of the brain, and some of those symptoms which I have mentioned to you appear from the wound, then desist from drawing out the arrow and leave it until his condition becomes clear after a few days.
Then proceed to draw out the arrow and extract it; but if it is fixed very firmly and does not yield to traction, use trephines around the arrow as I have described to you, then treat the site until it heals. But if the arrow has become concealed in some part of the body.
