And has disappeared from sensation, then search for it with a probe; if you feel it, draw it out with some instrument suitable for the purpose; but if you are unable to do so because of the narrowness of the wound and the depth of the arrow's penetration, and there is neither bone nor nerve nor vein in that place.
Then make an incision to widen the wound so that you can get hold of the arrow and draw it out; if it has two flanges by which to grasp it, then free the flesh adhering to them from every side by every means possible; proceed even if you are unable to free the flesh in breaking the flanges and splitting them until you succeed.
And if you attempt to extract the arrow from any location, use the twisting of your hand with the callipers in all directions until you free it, and be extremely careful lest the arrow breaks, for then it will be difficult for you to draw it out; if you are unable to do so at that time.
Leave it for days until the flesh around it putrefies, then return to it; for then it will be easier to extract. If hemorrhage occurs, use what we have mentioned concerning its treatment in its chapter. And be very careful not to cut an artery or nerve or tendon, and use artifice.
In any case, you can remove the arrow, and let it be done gently, carefully, and firmly as I have described to you. And it is fitting for you to use, when drawing out the arrow, that the patient should be in the same position he was in when the arrow struck him, which is best.
If you cannot do that, use whatever forms you can. As for the arrow which emerges from the opposite side, either something has protruded from it outwards, or you will find the point of the arrow by feeling near the skin, close to the surface and protruding.
So make an incision over it, and let the cut be as wide as the arrowheads will fit, then draw it out, for it will come out easily. If it is stuck in a bone, twist your hand in a rotating motion until the arrow acts upon the bone and widens its own space, then draw it out.
Otherwise, leave it for a few days and try again so that it may come out. If the arrow shaft is still in place, push it in. If the shaft has fallen out and you wish to use pressure, insert the hollow instrument into it so that its cavity enters the tail of the arrow, then push it in with this.
If the arrow is hollow, push it in with an instrument that fits into that cavity, for this will make the arrow come out easily. If the arrow is poisoned, you should cut out all the flesh where the poison has penetrated if possible, then treat it with what is suitable for that.
If the arrow is lodged in the chest, abdomen, bladder, or side and is near what can be felt with a probe, and you are able to make an incision over it, do so and take care not to cut any vein or nerve, then remove it.
Then suture the wound if it needs it and treat it until it heals.
Its ends are shaped like the beak of a bird, made as if it were a file; when it grasps an arrow or anything else, it does not let go. It can be made in large, small, and medium sizes, all according to the size of the arrow and the width of the wound.
Image of the hollow speculum.
Image of the pointed speculum.
This speculum has a pointed end like a stylet to make it easy to insert into a hollow arrow and push it through.
Chapter Ninety-Fifth.
On Phlebotomy.
There are thirty veins in the body which are customarily opened for phlebotomy, sixteen of them being in the head: the two temporal veins behind the ears known as the temporals, the two arteries in the temples that are visible, and the two veins in the hollows of the eyes known as the viewers.
And the vein that stands in the middle of the forehead, and the vein at the tip of the nose, and the two jugular veins in the neck, and the two veins in the upper lip of the mouth, and the two veins in the lower lip – these veins being known as the jaw veins – and the two veins under the tongue.
As for the veins that are opened for phlebotomy in the arm and hand, there are five: one of them is the cephalic vein, which is on the outer side and is commonly called the head vein; the basilic vein is the middle vein and is composed of a branch of the basilica and a branch of the cephalic vein and is commonly called the body vein.
The basilica, which is located on the inner side, is also called the axillary vein and is commonly called the belly vein; the brachial vein, which is located on the forearm, is the one that adheres to it and appears prominently above the thumb; and the median cubital vein.
It is the vein between the little toe and the next one, in the leg and foot there are three veins, one of which is under the popliteal fossa on the outer side, the second is the saphenous vein and it is located at the heel on the inner side and the femoral vein and it is located at the ankle on the outer side.
And in the other leg there are three veins like them, as for the two veins behind the ears, their incision is beneficial for chronic colds, migraines, scabies and poor chronic scalp sores, and how to cut them off as I describe.
This is done by shaving the head of the patient and rubbing the back of his head in the place of the veins with a rough rag very well, then strangling the patient's neck with a turban until the veins appear and their location behind the ears in the two lower places of the head, so search for them with your finger and where you feel their pulse under your finger, mark it there with ink.
Then take a skinny bistoury, which is known as the lancet, and insert it under the vein in the skin until the bistoury reaches the bone, then lift your hand with the vein and skin upwards and cut the vein with the skin in a severed piece, the length of the piece being the length of two closed fingers.
Or something like that, and you send from the blood the amount you want, then tighten them with ligatures and leave them until they heal. I have previously mentioned in the beginning of the book their cutting and cauterization. As for the two arteries in the temples, it is beneficial to cut them off for chronic migraines, severe headaches, persistent ophthalmia and acute copious discharge directed to the eyes.
And how to cut them off as I describe to you: tighten the patient's neck with a turban until the arteries appear clearly and their pulse is evident under your finger, then mark it with ink. Then lift the skin from above the artery upwards with your index finger.
The ligature is introduced from below and the artery is raised upwards and severed as was done with the other arteries. You let out blood according to your need, then loosen the ligature of the patient and place your finger on the artery for a while.
Then you place cotton and a bandage on it and tighten it firmly from above, leaving it until it heals. Their mention, cutting, and ligating were previously presented in the beginning of the book. As for bleeding the artery of the forehead, its benefit is after bleeding the temporal artery for chronic facial conditions such as scabies, ulcers, and roseola.
The way to bleed it is as I describe: You constrict the patient's neck with a turban until the artery appears, then you take the instrument called the axe, and this is its image.
You place the forked prong at the head of the axe on the artery itself and strike it from above with a comb or something similar, then leave the blood to flow according to the amount you desire. Then you loosen the patient's constriction and tighten it until it heals.
It may also be opened with a wide speculum, but it should not have a sharp tip like other speculums. Instead, it should have a slightly broad tip and you dissect it carefully because the bone is nearby and a thin speculum might break inside.
As for the two arteries in the temples, their benefit is for eye conditions such as scabies, redness, and ophthalmia, as well as facial diseases. As for how to bleed them, you constrict the patient's neck with a turban, then dissect them while standing above his head. The bleeding should be slightly slanted lengthwise with a small, slightly wide speculum.
Since there is no flesh in this location, a thin-tipped speculum might break. After you let out the desired amount of blood, place cotton on it and tighten it for one night, then loosen it. As for bleeding the nose artery, it is beneficial for acute fevers.
Severe headache and diseases of the face, such as erysipelas which occurs in the nose, especially if chronic, and how to treat it by having the patient stretch his neck then grasp his nose with your left hand and take a thin long forceps.
Insert it into the middle of the cartilage itself between the nasal septum, because the nerve does not appear there for feeling, then blood will protrude from it immediately. You should press your hand with the forceps a little and draw out as much blood as you need, then tie it up for a night, for it will heal quickly.
As for the tonsils, their excision is beneficial for shortness of breath and the onset of leprosy and black diseases that appear on the surface of the skin such as black vitiligo, scabies, and bad ulcers, and how to excise them is by having the patient tie under his neck with a string, and the operator standing on the head of the patient.
The patient sits on a chair then you incise the artery to its length, making a wide incision, a little then draw out from it a moderate amount of blood or according to what you see is needed, then do the same to the other artery then untie the string.
And you tie the arteries with a moderate tightness so that the patient does not choke and leave it until tomorrow, for the wound will heal. As for the arteries of the jaw, their excision after phlebotomy is beneficial for scabs in the mouth, corruption of the gums, bad ulcers, chapped lips and bad ulcers.
That occur in the nose and around it, and how to excise them is by having the patient sit in front of you and tie his neck with a turban then turn back his lips and look at the two arteries which you see one on the right of the lip and the other on its left and distinguish them by their redness.
You will also find thin black vessels around them, so cut them off with a complete incision; if it is difficult for you and you do not know which they are aim to cut the largest and most visible one; similarly do so to the two arteries in the lower lip.
As for the veins under the tongue, it is beneficial to cut them after cupping the temples for the phlegm which may be in the throat from diseases of the mouth and throat. The method of cutting them is that you seat the patient facing the sun, raise his tongue and look under the tongue on one side for a vein and on the other side for a vein, and their color tends towards blackness.
Cut them carefully and do not overcut, for beneath them are arteries; it may happen that there is bleeding from these arteries. As for the three veins which are cut at the elbow, they are the ones habitually cupped by all people, and cupping them is done in two ways.
Either by inserting a wide lancet or olive-shaped one to the vein, or by making an incision with a surgical scalpel (which is called a "nasl" and is pictured here).
It should be wide as you see, so that it is suitable for opening hollow veins which are full and prominent, apparent and thick, and contain thick blood, such as those. And this is a picture of the olive-shaped lancet:
This lancet is narrower and has a finer point, suitable for cupping thin veins which contain thin, yellowish blood. And this is an image of the "nasl" surgical scalpel:
This instrument is suitable for making incisions and comes in various shapes and thicknesses depending on the width and narrowness of the veins. It can also be used to determine other things, and it is well-known among craftsmen.
As for the basilic vein, one of these three veins, its benefit in ligating is that it draws blood from the ulcers which are under the throat and neck extending to the chest and abdomen. The ligator should be cautious when ligating it and be on his guard, for underneath it lies an artery; if he makes a mistake and inserts the scalpel too deeply, he will cut that artery.
This will cause bleeding, so it should not be ligated with a stitching scalpel but should be cut with a scalpel. If the basilic vein does not appear clearly, it should be avoided and another one sought, or some of its tributaries requested.
Or you may ligate the brachial artery in its place, for it is evident and should be cut with a scalpel as we have said. If you wish to ligate it specifically, before stretching the arm, you should feel the spot until you locate the pulse, then mark it with ink.
Then tie the arm and cut the vein obliquely with a scalpel, as we have said, being careful that the incision is made away from the artery's position. When you tighten the ligature and see swelling at the spot you marked with ink, know that this swelling is the artery, so avoid it.
If you see the blood spurt when ligating, as urine spurts from a boy, and it is thin and red, know that it is arterial blood. At this point hasten to place your finger on it for a long time, then remove your finger; if the bleeding stops, which it often does, tie the arm tightly.
Leave it and warn the patient not to neglect it, and let him be on guard and not move it for several days until it heals. If the bleeding does not stop and overcomes you and no medicine is available at that moment, amputate the artery if it appears to you; its ends will contract and the bleeding will stop.
Or take the shell of a pistachio nut, split it, and take one half and stretch it tightly over the place of the artery with a ligature and bandages until the next day. If the bleeding has stopped, otherwise treat it as previously mentioned with the application of styptic agents to stop hemorrhage, and its blood is not difficult to cut in most cases.
As for the place of the small wound and the ability of the ligature in the arm, teach him. As for the brachial artery, it is beneficial to stop it so that it draws blood from the upper head and lower body because it is composed of a branch of the basilica and a branch of the cephalic as we have said.
The one who dissects it should be on the neck from its division, for under it is a nerve. If he increases in the insertion of the scalpel and hits the nerve, numbness will occur in it which makes it difficult to see. It may not heal at all. This nerve often appears to the senses.
If it is hidden in some people and it is thin and does not appear, you should make your dissection of it a longitudinal incision with the scalpel and avoid the nerve as much as possible. If the artery is between two nerves, dissect the artery lengthwise. As for the cephalic artery, it is beneficial to stop it because it draws blood from the head.
It is useful for eye diseases and it is especially beneficial in this artery if you wish to dissect it; by inserting an olive scalpel or a wide rhinological scalpel because it is the safest of all arteries because there is no artery nor nerve underneath it.
However, you should avoid the head of the muscle only with the scalpel and seek the soft spot. It does not harm it if it is not hit in the first strike to repeat the dissection several times, except that it may swell in some people if it is not dissected in the first strike.
But that swelling does not harm it. As for the method of dissection and its consequences and what should be advanced in its repair, the first thing you should know is that dissection is either used to preserve health only.
As for its use in diseases and what is used to preserve health and maintain it and prevent the occurrence of diseases, cupping should be in one of the three veins in the elbow, namely the cephalic, basilic, and brachial veins, and cupping should be in the early spring when signs of fullness appear.
Cupping should be on Sunday or Tuesday after three hours have passed since sunrise. As for cupping that is used for diseases, it does not have a limited time but whenever necessity and urgency call for it, whether at night or day.
And in every hour and in every time, and children should not be cupped until they reach fourteen years of age, nor should the elderly who have exceeded sixty years of age be cupped. If anyone intends to cup for any reason.
It is necessary that his intestines be cleansed before cupping with a gentle enema if there is a lot of retained filth so that it does not attract to the veins during cupping, from the intestine surplus putrefaction that harms the main organs, and one who intends to cupping should not cup while intoxicated or drunk until that state has subsided.
And cupping should also be avoided after a bowel movement, vomiting, diarrhea, excessive intercourse, fatigue, exercise, staying up late, fasting and everything that weakens physical or mental strength. Then the blood is thinned before cupping if it is thick.
With foods, drinks and medicines if possible, then he enters the bath unless prevented by something or indulges in some exercise to thin the blood, and his cupping should be done in the afternoon as we said, and he intends to clear his chest that day of all bad psychological afflictions.
Such as worries, anger, and fear, and from all physical afflictions such as excessive fatigue and exertion and intercourse and the like. And his assembly prepares things that people have been accustomed to using from kinds of incense, fragrances, amusements and the like, each person according to what he can.
Then the fixer sits on a cushion that is higher than the cushion on which the patient sits, then he extends his arm and rubs it with his hand two or three times, then he tightens the bandage with the buckle and twists it two or three times, let the tightening be moderate.
Because when the tightening is not moderate, either by excessive tightening it prevents the flow of blood, or if it is relaxed, it also prevents the flow of blood, then after tightening, the patient should rub both his hands together with each other.
Until the veins swell and become apparent to the touch, then the fixer wipes the scalpel with a little of old olive oil in particular, then he places his left index finger on the same vein under the place he wants to dissect slightly so that the vein does not escape the blow. Because some veins are like a cord and they escape when phlebotomized, and some are full of air, so when the lancet is placed on it, it sinks under the lancet and deceives the phlebotomist, and he does not open the vein, and if he opens it, then it will only be a narrow opening.
Therefore, the phlebotomist must be firm and take his time in all these matters, then he lowers the lancet, if the vein opens at that time, otherwise he repeats it again under that place a little or above it quickly if the place does not swell.
If there is swelling or the patient is distressed, leave him for a day or two and do not tighten the bandage, as it may bring a hot boil, and he should not enter the bath, then repeat the phlebotomy if he wishes, if the lancet was inserted and the opening was small and the flow of blood was thin.
And if you fear that the amount of blood you want will not come out, reinsert the lancet into the same hole carefully while maintaining its straightness and widen the opening a little, do this quickly before the place swells, for in many people the place may swell with a small opening.
If you see that it is swollen, do not apply anything to it at all for it will not help you. Put some poppy oil on it, for it makes the flow of blood easier and it is better in this place than oil itself or any other unguent.
Use poppy oil in all your vein phlebotomies when the flow of blood is difficult. The elixir Parva and shekhanj also do this if one of them is put on the place, for blood will thin and dissolve if it is thick.
If a large swelling occurs at the site of phlebotomy, which often happens, especially to one who has not been phlebotomized before or whose vein was opened small, hasten and put a sponge soaked in warm salt water on the swelling and bind it for an hour, for it will dissolve.
You should do this after all the blood has come out of the vein itself or from another vein. If something black or green remains in the place after a few days, it does no harm; if you wish, apply some patience and myrrh dissolved on it.
Or some juice of the coloquintida or the like; a swelling and a protuberance often occur after phlebotomizing the basilica vein. Place your hand on it; if it feels soft when you press it, then that protuberance is bad, so beware of putting anything of what we mentioned on it.
For it may have bled arterial blood, but compress it with something astringent so that the place hardens and then treat it with all the other remedies until it heals. You should draw a quantity of blood from each person according to his strength and what color predominates in his blood.
For if the blood is black, let it flow out until it becomes red; and if you see that it is thick, let it flow out until it thins; and if it is sharp, let it flow out until its sharpness goes away. One who is full and strong and needs to have a large quantity of blood drawn at once
To widen the incision for bloodletting, and the lancet should be wide; but if he is weak, then the opposite of that. It should be brought out in several times, and the hole should be narrow. The best thing to use for bloodletting is a sharpened, polished scalpel, split not stitched.
This type of bloodletting is safe from hemorrhage and severing nerves, it is the best and safest compared to bloodletting by crosswise and lengthwise incisions. And whoever is used to fainting during bloodletting should be given before bloodletting something of bread soaked in pomegranate water or syrup.
If he is sharp and the blood comes out in three or four times, if he has a cold temperament, then let him take before bloodletting bread soaked in wine syrup or in sweet honey syrup with spices, or in good fragrant syrup.
If fainting occurs during bloodletting and its cause is the excessive blood loss, then he should be given meat broth and light fragrant syrup and use aromatics with aloes wood and rub his chest with it, and use all that we mentioned in the division in the chapter on fainting.
Whoever wants to relieve their arm and loosen their blood again, it is necessary for someone whose bloodletting was for much vomiting and their strength is weak, to let out a little blood, a little at a time, according to the strength on consecutive days.
But whoever wants to relieve their arm and loosen their blood again, and their body is strong, they should do that after seven or nine hours from their first bloodletting. But whoever wants to draw blood from their body to the opposite side it inclined to, it is necessary to perform bloodletting on the second or third day.
But whoever has a lot of blood in their body, which has become hot and intense and caused fever, it is necessary to draw out all the blood at once and take out a large amount and widen the incision until fainting occurs after you have agreed on all the conditions of bloodletting. And place your hand on his pulse when the blood flows so that death does not occur at the membrane, for this often happens if the ignorant person is careless and negligence occurs, and it should not be done if you wish to release the arm and let blood flow again.
And if the mouth of the vein has closed and blood is difficult to remove, do not pinch it hard or twist it forcefully, for that is very bad. Rather, either leave it until it softens again, or move the point of the scalpel to loosen the coagulated blood in the mouth of the vein.
Or apply some salt dissolved in water, or apply some of the Faruq antidote or the shakhnanza, and pinch it lightly until the blood comes out. If the vein has become swollen, leave it alone.
Do not touch it until the swelling subsides. If necessity requires you to let blood again, then either soften it above that spot or soften it in the other arm or in another vein.
As for bleeding the arm vein, it should be bled instead of the cephalic and basilic veins if they are not present or hidden because it is composed of them. The way to bleed it is to have the patient put his hand in hot water until the arm turns red and the vein appears clearly, then tie a bandage above it with moderate tightness.
Then bleed the vein slightly obliquely, neither wide nor long, and let the opening be wide. Make the incision above the wrist joint by a little. If the blood does not flow easily, return the hand to the bowl of hot water and let the blood flow into the water.
Until you have obtained the amount needed. If it is summertime, you may dispense with returning the hand to the hot water. The blood flows more easily into the hot water during wintertime. Bleeding this vein is safer than bleeding all other veins. Because there is no striking vein or nerve beneath it, as for cupping the left hand, it is beneficial for liver ailments, and the manner of performing it is to tighten the wrist of the hand with a bandage or your hand after immersing it in hot water until the veins swell.
Because there is no striking vein or nerve beneath it, as for cupping the left hand, it is beneficial for liver ailments, and the manner of performing it is to tighten the wrist of the hand with a bandage or your hand after immersing it in hot water until the veins swell. It becomes quite evident to the touch, then cup it slightly, and even if you cut it off entirely, it will not harm it in any way. Do not press deeply with the knife, for underneath is the nerve of the fingers, and the place is bare of flesh, then return the hand to the hot water.
Leave it so that the blood flows, for if you do not return it to hot water, the blood will congeal in the mouth of the vein and cease to flow. And when you have drawn out as much blood as needed, apply ointment and salt to the vein so that it does not heal quickly.
And you should do the same for every narrow branch. As for the benefit of cupping the left hand, it is beneficial for ailments of the spleen, and you perform it in the same manner as you did with the second, as for cupping the saphenous vein, its benefit is for diseases that are in the lower part of the body such as ailments of the uterus.
And retention of menstruation, kidney diseases, chronic ulcers of the thighs and legs, and suchlike diseases. As for the manner of performing it, immerse his leg in hot water and massage it until the veins swell then tighten above the ankle joint with a bandage and the vein is located near the heel, visible towards the big toe.
And from it branch out many smaller veins in the leg, so cup the widest one or at the heel where they converge, for this is best and safest. But if you cup on the front of the leg, be careful of the nerves beneath it.
Cup it slightly as if you were about to cut it off, and let the knife be sharp. And if the blood does not flow easily, let him immerse his leg in hot water again and leave the blood to flow until it is emptied. If the one who cups misses the vein on the first try...
Let him make the incision a little higher up, for the spot is safe and there is no fear of danger if you are careful of the nerve as we have said. So do you with the saphenous vein in the other leg. As for the femoral vein, its location is as we have said, at the back of the thigh on the inner side.
And the benefit of phlebotomy for hip pain if it is from hot blood and how to perform it: You should have the patient enter a bath, hasten, and tightly bind his leg from the hip to just above the heel with four fingers using a thin, long bandage; for it will not appear otherwise.
And if it appears, phlebotomize it in any position that suits you, either by torsion which is best, or by cutting it off with a knife or splitting it open; for its location is safe and it is very subtle in most people, so if you do not find it.
And if it does not appear at all to the sense, then phlebotomize some of its branches which appear on the back of the foot toward the little toe and the next one, being careful of the nerves and sending out as much blood as you wish.
Then release the binding and place cotton on the phlebotomy site and bind the area; for it will quickly heal.
Chapter Sixty-Six.
On Cupping and How to Use It.
Cupping instruments may be of horn, wood, copper, or glass. Cupping is performed in two ways: cupping with incision and bloodletting, and cupping without incision.
This type of cupping without incision is also performed in two ways.
And the temples, which are the two sides of the neck on both sides, and the jugular notch, which is below the lower jaw from the mouth, and the shoulder depressions, and the buttock depressions on the coccyx, and the elbow depressions, which are in the middle of the arms, and the leg depressions, and the wrist depressions.
Cupping draws blood from the thin, delicate veins scattered in the flesh. Therefore, strength is not lost as with venesection. Cupping should not be used with fire or without fire for any disease caused by fullness until the whole body is emptied.
If cupping is necessary due to illness or habit, we use it at any time: at the beginning of the month, at the end, and in the middle, and at any other time.
This is because some people, when they have an excess of blood that needs to be removed by cupping, experience heaviness and headache. Others experience fullness and redness in their face, head, and neck. Still others experience itching on their face and sides, darkness, and swelling around their eyes. And some itch where the cups were placed.
Some laugh excessively, some taste blood in their mouth and their gums bleed, and some spit up blood. Others sleep more, and some see blood, redness, fighting, and wounds in their dreams, and similar things. Whenever we see something like this...
especially if it occurs in the middle third of the month, we order cupping after two or three hours have passed. As for the benefit of puncturing cupping, it helps with heaviness in the head and watery discharge into the eyes.
However, this should only be done after the whole body has been emptied. This type of cupping can be a substitute for cephalic phlebotomy. It is important to avoid using it on someone with a cold brain or a cold, as it will greatly harm them.
Therefore, it should not be used by the elderly and those with cold diseases, and those who habitually use it will suffer forgetfulness.
Therefore, you should order the barber to lower his hand with the cupping instrument slightly downwards so as to avoid causing forgetfulness. As for cupping on the shoulder, it is a substitute for bloodletting from the temple and bloodletting from the basilic vein.
Therefore, it is beneficial for asthma, shortness of breath, headache, cough and fullness. Cupping on the shoulder should be raised slightly because if it is done lower, it will cause weakness in the heart and stomach.
As for cupping on the temples, it is beneficial for headaches, ophthalmia, migraine, spasms and toothache. It is a substitute for bloodletting from the basilic vein. You should order the barber not to press deeply with the cupping instrument so as not to sever an artery and cause bleeding.
As for cupping under the chin, it is beneficial for sores in the mouth and gum disease, and other diseases of the mouth. It may replace bloodletting from the temporal artery which is located in the lips. As for cupping on the shoulders, it is beneficial for palpitations caused by fullness and heat.
As for cupping on the abdomen, it is beneficial for what bloodletting from the three veins, basilic, temporal and cephalic, achieves because it draws blood from all those fine veins in the flesh and draws those fine veins from thicker ones so that the attraction reaches the three thickest veins.
You should order the barber not to press deeply with the cupping instrument because this area is bare of flesh and underneath it are nerves and arteries. As for a single cupping instrument applied on the coccyx, it is beneficial for hemorrhoids and sores in the lower region.
The barber should be ordered to have a large speculum, and it should be made of brass because the place requires strong pressure, and perhaps a glass speculum might break, and many conditions are stipulated for it. As for the leg specula, their fullness is less than what is evident.
Because it draws blood from all over the body and is beneficial for chronic pains in the kidneys, womb, and bladder, it brings on menstruation and is beneficial for pimples and boils, and acts as cupping for the veins and arteries, except that it weakens the body greatly and fainting occurs in most people. The benefit of cupping the arteries is close to the benefit of cupping the legs.
How to apply the cupping glasses: first place the empty glass and suck moderately, do not leave the glasses on for long but rather place them quickly and remove them quickly so that the humors are drawn evenly to the spot, repeat this process continuously until you see the spot has reddened and opened.
When redness appears, then tighten and gradually resume sucking, then look at the condition of the bodies, for whoever has soft flesh with lax pores, it should only be tightened once so that the place does not become ulcerated.
Order the barber to widen the suction and deepen it a little, and adjust the suction gently and with delicate movement. If the blood is thick, he should tighten it twice: the first time to open a path for the thin blood and its water, and the second time to thoroughly extract the thick blood.
If the blood is very cloudy, repeat the suction a third time to achieve the goal. In general, if we want to extract a little blood, one tightening is sufficient, but if we want to extract a lot of blood, we tighten more times. And if we think that the blood is thick,
It is necessary to make a deep incision, and the proper depth of the incision is the skin only, what should not be used when applying cupping glasses and water, and what the phlebotomist warns against. As for those whose skin is thick, hard, rough and has narrow pores.
It is necessary to smear the places of cupping with opening, softening and dissolving ointments. As for in summer time it is like ointment of Khara (a type of gum) or violet ointment or sweet almond ointment or pumpkin seed ointment.
As for in winter, it is like narcissus ointment or lily ointment or chamomile ointment or hyacinth and the like. If the humor is thick and cold, let the ointment be that of marjoram or rue or bean oil or caraway and the like.
If the phlebotomist has wide pores and soft flesh, it is necessary to abstain from ointment, and these should wash their cupping glasses after cupping with rose water or cold water or fox grape water or pumpkin water or melilot water and the like. As for those whose blood has a lot of moisture...
...they should wash their cupping glasses with vinegar or the water of tamarind and sumac and the like. As for those whose humor is thick, they should wash their cupping glasses with old wine or marjoram water or caraway decoction or chamomile and the like. Cupping should be avoided in the bath and after the bath.
Rather, it should be used an hour or two after leaving the bath, and no one should sleep after cupping. What the phlebotomist and the one who is cupped should do before and after cupping.
First, it is necessary to see if the phlebotomist or the one who is cupped is bilious and his blood is mostly sharp and inflamed. He should take cold foods like pomegranates, dandelion with vinegar, lettuce, syrup of vinegar and marshmallow, etc. And let his food be poultry, lamb meat, sparrow hawks, and the like.
And whoever has a cold temperament should be given honey drinks, syrup of marshmallow or vinegar syrup with benzoin, and take moderately aged aromatic wine which is between old and new wine. He should be ordered to eat little and his food should consist of sparrows, pigeons, thrushes, and young white doves. Drinks on the day of cupping and bloodletting should be more than food.
And it may be necessary to give some people, at times, the faruq antidote or musk medicine or shalti before cupping and bloodletting or after them to strengthen the principal organs and thin the blood.  It should not be given to those who are emaciated.
As for cuppings without a condition, they are the ones that are placed on the liver, spleen, breasts, abdomen, scrotum and the location of the kidneys and right hip because these organs cannot tolerate a condition on them, but rather it is desired to draw blood from one organ to another.
Such as placing the cupping on the breasts for the ailment of shivering or using it to draw away cold wind that may have lodged in the organ, such as placing the cupping on the abdomen and scrotum, because it loosens the organ and heats it and takes away pain by dissolving that wind.
It may also be placed on the kidneys if there is a blockage or stone in them, for by its strong suction it may open the blockage or remove the stone from its place. It also does this when placed on the liver and spleen when there is wind that disturbs them. These cuppings may be used empty with suction only.
It may also be used with fire and may also be used filled with lukewarm water in cases of sciatica, filling the cupping, which should be large, with hot water alone or with water that has cooked some herbs suitable for this purpose.
Then it is placed full on the area, held and removed, and repeated as needed. This is an image of the cupping used with fire:
Its mouth shall be the width of two fingers open, as we have depicted it, and its depth shall be half a palm. There shall be on its side, near its middle, a small hole the size into which a needle can enter, made of Chinese copper or yellow copper with a thick rim.
It is a smooth, flat, rolled surface so as not to injure the member when placed upon it. In its center there shall be a transverse groove made of copper or iron where the candle with fire shall be placed. This speculum may be made larger or smaller than what we have described according to the diseases.
And as for the way this speculum is used, it is not the same for the genitals of boys and thin men as it is for those of adult males. As for how to place this speculum with fire upon the member, you should take a tightly wound wick with fire made of linen or a small candle made of pitch and place it on the center of the hardened rod that is in the middle of the speculum.
So that the fire ascends upward toward the bottom of the speculum so that the patient's body is not burned, then it is placed on the member and the finger is held on the hole we mentioned until, when we grasp the speculum as needed, the finger is removed.
And the steam escapes through that hole and the speculum loosens at its place, then you remove the wick as described and replace it if needed. As for the speculum used in the disease of fistula with water, there is no hardened rod or hole in it.
And it is used only by filling it with water and placing it on the member. This is its illustration:
And this speculum, the larger it is so as to hold a lot of water, the better it is.
Chapter Seventy-Nine.
On the Suspension of the Leeches.
Leeches are used in most cases for members where specula cannot be placed, either because they are small, such as the lip and gums, or because the member is bare of flesh, such as the finger and nose.
How to use it is to aim for the leeches that are in fresh, pure water from streams, then leave them for a day and a night in fresh water until they are hungry and nothing remains in their abdomen. Then the body is evacuated first by phlebotomy or cupping, then the affected limb is wiped.
Until it reddens, then place the leech on it. When it is full, it will fall off. If possible, suck the area with a syringe, for this is more beneficial. Otherwise, wash the area with vinegar then with plenty of water, and massage and squeeze it. If bleeding persists after the leech falls off, and this is a hemorrhage, soak a linen cloth in cold water.
Place it over the area until the hemorrhage stops. If the blood is excessive, sprinkle powdered alum or turmeric or something similar from astringents on it until the bleeding stops, or apply half a peeled fig to the area.
Leave it until the fig adheres to the area, for the blood will stop. If it is necessary to reapply the leech, do not use the same leech if possible. If the leech refuses to attach, wipe the area with fresh blood.
Or insert a needle into the area until some blood comes out, then place the leech. It will stick to the spot if it senses blood. If you want it to fall off, sprinkle something on it, such as patience or salt or ash, and it will detach.
Chapter Three.
On Surgery.
This chapter is also about what is needed in the craft of medicine, which is setting broken bones and dislocations.
Know, my sons, that this chapter may be claimed by ignorant physicians and laymen who have never browsed through the books of our predecessors or read a single letter from them.
For this reason, this art has become extinct in our country, and I have never encountered anyone who was skilled in it at all. And I only gained what I gained from it through my long reading of the books of the ancients and my eagerness to understand them until I extracted the knowledge of that from them, then I adhered to experimentation and training throughout my life.
And I have drawn for you in this chapter everything that my knowledge has encompassed. And I have put my experience into practice after bringing it closer to you and freeing it from the branches of prolixity, abbreviating it to the utmost extent, explaining it to the greatest clarity, and illustrating for you many pictures of the instruments used in it.
For it is an increase in explanation as I did in the two preceding chapters, and there is no power except with God, the Most High, the Great.
The First Chapter.
In it are general and comprehensive matters concerning the breaking of bones which must be presented before we begin to mention the broken and dislocated members one by one, it is necessary that we mention at the beginning of this chapter some general sayings and chapters. You are compelled to understand them first and grasp their truth, you and anyone else who is eager to learn this honorable craft besides you.
So I say that whenever one is affected by a fracture or dislocation or sprain or fall, it is necessary to hasten first to reduce it or loosen it or both of them. Unless something prevents it, such as weakness of strength, or the one affected by that is a child or an old senile man, or the time is extremely hot or extremely cold.
Then he should limit his diet to cold legumes and poultry meat and avoid drinks and thick meats and overeating any food that fills the veins with blood until the swelling is safeguarded and you do not expect a discharge of material into the affected area.
Then let the patient return to his former regimen by which he was accustomed. And if the broken bone is being set, the patient should be nourished with very nourishing, thick, and firm foods, such as barley porridge, rice pudding, lentils, chickpeas, and beef tripe. And eggs, tender fish, thick drinks, and the like. For by this regimen the fracture will unite more quickly and better. Know that broken bones in robust men and the elderly cannot rejoin and fuse to their original nature.
Because of the dryness and stiffness of their bones, they may sometimes join and fuse, but only if the bones were extremely soft, like those of young boys. However, nature produces something resembling glue on all sides of the broken bone. This is thick and adheres to it, drawing it together until the pieces are held firmly in place.
It binds them together until they reach the utmost strength and firmness as they were originally, so that nothing hinders his actions.
That is why it is necessary for the patient's food to consist of those things that have firmness, viscosity, and thickness, as we have said. Know that fractures may differ in type according to the difference in the organs, for a fracture of the leg bone is unlike a fracture of the skull bone, and a fracture of the rib bone is unlike a fracture of the spine.
And so are all the other organs, each different from the others. We will go on to describe each type of fracture in detail in its own chapter.  It is also possible for the type of bone fracture itself to vary.
For it may be a clean break without any splintering, or it may be a lengthwise fracture with splinters and both detached and attached bony fragments. The fracture may also occur with a wound and rupture of the skin, or it may be a slight crack.
For each type there is a particular trick in setting it, as will be mentioned in detail in its place. One of the signs by which a fracture is recognized is bending, protrusion, and tenderness to touch, and crepitus when pressed with the hand.
So when there is no apparent bending in the place, nor crepitus, nor tenderness on feeling the bone, nor disturbance by the patient and he experiences little pain, then there is no fracture. It may be a sprain or a slight crack or a minor fissure. Therefore, it should not be moved at all by traction or pressure.
Rather, apply to it the medications that will be mentioned later as appropriate for the location, and then bind it with gentle compression. Know that when a bone is broken and divided into two without splintering, each part has separated from its counterpart.
You should hasten to straighten and set it as soon as possible before a hot swelling occurs. If a hot swelling does occur, leave it for several days until the swelling subsides, then set it in any way you can with gentleness and skill.
Know that setting and straightening it is easier than a bone in which splintering has occurred. It is then compressed as will be mentioned later. But if there are bone fragments, the fractured limb must be extended on both sides, whether it is an arm or a leg.
Either with your hands if the limb is small, or with cords, or with a cord and your hand. The limb should be placed in its natural position on a level surface so that when the broken bone ends are extended, you can then push those protrusions back into place with all the skill and gentleness you can muster.
Do your utmost to avoid causing pain or discomfort to the patient. Strive to join one bone to the other in the best possible position. At that time, you should touch and feel them with your hand. If you notice anything amiss, correct it and set it as best you can.
Beware of excessive tension and forceful probing, as many ignorant people do, for they often cause harm with their actions, introducing a hot cautery or probe into the member, as I have witnessed them doing repeatedly.
Then, after proper alignment and tightening, ensure that the limb remains still and at ease. Warn the patient not to move it while awake or asleep, when turning, restless, or during bowel movements, and all other movements as much as possible.  Have them strive for a position of the limb that avoids pain.
Should the patient feel pain or discomfort in a particular position, they should move to another where there is no pain and still ensure that position remains level and straight.
So that the limb does not become crooked if forced, as for how to splint a broken limb, I will describe it to you. Know that broken limbs vary in size and shape, so what is small like an arm, finger, forearm, etc.
Should be wrapped with soft, thin strips of cloth. But what is large like the thigh, back, or chest, should be wrapped with wide, sturdy bandages, for a broad bandage is necessary to secure and bind a large limb evenly on all sides without any irregularity.
Then, after you have finished setting it, apply the appropriate plaster. Place a soft pad over the fracture site, then begin wrapping the bandage directly over the fracture itself, three or four times depending on the limb, tightening your hand slightly with each wrap and then leading it upwards from the fracture site.
Tighten it less than the bandage over the fracture, then space the wrap slightly away from the fracture site and loosen the tension gradually until you achieve a secure position. Then take another bandage and wrap that as well over the fractured area.
Then take the bandage and apply it to the lower side of the fracture, doing so with the same tension and looseness as you did with the first bandage above. Then place between the layers soft tow or linen cloth to equalize the deformity of the fracture.
If there is a deformity, do not put anything in it; otherwise, apply another bandage over it, and then fix splints firmly on these bandages, unless there is swelling or hot inflammation in the limb.
But if there is swelling or hot inflammation, apply something to soothe the swelling and reduce it. Leave it for several days; then fix the splints on it. The splints should be made of half-canes that are wide, carved, and skillfully prepared, or they may be made of ravenwood.
These splints can be made from pine, palm leaves, reeds, or any other suitable material. The width of each splint should be as follows and in this exact shape.
Except that the splint placed directly on the fracture should be slightly thicker and wider than the other splints. The length of the splint should be made according to the size of the broken limb. Figure of the Splint:
Then tie another bandage over the splints with the same tightness as the first bandage, and then tie it firmly from above as we have mentioned about tightening, which should be tighter at the site of the fracture. The further away from the fracture, the looser the tightness.
The threads should be of medium thickness, not too thick or too thin, and preferably made from damp linen, because if the threads are too thick, as I have seen foolish people do by making their threads from twisted linen strips, it is a grave mistake.
This is because the tension with thick threads will be excessive, and thin threads are also unsuitable because they will not provide the desired tension. There should be no less than the width of a finger between each splint.
If the patient is hurt by the ends of the splints after they are tightened in the correct places, put something soft like tow or wadding underneath them so that he will not be hurt by them. As for a fracture with a wound and a rupture in the skin, we will deal with that separately.
Know that not every broken limb should be splinted from the first day, for if the limb is large, splints should not be applied to it until five or seven days or more have passed, depending on your confidence in preventing a hot swelling.
This is the description of the bandages that were used by those who came before us, which are placed on fractures, dislocations, and sprains. It is a general bandage that is short, binds fractures, and suits most temperaments, especially boys and women, because it is not excessively hot or cold.
Take some bran from a mill, which is the residue of flour that clings to the walls of the mill during grinding. Knead it as is, without sifting it, with egg white, making the dough neither too thick nor too thin, and then use it.
Here is the description of another bandage that binds fractures, dislocations, and sprains. Take ten drachmas of each of: mastic, frankincense, acacia, rue, myrrh, and sugar; five drachmas of each of: saffron and patience; twenty drachmas of aloe wood; and twenty drachmas of Armenian or Roman clay. Pound all of these ingredients together and mix them with aloe wood water or egg white if the patient's temperament is hot, then use this bandage. It is excellent for binding bones together quickly and suits most people due to its moderation.
Here is the description of another bandage that is also good for fractures and sprains. Take ten drachmas each of myrrh, mastic, and white frankincense; five drachmas each of saffron and patience; six drachmas of acacia; and twenty drachmas of Armenian clay. Pound all of these ingredients finely, sift them, and knead them with water or egg white, then use the bandage.
Description of a poultice for joints and bones that have become dislocated from their places, and to relieve the pain accompanying them, as well as bone ache and fracture; take purified wool, dip it in vinegar and cooked oil, and apply it to the affected area. This poultice does not possess the power to heal.
But it is excellent in relieving hot swelling and expelling pains, especially. Description of another poultice that heals a broken bone; take the leaf of the common fig and the leaf of wild poppy and pound them all together and apply them damp. Description of a shorter poultice used when a bone fracture is set.
And you want to analyze the rest of the swelling, take from the root of marshmallow and chamomile and nard and caraway flour from each one part, pound all together and knead it with plaster if the member is not inflamed; but if it is inflamed, knead it with coriander water or with water and use it.
Description of another poultice stronger in resolution than the first, used when a hard swelling occurs after setting a bone; take from the root of marshmallow and flaxseed and fenugreek and rosemary and marjoram and nard and chamomile from each one part.
Pound all together and knead it with watercress water or fresh water or plaster, all that according to the heat of the member and its calmness; as for Hippocrates, he did not mention in his book to put on the broken member when setting it.
Except for cerate made from wax and oil only, and described to be medium between thickness and thinness; as for Galen, he saw that on the broken member when setting it, things which have hollows with some heat should be placed, such as myrrh and frankincense and mastic, as we mentioned.
Description of a poultice beneficial for weakness and pain; take tragacanth and sesame seed and human hair from someone who has recovered or bird feathers and marshmallow and salt all equal parts, pound and strain and knead and apply as a poultice. And as for the amount that should remain of the bandage.
Then it is dissolved, that is to say, you should look and see if the patient does not experience pain or rubbing or movement of the broken bone from its place; then do not dissolve it for many days. If he experiences severe rubbing or disturbing pain or swelling in the place.
Then hasten to dissolve it at once and do not delay that at all, and remove the dressing from it. Then take a soft cloth or a sea sponge moistened with lukewarm water and wash the place with it until the rubbing subsides and the pain is relieved; then leave the limb to rest for an hour.
Then apply to it wool dipped in vinegar and oil or rose ointment and tie it on for a night until you are safe from the hot swelling and the swelling of the limb subsides and its pain goes away; then return it to gentle tension and a light dressing, and do not tighten it as you did the first time.
And be kind to him until he recovers. If you see that the swelling, redness, pain, and swelling, and all the symptoms have disappeared altogether and needed a dressing and tension, return it as you did at first, whether or not anything of what we mentioned happened in the limb.
Then do not dissolve it except after three days or four or five or seven, and it may be left for twenty days, all of that depending on what appears to you from the condition of the limb as we said until, if necessary, the bone is broken and the flesh is close to congealing on it.
Then also increase the tension at that time more than all your first tension and also increase the thickening of the patient's food as mentioned above. If you see that the place of the fracture has dried up and become weaker than it should be, know that food is prevented from reaching it.
So wash it with lukewarm water every time you dissolve it, and let that be every three days, and lighten the tension a little, for by this deed food is carried to the limb and it heals quickly. As for what ignorant bone-setters do, breaking the limb again.
If it is not first set correctly and then straightened, it is a great error on their part and a serious deception. If it were correct, those who came before would have mentioned it in their books and acted accordingly. I have never seen any trace of this from anyone among them. And the right course is not to do it.
Chapter Two.
On Accidental Fracture of the Head.
There are many types of accidental fracture in the head, with different shapes and diverse causes. Some fractures may result from a sword blow, completely splitting the bone until it reaches the dura mater beneath. Just as a chisel cleaves wood.
This type of fracture is therefore called chisel-like. Or, a sword may cleave only part of the bone, splitting its surface but not penetrating to the end; this type is called a complete avulsion. The wound from these fractures may be either large or small.
Other fractures may be crush injuries or contusions, caused by a blow from a stone or a fall onto one. This type of fracture may penetrate almost to the membrane beneath the bone, or it may be located on the surface of the bone.
The wounds from these fractures are also either large or small.  There are also subtle fractures within the bone, at the hairline, which manifest as slight headaches.
This type is therefore called a hair-line fracture. There are also fractures caused by a fall or blow from a stone, which drive a portion of the bone inward, creating a depression in the area, similar to what happens to copper when struck, causing part of it to indent.
This is most common in moist skulls, such as those of children. All these types of fractures may have both loose and fixed fragments, and their treatment will be discussed in detail later. We will identify all these types of fractures through examination.
And examine it with probes, and extract the corrupted flesh from upon it. As for the hairy type, it is known by exposing the bone and rubbing and smearing it with ink; for the fracture will appear black. As for the treatment of the fracture, first look at the patient's symptoms.
If you see from his symptoms a clear indication of fear, such as vomiting bile, extension and loss of mind, cessation of voice, fainting, a sharp fever,  the pupils of the eyes being dilated and red, and the like of these symptoms, do not approach the patient or treat him; for death is sure to occur with these symptoms in most cases.
And if you see symptoms that do not frighten you and hope for his safety, then proceed with his treatment. For if the wounded man comes to you immediately after he has been injured, and that was during the winter days.
You should strive to remove the bone before the fourteenth day in any case, and if it is during the summer days, you should hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, lest what is under the bone of membrane spoils, and those symptoms we have described occur.
But if the fracture of the bone has reached the membrane covering the brain and there was crushing and bruising, then you should cut off the crushed bruised part as I will describe to you, which is to shave the head of the wounded patient.
And expose the bone in any way you are able, and according to the shape of the wound and what relieves the patient, if upon exposing the bone blood flows or a hot swelling appears to you, then address that as is appropriate.
That is to stuff the place with cloths soaked in rose water and ointment, clean the wound until the swelling subsides and the bleeding stops, then proceed to grind the bone and remove it, and this can be done in one of two ways.
As for the first way, it is to cut the bone with a thin, narrow blade and this is its image:
Then you use another section after this section, which is slightly wider than it, and this is its picture:
Then you also use another section which is also wider than the second, and it must be the case that you have several different sections, some of which are wider than others and some of which are shorter than others, and they should be extremely sharp at their ends, and let them be of Indian iron or good steel.
And use gentleness in striking the section so as not to shake the head and hurt it, for if the bone is strong and hard, then it should be pierced around it before you use the sections with awls that they called ungraving awls and not deep-boring awls.
Because it does not exceed the limit of the skull bone to beyond it, so that the awl has a round letter below its sharp head, resembling a collar or a small circle, preventing it from diving and exceeding the thickness of the bone.
And you should take many of these awls, each of which is suitable for the thickness of that bone, so that you have for each skull a pierced amount of its sharp end in length and shortness according to the thickness of that skull, and this is an image of three types. Of awls, large, medium, and small:
As for the method of piercing around the broken bone, it is to place the awl on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know that the bone has been pierced, then move the awl to another location and make the distance between each hole the width of a chisel or thereabouts.
Then cut between each two holes with the sections and do this with as much gentleness as you can, as we said, until you remove the bone either with your hand or with something else from some of the instruments that have been prepared for that.
Such as forceps and fine pincers, and you should be extremely careful that the awl or section touches anything from the peritoneum, and if you strengthen the bone and separate it from the peritoneum if it is attached to it and remove it, then it must be cleaned.
The roughness that remains on the bone is smoothed with another instrument resembling a curette, but it should be thinner and more delicate than all other curettes. If there are any small pieces of bone or fragments remaining, they should be carefully removed with the instruments you have.
The wound is then treated with bandages and ointments, the mention of which will follow. As for the other side of the work, it is very easy and free from danger, as Galen mentioned and praised it greatly, and this is what he said. You should first begin by exposing the part of the bone in the place where the fracture is most severe and prominent, so that once you have exposed that part, you can place the tip of this lentiform curette underneath it. And this is its image:
The lentiform part of it should be smooth so as not to cut anything, and the sharp part of it should be on its sides extending lengthwise, as you see, so that the lentiform part rests against the peritoneum and the sharp edge of the curette is in the bone, then you strike the curette from one side.
With a small mallet until you cut through all the bone gently, as it rotates, and you are safe from the membrane, nothing frightening will happen at all, even if the healer is the most ignorant and cowardly of people, yes, even if he were soft.
If there is anything of the membrane still adhering to some parts of the bone, scrape it off with the tip of the lentiform curette itself and carefully free it; for it will be freed without harm or fear.
But if the fracture of the bone did not extend into the membrane and what broke off from the bone was a surface, and roughness and fine fragments remained in it, then you should remove that roughness and extract those fragments with delicate rasps, of which several have been made of different sizes so that you can use whichever one is most suitable and appropriate for each location according to what the work requires.
And the bone should be shaped according to its own form, and one should use in the first scraping of the bone the widest of those scrapers, then after that use a thinner one, and so on until you arrive at using the finest and thinnest of them all.
As for the other small, hairy fissures and fine fractures, treatment suitable to each one should be used, according to what leads to its healing; this is something that is not hidden from anyone who has the least training in this craft and has considered what we have written and explained about large fractures.
If the membrane remains exposed when you remove the bone, you should take a linen rag the size of the wound, soak it in rose ointment, and place it over the mouth of the wound, then take another rag folded twice or thrice. Soak it in syrup and rose ointment, and place it on top of the first rag. Do this as gently as possible so as not to weigh down the peritoneum, then use a wide bandage over it and do not tighten it except enough to hold the wound only.
Leave it with this treatment for a day or two until you are sure of the hot swelling subsiding, then remove it and afterwards use medicines that have a drying effect, such as asphodel roots, caraway seeds, gum tragacanth, and costmary, and so on.  Make a poultice from these drugs.
Spread it on the wound as it is dry. In general, use a medicine that will cleanse but not sting.  Take great care while treating the wound to keep it clean, without any fat, dirt, or pus accumulating in it at all.
For pus, if it collects on the membrane of the brain, will corrupt and rot it, and as a result of this the patient will suffer greatly. It may also appear in the peritoneum of the head when the bone is exposed, especially if its treatment is neglected.
If a black spot appears on the surface and you see the symptoms of gangrene we have mentioned, know for certain that it is doomed. Even if the blackness occurred due to a medicine applied to it, and if that medicine was strong enough to cause such blackness.
You should take one part honey and three parts rose oil, beat them well together, soak a cloth in the mixture, and place it on the abdomen. Then treat it with the types of treatment that are necessary until it is healed.
Chapter Three.
On setting a broken nose.
Know that the only part of the nose that can break is its upper bridge, either both sides or one, because they are bone. The lower part is cartilaginous and does not break but may suffer bruising, distortion, and displacement.
If one of the bridges breaks, you should insert your little finger into the nostril and straighten the fracture from the inside with your index finger and thumb on the outside until the nose returns to its natural shape. Do this gently and be careful not to cause the patient any pain.
If the fracture is at the top of the nose and cannot be reached with a finger, it should be set with the blunt end of a rod slightly thickened. If the fracture is on both sides, do the same. Set the fracture as soon as possible, ideally on the first day after the break.
If possible, do so; otherwise, after the seventh or tenth day when the hot swelling has subsided. Then insert a wick of linen cloth into the nostril if the fracture is on one side, or two wicks if it is on both sides.
Let the wick be thick enough to fill the nostril. Some early setters have said that the wick should be soaked in butter and changed every day, but I do not think so. Instead, soak the wick in egg white mixed with powdered brick dust.
Then the splint is left until the bone is set and the cartilage hardens, and pipes from goose feathers may be inserted into the nose in place of the splint after they have been wrapped with soft rags; this will make the fixation for a broken nose stronger, and so that the patient does not refrain from breathing.
This is not something necessary if you wish to make it, and if you wish to make splints; if swelling occurs in the nose during your work, dress the nose with cotton or cotton wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil or something from the diachylon plaster.
If hot swelling does not occur, it should be dressed from the outside with fine semolina and powdered candy, kneaded with egg white; then a soft compress is placed over it, and the nose is not tied up at all, if the nasal bones are broken in a small way.
Or if they are shattered, it should be incised and they should be removed with the instrument suitable for that; then the incision is sutured and treated with ointments appropriate for healing and closing; if a wound occurs inside the nose, it should be treated with a splint and lead pipes should be used. Until it heals.
Chapter Four.
On Setting a Broken Lower Jaw.
If the lower jaw is broken and there is no wound, if the fracture is only external and has not been broken in two and displaced inwards, then recognition is easy.
If the fracture is on the right side, you should insert your left-hand index finger into the patient’s mouth, and similarly if it is on the left side, insert the index finger of your right hand, and raise the prominence of the fracture from inside gently outwards.
Your other hand outside the bone will control its setting; if the jaw fracture has been split in two, you should use traction on both sides to straighten it out so that you can set it; if the teeth have become loose.
Or separate it, and tie what you hope to keep with gold, silver or silk thread; then put plaster on the broken bone, and place a folded cloth over it, and a large firm splint or piece of shoe leather equal in length to the bone. Then bind it from above according to what is suitable for tying and fitting so that it does not become loose, and order the patient to rest and be still, and make his food soft porridges; if you think that something has changed in shape in any way, hasten to undo it on the third day.
Then bind it from above according to what is suitable for tying and fitting so that it does not become loose, and order the patient to rest and be still, and make his food soft porridges; if you think that something has changed in shape in any way, hasten to undo it on the third day. Then correct what you bandaged with plaster of Paris mixed with egg white or semolina flour after removing the plaster from it, and place a soft compress on the bandage; as long as that bandage adheres to it and the bone does not change condition, leave it.
Do not undo it until it heals and the fracture becomes strong, for this fracture often strengthens in three weeks; if a hot swelling appears during this time, use what we have repeatedly mentioned to relieve it until that swelling subsides.
If the fracture is with an open wound, and it has healed from the bone fragments or pieces, then be gentle in removing those fragments with what suits you to remove them from the instrument; if the mouth of the wound is narrow, widen it with a scalpel as needed.
Then if you have removed those fragments and nothing remains, suture the wound opening if it is wide; otherwise apply one of the ointments suitable for that purpose. Stitch the wound until it heals.
Chapter Five.
On setting a broken clavicle.
The clavicle most often breaks in front of the end near the shoulder, and the fracture occurs in one of three ways: either it breaks and splits into two without fragments forming, which is the easiest to set;
If the fracture occurs with splinters, it is more difficult to set; or if the fracture occurs with a wound. If the fracture is without a wound, you should have two attendants: one to hold the arm above the broken shoulder and the other to extend the neck towards the opposite side.
Then straighten the fracture with your fingers until it assumes its proper shape and there is neither a bump nor a bend. If you need to extend it further, you should place a ball of linen or wool under the patient's armpit.
The size of the bone should be according to your need; extend and raise the shoulder, and press the ball with your hand until the fracture is straightened as it should be. If you are unable to pull the end of the clavicle outward because it has gone deep, then the patient should lie on his back.
A medium-sized cushion should be placed under his elbow, and the attendant should press down on his elbow so that the deep clavicular bone rises upward. Then straighten the fracture with your fingers.
If you feel that a fragment of the clavicle has broken and is moving, you should cut it open and carefully remove that fragment. If the fragment is trapped in the bone, try to cut it with one of the cutters you have prepared for this purpose. After placing the apparatus under the clavicle to keep the fracture in place, which is made of wood or iron, and this is its image:
It resembles a spoon without a bend; its width should be according to the size of the bone, whether large or small. Its length should also be according to what you can work with. Let it have two ends as you see, one wider and the other narrower.
If the wound you incised to remove the bone fragment is wide and you are confident there will be no hot swelling, then bring the edges of the wound together with sutures. If the incision is small or you fear hot swelling, then treat the wound with linen and bandages according to the extent of the incision.
If a hot tumor appears, moisten the rag in rose oil and vinegar or wine and apply it. As for setting a bone if there is no wound or incision, it is to apply on the bone a poultice made of mill dust with egg white. Then place soft padding on it, and then put a ball under his armpit if he needs it; then take a turban that is very long, its width being about a span, and carry a folded bandage over the padding and poultice; then take a splint made from a thin board. Its width should be three fingers and its length the same; then insert it into a rag, and insert that rag with the splint in the part of the turban which falls on the fracture site, then tighten the turban over the fracture as you wind it round. Let it pass over his neck and under his sound armpit, and under the arm of the patient; and wind it several times on each side, as you see that the tightening secures the fracture firmly; this is not hidden from you.
And remember always not to remove the splint from the broken bone. Therefore, you should examine the patient every day, and whenever the bandage becomes loose and you see that the splint has shifted, correct this and tighten the bandage. Then make the patient sleep on his back and put a small pillow under his armpit at night when he sleeps.
So that his arm is raised from his side and the clavicle fracture is elevated with the elevation of the elbow; or bind his arm to his neck, and do not loosen the bandage unless there occurs in the place an accident of abrasion or a swelling for twelve days.
Then renew the poultice if you see a need for it. And continue the tightening and leave it until the clavicle fracture unites and consolidates, becoming strong in twenty-eight days. And it may be less in some people.
Chapter Six.
In setting a fractured shoulder, know that the shoulder rarely breaks in its wide part but rather in its angles. Whenever it breaks in one place or in the middle, which is known by touch, the treatment is applied according to the type of fracture.
Set it and return it to its natural shape as best you can, then apply a mixture of rye flour with egg white and soft lint to the area. Place a bandage of folded cloth on top, and then put a wide splint made of wood over it.
Make the splint thin enough to cover the entire shoulder or slightly wider, and if there is swelling in one part of the shoulder under the splint, smooth it with soft lint until the splint lies evenly. Then tie it securely with a long turban.
Tie it tightly and make sure the splint does not move from its place. Check the bandage every day, and whenever it loosens, tighten it and adjust the splint if it has shifted. Let the patient lie on his right side, and the shoulder will heal in twenty days.
days, or twenty-five days at most. When this period is complete, untie the bandage and you will be safe, for it is one of the bones that is not prone to shattering or dislocation. If a bone fragment protrudes and lies under the skin, cut over it and remove it. Do what was mentioned for a fractured clavicle to relieve any hot swelling that may occur.
Chapter Seven.
In setting a fractured rib.
The chest may break in the middle, which is rare, while its ends are more frequently broken and shattered. If the middle breaks, it will tend to slope downwards and cause severe pain, shortness of breath, and coughing.
And it may happen that blood flows and the indentation in the broken bone becomes apparent, which is not hidden from feeling. To set it, the patient should lie on his back, a pillow being placed between his shoulder blades, then his elbows are pressed down and his ribs are brought together with the hands on both sides, and he is carefully straightened in every way possible.
And it should be held until the bone regains its proper shape, then a poultice and bandage are applied to it, and on top of that a splint is placed made from a thin board of boxwood or willow, or something similar in lightness after it has been wrapped in cloth. Then it is carefully tied over the broken bone so that it does not slip, and the bandage is passed over the shoulder in a circular motion several times and tightened firmly. Then the bandage is checked at all times, and whenever it loosens it is tightened.
And if necessity requires it to be undone at times, if pain, inflammation, or swelling appears in the place, hasten to undo it and remove the poultice. Then rectify what has occurred by treating it accordingly, and replace the poultice if you see fit. And bind it firmly until it heals.
Chapter Eight.
In setting the ribs if they are broken, know that fractures in the ribs occur most often in the thick parts following the back and at their ends in front, because these parts are cartilaginous. This is not hidden from feeling when examining with the fingers.
Set it by straightening the fracture with your fingers as much as possible until the shape is restored, then bandage and bind the broken bone with a splint if necessary. If the rib fracture is angled inward, the patient will experience severe pain.
And it may cause a cough similar to that experienced by one with pleurisy, because the bone presses on the diaphragm and also causes difficulty breathing, coughing, and profuse expectoration of blood. This is difficult to treat, and earlier practitioners have devised many tricks for dealing with it.
Some say that the diet of the patient should be such as to produce wind and flatulence, so that the belly may swell and distend, and the fracture may be driven outwards. We disapprove of this, lest it encourage the formation of a hot swelling. If a swelling has not already occurred, it will cause one to form; and if it has already formed, it will increase and aggravate it.
Some say that a cupping glass should be placed over the seat of the fracture and then drawn off forcibly, which is somewhat like cauterization, except that they fear the cupping glass may draw in extraneous matter to the place on account of its weakness. Others say that the seat of the fracture should be covered with wool steeped in hot oil.
The dressing should be made so as to fill the space between the ribs, so that the bandage may lie flat; if it is wound round a convexity, it will not lie flat. The patient should then be treated for his sprain with diet and medicine, provided he is not suffering from any severe pain which he cannot bear. The bone was found to be pressing upon the diaphragm in a painful manner, and we were afraid for the patient, so we decided to make an incision over the seat of the fracture, expose the broken rib, and place under it the instrument for preserving the peritoneum, which has already been described.
