We then cut through the bone carefully and extracted it.
We then unite the edges of the wound by sutures if it is large, and treat it with ointments until it heals. If a hot swelling should appear during this time, hasten to apply dressings smeared with rose oil to the place, and treat the patient internally with remedies to allay the swelling.
He should lie on the side which is easier for him to sleep upon until he recovers.
Chapter Nine.
On the Reduction of Dislocations of the Dorsal Vertebrae and Cervical Vertebrae.
As for the bones of the neck, fractures rarely occur there, sprains being much more common. The same is true of the dorsal vertebrae. If someone suffers such an injury and you wish to know whether it will heal or not... Look and see if his hands have become relaxed and numb and lifeless, and he is unable to move them or spread them or clench them; and if you prick them or stick them with a needle he does not feel it nor find pain in them, then know that he will not recover in most cases for he is lost.
And if he moves them and feels the pricking and sticking, then know that the marrow of the bone has been spared and that the patient will recover with treatment. If a swelling in the back is similarly affected and you wish to know whether it too will recover or not.
Look at his legs; if you see that they have become relaxed and what happened in the hands has also occurred in them, then if he lies on his back the wind and excrement pass involuntarily, and if he lies on his stomach urine passes involuntarily. And if he lies on his back and wishes to urinate but cannot, then know that he is lost; do not concern yourself with treating him. If none of these things occur to him the matter is lighter, and the treatment for what has happened is to aim at soothing the hot swelling.
By placing on the diseased vertebra rose oil alone or with roasted egg yolks, apply this three times a day until the hot swelling subsides, then place one of the reinforcing dressings that has been starched upon the spot and bind it tight with a bandage. Order him to remain still and quiet.
Let him not sleep except on the side where he does not feel pain until he recovers. If, at the time of the injury, a fragment or something else of bone has become detached and healed, then it is necessary to cut open his skin and extract that bone. Then bring together the edges of the wound, if it is large, with stitches and treat it with pounded ointments until it heals. If the last bone of the coccyx has been broken, then enter your left thumb into the anus
The broken bone is set with the other hand according to what can be achieved and then it is held in place with a dressing and bandage if necessary, and then it is bound tight.
If you feel a broken fragment in it, cut over it and remove it, and treat the wound as previously described until it heals.
Chapter Ten.
On setting a hip fracture.
Hip bones rarely break; but when they do, the fracture usually consists of a shattering at the extremities and a lengthwise crack with an inward inclination. The patient experiences pain in the area, numbness and a tingling sensation in the leg on the side of the fracture.
To set it, pass your hand over it until you locate the fracture and determine its shape. If the fracture is only at the extremities, set that fracture according to what seems possible to you so that it resembles its natural form.
If the fracture is lengthwise or has inclined inwards, place the patient on their stomach so that you can set the fracture. Once it is set, apply a dressing over it and then put a splint of wood or leather on top and bind it tight.
Do not fear the displacement of the fracture or the loosening of the splint. Level any depressions in the splint with padding so that the bandage tightens evenly. Instruct the patient to sleep on their back or on their right side; if swelling occurs, apply a cold compress.
Stop stretching and reset the fracture until the swelling subsides. Apply a poultice as previously described to reduce the swelling, then return to setting the fracture and binding it properly. If there are fragments or splinters at the extremities of the bone, do not remove them.
Do not touch them but set them from the outside as we have said and leave it bound until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On the setting of a humerus fracture.
The humerus is what is between the elbow and the head of the shoulder, if it breaks, set it in one of two ways. One of them is to take a curved, smooth rod of medium thickness as shown: .
And tie two bands at its ends, then hang it from a high place and have the patient sit on a chair, then put his broken arm on the rod so that his armpit becomes stuck in the middle of the curve of the rod, then hang something heavy from above it.
Or a servant extends it downwards, then the doctor straightens the fracture with his hands together until he returns the fracture to its proper place, and the other way is for the patient to lie on his back and hang his hand from his neck with a band.
Then order two servants, one to hold above the fracture with his hands and the other to hold below it, and have each one extend it to his side, and if you want the extension to be stronger, tie a band under the fracture and a band above it and have each of the servants extend them to their sides.
If the fracture is near the end of the elbow, the middle of the band should be under the armpit and the other under the fracture towards the elbow, and similarly if the fracture is near the elbow, the band should be on that place and on the elbow itself.
Then straighten the fracture gently without violence until it becomes even as it should be and joins a good union, then tighten it if no hot swelling appears, but if hot swelling does appear, leave it loosened until the seventh day and put a cooling poultice soaked in vinegar and rose oil on it.
Until the swelling subsides, then tighten it at that time. The way to tighten it is to hold the bandage on the fracture, then hold a wrap of new cloth over the bandage, then gather the arm on the humerus itself and place his hand open on his elbow.
If the rags and binding are strong enough to support the arm and forearm, then the arm may serve as a splint if nothing prevents this and there is no change in the broken bone. If you fear that there might be a change, use splints.
Place on the fracture itself a splint that is wider and stronger than other splints, and leave the width of a finger between each splint. The length of the splints should be according to the fracture with an addition of three fingers on each side.
Then tighten the bandages as mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And your binding should be tightest over the fracture site.
The farther from the fracture, the looser the binding should be. If you see that the splints and bindings are as we have described when setting the limb, then do so.
If you fear hot swelling, leave the binding and splints until the seventh day as we have said, then examine the bandage every three days so that no chafing or bloating occurs or that food is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive tightness, correcting all of this as we have mentioned.
If you are confident that none of this will happen, do not loosen the bandage except after many days. The patient should lie on his back with his hand on his stomach and a pillow filled with moderate wool placed under his arm.
Check at all times, day or night, to ensure that the shape of the broken limb is not distorted or the bandage loosened, correcting all of this with your utmost effort. Make sure the patient's diet is as we have prescribed, starting with soft foods until the bone begins to strengthen.
Then the patient's diet should be increased. It is customary for the arm and leg to strengthen in forty days, at which time the bandage should be removed, baths should be taken, and ointments suitable for this purpose should be applied. If the fracture was severe and crushed...
Do not undo its ligature for fifty days or two months.
Chapter the Twelfth.
On setting a broken arm.
The arm is composed of two bones called the radius and ulna, one being smaller which lies next to the thumb, and the other larger which lies beneath the smaller one. Sometimes only the larger bone breaks, or only the smaller one, and sometimes they both break.
When the smaller upper bone breaks, setting it is easy and healing is quicker. When the lower ulna breaks, the fracture is bad and healing is difficult. The worst case is when both bones break together. If the broken bone is the smaller upper radius.
The physician, when setting it, should make the stretch slight and gentle so as to straighten it. If the ulna is broken, he should apply a stronger stretch. And if both bones are broken, he should apply an even stronger stretch.
The shape of the hand should be maintained during setting and stretching, placed extended on a cushion, with the thumb raised higher than all the other fingers, the little finger lowest of all, and the ring finger squared upon itself.
Let the cushion be at a suitable height in front of him so that the patient does not find it tiring.
Then a servant should extend the arm from below, either with his hand or with a bandage, and another servant extends it from above in the same manner. The physician then straightens the bone until he returns it to the best possible shape. If there are bone fragments in the fracture, they are removed.
Return every fragment to its place as best you can. If a loose fragment appears which is threatening the skin and there is no hope of setting it, cut over it and remove it in the manner we have previously described.  If the fracture is accompanied by a wound, there is a separate chapter for it.
You should treat it from there, for if there appears in the first dressing a hot swelling, then apply a poultice of linen cloth impregnated with rose oil and white wax, let it be moderate in thickness, and bind it gently until the swelling subsides.
Then remove the linen cloth and apply a plaster prepared from powdered rye flour with egg white, then apply splints; let the splint placed on the fracture itself be slightly wider and stronger. Know that the number of arm splints is six in most cases.
Whether the fracture was in one bone or in both together, then make your pressure on the fracture site firmer and tighter, and whenever you turn with the pressure upward or downward, make the pressure a little looser as previously mentioned at the beginning of this chapter.
Let the cloths that are wrapped around the fracture be soft, damp cloths and not very hard, and let the thread with which it is tightened be linen, especially of medium thickness as we have described, and check the limb and ligaments after a few days.
For if something needs to be corrected, such as a chafing that has occurred in the limb, then the limb should be soaked in warm water until the chafing subsides and the limb should be left untied for a night so that it can rest, then tightening should be resumed.
If the pressure has slackened and the bone has shifted or something similar, correct it all with your best effort. Also see if food is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive tightness, then you should loosen it a little and leave it for a few days so that nourishment can flow to it, and then tighten it again.
If none of what we have mentioned occurs to the patient, then he should not be released until after twenty days or so. Then hang the patient's arm around his neck and let his arm be moderate; he should restrain himself from tumultuous movements and sleep on his back.
Know that this fracture of the arm will be healed in thirty days or in thirty-two, and perhaps it will be healed in twenty-eight days, all depending on the states of temperaments and strengths.
Chapter Thirteen.
In setting a fracture of the hand and fingers.
The metacarpus of the hand and phalanges of the fingers are rarely fractured, but they are often bruised; so whenever a fracture or bruise occurs in the hand.
The patient should sit cross-legged with a chair directly in front of him.
Then he should place his hand on it extended, and the servant should extend the broken bone; the physician straightens it until it unites well. Then a bandage and splint should be applied if there is no hot swelling. Afterward, a splint should be applied over the area.
Having padded it with a soft cloth, if the fracture is downward towards the palm of the hand, make a sphere-like shape from cloth and have the patient grasp it with his broken hand; then tie it securely with a long cloth.
Let the splint be made of supple leather so that it conforms to the bending of the entire hand, and tie it securely. If the fracture is outward, a splint should be placed on top and another below the hand so that the hand remains open and upright.
Then apply a bandage as the hand rotates, interlacing it between the fingers. If a fracture occurs in one of the phalanges, and it is the thumb, straighten it properly and then bind it with the hand; if you wish, you may make a small upright splint to support the fracture.
And let it not move. If the fracture is in any of the other fingers like the middle, index, ring or little finger, straighten it and bind it with the adjacent healthy finger, or bind them all together; this is best. Or place a small upright splint on them.
As we said in the case of a sprain and when you set it after keeping away from a hot swelling, meet it with what is appropriate whenever any of that occurs as has been repeatedly described.
Chapter Fourteen.
In setting a fracture of the thigh.
The femur often breaks and is revealed by feeling because it turns forward and backward, and its setting is done by tightening a ligature above the fracture and another ligature below the fracture while the patient lies face down.
Then each ligature is extended to its side in moderation, if the fracture is in the middle of the bone; but if it is near the origin of the thigh, a soft ligature should be tightened, either from wool or something similar.
At the origin of the thigh towards the groin, the pull should be upward and the other ligature below the fracture; and likewise if the fracture is near the knee, let the ligature be near the knee so that the pull is downward. Then the physician straightens the bone with both hands until it returns to its natural shape.
And the bone unites well, then a bandage and strapping should be applied unless the limb becomes hot and swollen; if so leave it for a few days until the swelling subsides and then return to its treatment.
As for strapping it, a broad firm bandage should be wound twice or thrice over the fracture, and what is left over should remain; then the leg is wound until the heel is at the origin of the thigh and a long thread is inserted between the thigh and the leg near the knee from below, with its ends above on both sides.
Then the remainder of the bandage is wound around the leg and thigh, and splints are placed on the thigh at the site of the fracture itself, and one splint is made from them on the tibia bone; then the space between the thigh and the leg is filled with soft rags so that the strapping is even.
Then begin to bind strongly from the middle over the fracture, three or four turns; and the farther you are from the fracture with the bandage, the weaker, gentler, and looser your binding should be.
Then proceed to the ends of the thread which you inserted between the thigh and the leg, and tie them to the following splints above; then pass the ends of the thread down until they reach the ankle of the foot, and tighten them also at the ends of the splints on the other side.
So that the bandage does not slip from its place, then leave it tight as long as the limb does not complain of pain, swelling, or bloating, or anything similar; if any of these occur, hasten to loosen it and correct whatever has happened as we have mentioned many times before.
If there is a fragment of bone that protrudes, it should be straightened if possible; otherwise, cut it off and remove it, and treat the wound as previously mentioned until it heals. It is sometimes possible to bind this fracture from the thigh without adding the leg with splints.
As we mentioned for the humerus and arm, but setting it this way does not cause limping; if you set only the thigh without joining it to the leg, its owner will always limp.
Know that the thigh heals in fifty days or a little more or less, all depending on the difference in temperaments and other conditions.
Chapter Fifteen.
On Fractures of the Patella.
The kneecap is rarely fractured, though it often suffers bruising; if it does fracture, it is either a crack or a fragmentation into pieces, which may occur with or without a wound.
You determine all this by feeling, and set the fractured parts together with your fingers until they unite and meld as much as possible, exercising caution and skill; then apply a bandage and place a splint around it if needed.
And tighten from above the corresponding ligature, then proceed in all cases which we have described in other fractures, such as hot swelling and the like, by meeting every obstacle with what suits it until it heals.
Chapter Sixteen.
In the setting of a fractured leg.
The leg has two bones, one thick and called the tibia, and the other slender and called the fibula. They are subject to the same types of fractures as the bones of the arm. Therefore, setting it is the same as setting the arm, and the work is one. If both bones are fractured, the leg turns in all directions; if the slender bone is fractured, the leg turns forward; and if the thicker bone is fractured, and this occurs from below, then it is something that will not be hidden from you.
So use traction and alignment and bind the splints accordingly, except that if the fracture of the leg is a severe one with many fragments, the traction should be less and lighter, and you should endeavor to set it carefully.
And in the leg there is something extra to do compared to the arm, which is that when you have aligned the splints and finished all your work, take two small pieces of pine wood which are used for leveling the rooms placed between the cracks of the boards or they can be from palm leaves or the like.
Choose from them those that are slightly thick and not thin, and let their length be along the leg from the knee down; then wrap a cloth twice along the length of each one and place one on the leg and the other on the opposite side.
Let them extend from the knee to below the foot, then tie the two pieces of wood in three places at the ends and the middle. By this restraint, the leg will be prevented from tilting right and left and will be well immobilized; a wooden gutter can also be used along the length of the leg.
And a dressing should be placed upon it to preserve it from movement, and this is especially what should be done in a fracture if there is a wound with it. Then the leg should be examined every day, and great care should be taken of it for swelling or bloating or any such thing. So, whenever any of these things happen, treat them accordingly until it is healed, and the bone of the leg will be joined in thirty days or so.
Chapter Seventeenth.
On Fractures of the Bones of the Leg and Fingers.
As for the heel, fracture does not occur to it at all. As for the bones of the leg, they may be fractured, and the fingers are also rarely fractured, but rather bruised in most cases.
If a fracture occurs to the bones of the leg and you see that those bones are somewhat overlapping, let the patient place his foot on the ground upright as if walking, then you put your foot on what has risen from those bones and press it down and straighten it.
Until it returns to its place, then apply a bandage and splint over it, and place under the sole of the foot a small plate with two flat heads, then tighten the foot firmly with it after wrapping it with rags and whatever else it needs.
If three or four days have passed, loosen the bandage, for you will find the bones level whether they were broken or dislocated. Do as you see. As for if some of the fingers are broken, force them and straighten them according to what I have described to you for setting the fingers of the hand.
Then make a splint for the broken finger along the length of the finger, and let it be slightly wider than it. Then put under the foot this plate that I have described to you and tighten it firmly; if two, three or more bones are broken.
Make a splint for each finger, according to its size, inserted in a soft cloth and tighten the plate at the bottom of the foot, letting each head of the two heads of the plate extend outward from the sole of the foot for proper adjustment.
And you must not forget to fulfill all that we have mentioned to you in the rest of the fractures regarding the symptoms which we have described, and meet each symptom with what is appropriate.
Chapter Eighteen.
In the fracture of a woman's pubis bone and a man's femur.
When a woman's vulva is fractured, seat her upright then incline her slightly towards her back and hold her from behind. Then have the midwife fill her vulva with cotton until it is full and becomes like a ball in her vulva.
Then shake the woman and raise her torso slightly; the cotton will come out until it is at the opening of the vulva like a ball, and the bone fracture will be reduced. Then apply a dressing on her back. Whenever she wishes to urinate, remove the cotton gently so that she can urinate.
Then return it in the same way it was first inserted, and she returns to her original dressing. Do this for seven days or so, for it will then heal. If you wish, you can take a sheep's bladder, stretch a reed tube over its mouth, and insert the whole bladder into her vulva.
Then blow strongly into the tube until the bladder inflates inside the vulva; the fracture will be reduced. Then stuff it with cotton and remain for several days as we have described until it is healed. As for when the pubic bone of a man or woman is fractured, the same method is used to set it.
And it is set in the same way we described for the hip bone. It will not be hidden from you what is correct in these unusual fractures, which rarely occur, especially for one who has some experience and understands this book of mine, yes, because the fracture itself indicates to you in most cases.
The right way to set and bind it, so understand. As for a man, if his thigh bone is fractured like a goose's neck, insert the penis into it, then put a bandage of cloth over it and wrap it. Leave it for three days or so until it heals.
Chapter Nineteen.
In setting bone fractures if there is a wound.
Whoever has a fracture with a wound, especially if the bone is large like the femur or humerus or the like, should hasten to bleed him as soon as possible if the conditions for bleeding allow, as we have previously mentioned.
If the wound is bleeding, you should hasten to cauterize it by sprinkling powdered alum over it if nothing else is available; then proceed to set the fracture on that same day and do not delay.
If no hot swelling occurs, but if a hot swelling does occur, leave the setting until the ninth day so that the hot swelling subsides and do not approach it at all on the third or fourth day because it will be subject to bad symptoms.
If the broken bone is protruding onto the exposed skin, you should try to return it and straighten it gently with your hands and a slight extension. If you cannot return it and straighten it with your hands, return it with this instrument which is made of iron seven or eight fingers long. Its width should be according to the size of the wound.
Therefore, the physician should have three or four of them, as needed for each type of fracture treatment, and let them be rounded with a slight thickness so that they do not bend when pressed upon during work.
And it should have a sharp end with a bend at its end, the top being thicker and from the middle to the bottom very thin. This is its form:
It is called a "periosteum" in Greek, meaning a small spatula. The sharp bent end should be placed on the protruding bone and pushed once so that when the bone returns and straightens somewhat, it can then straighten the ends of the fracture against each other.
If the broken end is thin and the instrument does not grasp it well, the end of that bone should be cut so that the instrument can take hold of it. If you cannot reduce the bone at all as we have described, cut it according to the shapes of the sections we mentioned.
Or spread it with one of the instruments as you are able, then remove any remaining roughness and thin scales from the bone. If, after reduction, the patient experiences severe, painful distress, know that the bone has not returned to its natural place.
If you can reduce it to its natural place, do so, for you will greatly benefit the patient. Once you have completed setting the bone, soak a cloth in a dark astringent liquid, especially if it is summer, and do not put anything greasy on the wound.
So that decay and putrefaction do not occur, then use splints when you have finished setting the bone and leave the wound uncovered by cutting a hole in the bandages with scissors to the size of the wound.
Be very careful not to tighten the wound with the fracture, for ignorant physicians often do this and cause their patients either death or suppuration or necrosis. Let your binding be loose and gentle, unlike the binding of all other fractures.
If the wound is bad or a large one and you fear some of the poor symptoms we have described, and he experiences worrisome pain in the area, then you should not apply splints. Instead, make him bandages of strong cloth where the splints would be and bind it tightly.
And if, after a day or two, you see that pus has begun to form in the wound, remove the cloth that was placed on it with the astringent liquid and then use compresses and ointments that we are accustomed to treating wounds with, such as the quadripartite ointment and the like. And you should loosen the ligature.
You should inspect the wound every morning and evening until it heals and closes. The affected part should be positioned so that pus flows easily downwards. If, after many days, the wound has not closed or the pus continues to flow, know that there are small bone fragments present.
You should then explore the wound with a probe. If any of those fragments are clean, remove and extract them. If any are not clean and cause pain and inflammation to the limb, cut them into pieces and remove them in any way possible.
If the wound shows signs of coldness, putrefaction, or any other type of corruption, you should treat each condition according to the remedies described in its respective chapter.
Remember this well: if a large bone is broken and you are dealing with a major limb like the femur or humerus, do not attempt to pull it out or reposition it. This often results in death, so leave it until it decays and it may fall out on its own after twenty or thirty days.
Then treat the wound if you see a place suitable for treatment, otherwise leave it alone.
Chapter Twenty.
On the Treatment of Complications Arising from Certain Fractures.
This complication often occurs after a fracture has healed, especially those near joints. It can cause deformity and prevent the limb from functioning normally.
If the complication is soft, use astringent remedies such as mastic, frankincense, myrrh, asafoetida, acacia, or similar substances. Combine some or all of these with an astringent decoction, egg white, or vinegar.
Apply this paste to the complication and bandage it tightly, leaving the bandage in place for many days before removing it and applying a fresh one. Continue this process until the complication subsides, God willing. Alternatively, apply a tight lead plate over the area.
Lead has a property that dissolves everything that is found in the organs. If a concretion has become hardened and severe, and necessity compels its removal, cut it open from above and remove the excess that results, or scrape it with some instruments until it is gone, then treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-One.
In the treatment of a fracture, if it has knitted but the limb afterwards remains weak and unlike its original nature.
If a bone fracture has knitted but the limb afterwards remains weak and frail, this is due to many reasons. One of them is either for the excessive loosening and tying of ligaments in a way that is not appropriate, or for the excessive tightening of ligaments so that nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb, or for excessive prolonged traction, or for excessive movements at an inappropriate time, or for a deficiency of blood in the patient's body and its weakness.
The treatment for this is to nourish the patient and enrich his body so that blood increases within it, use baths, introduce cheerfulness and joy into his life, and so on. Then apply pitch to the limb so that it attracts a great deal of nourishment to itself, or continue to traction it with lukewarm water. So that nourishment flows into it and it returns to its natural form.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
In the treatment of broken bones that have knitted crookedly and prevent it from functioning properly.
When a limb, after its fracture has healed, presents with a crookedness or a protuberance of the broken bone, or a complication that disfigures the limb. Unless the limb is prevented from performing its natural function, one should not accept the statement of someone who claims to break the bone again from the beginning, for many ignorant physicians and bone-setters used to do this in our country.
This action is highly reprehensible and leads to great danger, the easiest of which is distortion. However, if the crookedness and complexity are soft, it should be straightened with water in which relaxing herbs such as marshmallow leaf and its root, rosemary, and the like have been cooked.
It is bandaged with relaxing bandages such as well-made diachylon, or saliva from the root of marshmallow is taken and beaten with chicken fat and sherbet ointment and bandaged with it, or a plump fig is taken and pounded with dove's dung and other remedies called antispasmodics.
And complexity is often resolved by continuous gentle massage done with the hands, using movement of the limb in all directions at all times. If the crookedness has been longstanding and severe, and has hardened, and iron treatment becomes necessary.
It should be incised above and the bone connection released, and what remains of the crookedness or bone should be cut into fine sections with great care and gentleness, then the wound should be treated as previously mentioned until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
Dislocation is the displacement of a joint from its place, which impedes movement and distorts the shape of the limb, and causes severe pain to the patient.  Therefore, whenever someone suffers a dislocation, they should be promptly treated to relocate it.
Do not delay treatment at all, for if it is delayed, swelling will occur, making it difficult to relocate the dislocation. Therefore, it should not be delayed, nor moved, nor stretched while it is swollen, because this often causes the patient severe pain and spasms.
But if this occurs, one should promptly perform bloodletting on the patient and then leave them until the swelling subsides somewhat, then bathe the limb in hot water and oil, and gently relocate it. Then treat each limb as mentioned in its appropriate place.
And I have also arranged the chapters on dislocations according to the order of the fracture, from the top of the body to the bottom.
Chapter Twenty-Fourth.
In the Treatment of Dislocation of the Lower Jaw.
Dislocations of the jaws are rare, and their occurrence can take two forms: either a slight displacement where they relax a little, or a complete dislocation where they relax fully. Toward the chest, so that the patient's saliva flows and he cannot hold it back, nor can he clench his jaw, and his tongue stumbles in speech; as for a slight dislocation, it often returns on its own quite easily.
But if the dislocation is complete, it should be immediately reduced without delay. The assistant should hold the patient's head while the surgeon inserts the thumb of one hand into the base of the jaw inside the mouth, if the dislocation is on one side.
Or he inserts both thumbs if the dislocation is on both sides, and with the other fingers outside, he straightens it; he then instructs the patient to relax his jaw and let it go in all directions, while the surgeon straightens the jaw and pushes it until it returns to its place.
If it is difficult to reduce them, especially if both jaws are dislocated, apply a compress soaked in hot water and ointment to facilitate their return; do not delay the reduction as we have said. Once they are back in place, aligned, and the patient's mouth closes without relaxing, then place bandages of cloth over them with splints made of wax and ointment.
Then tie them gently with a loose bandage. The patient should sleep on their back, with their head elevated between two pillows so that they do not move it to the right or left, and they should refrain from chewing anything but have soft food prepared for them.
Even if the pain subsides and the jaw heals, let him eat what he desires, but let him do so gently and not force open his mouth when eating, drinking, or yawning until the jaw is healed and cured.
If the jaws are difficult to bring back together after they have become separated at any time and do not return to their place, it often results in fevers and constant headaches, and sometimes the patient's stomach will erupt and he may vomit repeatedly; then if you see this, know that he is corrupted.
And often one who suffers from this dies within ten days.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
On Reducing a Dislocated Shoulder and Clavicle.
As for the clavicle, it does not dislocate inward due to its connection to the chest; however, it may dislocate outward, which becomes apparent upon examination.  Compel the patient to lie on his back and extend his arms.
Then press the area with your hand with force, and it will return; then apply a bandage and splints to it and bind them tightly. As for its end which connects to the shoulder, it rarely dislocates; but if it does happen to dislocate one day, it should be reduced and set as we have described and as is suitable.
Then apply a bandage, splints and binding to it and order the patient to observe rest and tranquility until he is healed. With this same treatment, the end of the clavicle is also reduced if it has become displaced.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
On Reducing a Dislocated Shoulder.
Know that the shoulder dislocates in three ways: one is dislocation towards the armpit downwards; the second is dislocation towards the chest; and rarely, it may dislocate above the shoulder.
And it does not break backwards to the shoulder or forward to the nerve, and most often it breaks and comes out downwards towards the armpit, especially in those with little flesh because it comes out quickly in them and enters quickly.
As for those with much flesh, it is otherwise, meaning that it comes out with difficulty and enters with difficulty. And perhaps some people experience a blow or a fall so the elbow swells and becomes hot, and they think that it has broken, so it should be examined to determine its soundness.
Then you proceed with its treatment, and you recognize the dislocation if it is downwards towards the armpit; if you compare between the dislocated elbow and the sound one, you will find an apparent difference and you will find the head of the elbow in a depression and when touching under the armpit, the head of the elbow feels like an egg.
And the patient cannot raise his hand to his ear nor move it with all its movements. Likewise, if it dislocates towards the chest or upwards, you will find that apparent upon touching and it is not hidden.
And this dislocation can be easily reduced if it is recent or the patient is a boy, and to reduce it, have a servant raise his hand upwards, then place your thumbs under his armpit and lift the joint strongly upwards to its place while the servant raises his hand and extends it upwards.
Then he lowers it downwards, and it will return quickly. If it does not return as mentioned and the dislocation has been for many days, then the patient should bathe in hot water and use a poultice that relaxes and softens, such as cooking the root of marshmallow, fenugreek, and rosemary in water and apply it.
Then the patient lies on his back and you place a ball of wool under his armpit, which should be moderate between softness and hardness, then the doctor puts his heel on the ball and holds the patient's hand with his hand and extends it downwards.
And he will push the ball with his foot, for it will return to its place. And if you wish to return it in this manner, prepare a man taller than the patient and have him stand on the side and insert his elbow under the patient's armpit and raise his armpit upwards until the patient is suspended in the air. And another attendant will pull the patient's hand down to his belly; if the patient is light, something else should be hung on him to weigh him down, for it returns the jaw to its place. It may also be returned in another way.
Another way is to focus on the ground a long piece of wood with a round head like the pestle, not too thick nor too thin; then it is placed under the patient's armpit after placing a soft rag on the head of the wood and the patient standing along the length of the wood.
Then he extends his hand down from the other side and also stretches his body from the same side with force, for the joint quickly returns to its place. If it is difficult to return it by all that we have mentioned, then use this remedy: take a piece of wood about two arm's lengths long. And its width is the size of four fingers and its thickness is the size of two fingers, it has a round head to make it easy to enter deep into the armpit in this way: .
Then a soft rag is tied on the round head so that the wood does not hurt the patient, then it is placed under the armpit and the whole hand and arm are extended on the wood, and the wood is tied to the upper arm, forearm, and end of the hand.
Then the arm is placed on a horizontal beam ladder and the hand is extended downwards, leaving the rest of the body hanging from the other side, for the joint enters its place. And if it enters in any way possible, then a ball of moderate size made of wool should be placed under the armpit.
Then a prepared poultice of mill dust, frankincense, and egg white is applied over the entire shoulder as it rotates from above; then the ball is firmly tightened under the armpit and bandaged over the poultice from above, with his hand hanging around his neck.
His hand is left immobile for seven days, and the patient's diet should be light so that the limb may strengthen, for this is quicker for its recovery. Then after seven or five days it is loosened and exercised with movement; if it remains firm and does not relax, it has healed.
But if the joint repeatedly dislocates due to moisture or some other cause, cauterization should be used with three white-hot irons as previously described in the chapter on cautery. If all this is done and the bandage is loosened after seven days, and the joint does not remain stable.
The poultice and bandage were repeated several times, but it did not hold; it fell and relaxed, and you could not raise it upward. Know then that the nerve in the head of the shoulder has been severed, stretched, or relaxed. Therefore, know at that time that the joint will never remain stable in its place.
As for the dislocation which occurs towards the chest and the breastbone, and behind it, it is reduced by pushing and pulling with the hands until it returns. All bandaging and treatment are used in it until it heals. If stiffness develops in the limb afterward and movement is slow.
The patient should frequently use the bath so that the stiffness softens and returns to its original state.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On the Treatment of Elbow Dislocation.
The elbow joint dislocates with difficulty and also returns with difficulty. It dislocates in all directions, especially forward or backward. Its dislocation is not hidden from you, as it lies under sight and touch, regardless of the way it has dislocated.
And if you join the dislocated joint to the sound one, it will become clear to you by an obvious sign; the joint will be crooked and the arm will not be able to bend nor touch the elbow with the hand. You should hasten to return the dislocation from its position before a hot swelling occurs for it.
For if a hot swelling occurs for it, it will be difficult to return and it may not heal at all, especially if the dislocation is backwards; for it is the worst of all types of dislocations, the most painful and death often comes with it.
And if it can be returned, let a servant extend his hand with both hands and arms outstretched, while the doctor's hand is above the elbow and below it; he pushes the joint with the thumbs of both his hands or the base of his palm until it returns to its place.
And if the dislocation is forward, it may be returned by bending the hand once so that the base of its palm strikes the elbow opposite it; and if the dislocation does not return, use a very powerful stretching method; which is to extend the arm by two servants.
And the patient is also held by two servants so that he does not move during stretching, then the arm is rotated in all directions after a folded cloth or a wide bandage is wrapped around it; and if the doctor begins with his hands to massage the joint, let him anoint it with oil so that it will be easier for the joint to slip back into place.
Then the joint is pushed hard until it returns, and after its return, it should be bandaged with a bandage containing a grip and drying with egg white; and it should be tightened tightly and the arm hung from the patient's neck and left for days.
Then it is removed; if the joint is secure in its place, remove the bandage and leave it alone. If you see that the joint has not become firm, reapply the bandage and splint and leave it for days until it becomes firm then remove it. If inflammation occurs after the joint returns and the bandage is removed
If there is stiffness in movement, let him use moistening in the bath and gentle massage and pinching until it softens, or place on the joint a fat sheep's caul, then bind it and leave it for a day and night, then remove it and put him in the bath, and when he sweats, rub the joint with a moderate rubbing.
Then repeat the sheep's caul again and again with entering the bath until it softens, and if you wish to put on him fresh cow dung moistened slightly with butter and bind it, do that several times, for it softens and returns to its former state.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
On the Treatment of Dislocation of the Wrist.
The wrist of the hand is often dislocated, and returning it to place is easier than with other joints; however, it should be hastened as soon as it is dislocated, before the location swells or becomes hot and inflamed.
To return it to place, put the wrist of the patient on a board and have an attendant extend his hand; then the physician places his hand on the prominence of the joint and pushes until it returns; however, he must look to see if the dislocation has moved towards the inside of the hand.
Let the patient place the outer side of his hand on the board when extending and returning it. If the dislocation is prominent towards the outer side of the hand, let him place the inner side of his hand on the board so that the physician's hand falls on the same prominence of the joint. If it returns immediately, that is enough; otherwise, bind it with a bandage that reduces swelling.
Leave it alone and do not return to treating it, for it cannot bear it, and you will not be able to return it after a few days have passed; however, the joint remains in its place and does not harm the patient unless his hand becomes weak and unable to grasp anything.
If that happens, then know that the nerve has been severed or crushed, and there is nothing to be done except to bind it tightly with a splint; perhaps this will help, and perhaps it will not help at all.
Then it is softened and trained, for if its movement becomes difficult and there appears some stiffness in it, it should be softened with warm water and rubbed many times until it becomes pliable.
The Twenty-Ninth Chapter.
In the Treatment of Dislocated Fingers.
Fingers may dislocate in any direction, so when a finger dislocates toward the back of the hand or its palm, extend the finger and push the dislocation with your thumb until it returns, then tie the tip of the finger and hang it towards the side to which it dislocated.
Leave it for two days, then release it and extend it until it stands straight that day. If it is at night, tie it in the same manner. Do not stop releasing it during the day and train it with movement and tie it at night. Continue this for several days until it strengthens.
Do the same if it dislocates into the palm of the hand, tying it toward the same side and repeating the first action until it heals. Similarly, do this whenever it dislocates in any other direction.
The Thirtieth Chapter.
In the Treatment of a Dislocated Vertebra.
When a vertebra from the vertebrae of the back or neck becomes completely dislocated or many vertebrae become displaced from their positions, there is no treatment for it because death hastens to the patient.
A sign of this is that the patient's stool comes out involuntarily and he cannot hold it back, and often some of his limbs relax, either his legs or his arms, or one of them. As for if only one vertebra becomes displaced, it often heals.
Displacement can occur in four directions, and the one that occurs backward is called a hump. As for its treatment, observe if the hump has occurred since childhood, there is no treatment for it and it will not heal at all. But the one that occurred from a fall, a blow, or something similar...
The ancients have mentioned it with various methods of treatment in lengthy speech, most of which is not very beneficial. I have summarized this, and a little suffices in place of much of what they have brought forth in terms of approaching the meaning and explaining it, and I have depicted the instrument differently than they have described and explained it.
I say that the prominence that occurs in front of the chest has no remedy, nor is there any cure for it, and neither does the one to the sides. Only the one that occurs in the back can be treated, particularly with what I will describe: which is to lay the patient on his face.
On a flat workbench near a wall, spread a wet rug underneath so that his chest is not hurt, then place an upright wooden plank embedded in a hole in the ground at one end of the workbench toward his head and another wooden plank toward his feet at the other end of the workbench.
Have a servant hold the plank and let it not be fixed in the hole, and another servant hold the other plank in that manner; then wrap a soft, tight bandage around the patient's chest and under his armpits, extending one end of the bandage to the plank at his head and tying it there.
Then tighten another bandage over his thighs and above his knees and at his ankles; then gather all the bandages together and tie them to the plank at his feet, and each servant extends the plank toward him with the bandages, and the planks do not move from their fixed positions.
But they are not fixed as we have said, and the physician places his hands on the prominence firmly until it returns or he places a board on it and then leans against the board with his legs so that it returns. If it does not return with this treatment, take a board about three cubits long.
Carve a place in the wall, which we said should be near the patient, where the end of the board will fit; then place the middle of the board on the prominence, and the physician puts his feet on the other end and pulls strongly until it compresses the prominence.
And return it to its place, and if you wish to make it with the screw that is twisted by hand, it is to insert in the ground at the head of the patient at the end of the bed two pieces of wood each a cubit long, and also at his feet at the end of the bed.
Let there be a span between each piece of wood, and let a hole be made in each piece of wood through which the screw is passed, and all the wood is firmly secured to the ground so that it does not move at all, then insert a round stick which is the screw into which the ligature is twisted in the holes of the two pieces of wood.
And at its end there is a hole in which a stick a cubit long is fixed to be twisted, and the same is done in the other two pieces of wood, then tighten the ligature that was tied on the patient's chest in the screw at his head, and the ligatures that were tied on his legs in the screw at his feet.
Then a servant stands at each screw and twists it with the turner by which the screw is twisted, and the physician straightens the hump as we have described. This is a picture of the screw, the bed and the patient:
Then after the vertebra returns and the position is even, it should be covered with a dried plaster bandage made with egg white then linen cloth, and then place a splint of wood on top of the bandage, which is three fingers wide or so, and as long as the hump was.
And on top of some proper beads, and then it is tied with the appropriate ligature. The patient should use a soft diet until they are healed. If there remains some protrusion in the place at the end of the healing, medicines that relax and soften should be used, along with the board we described for a long time.
A lead plate may also be used for this purpose. Sometimes a protrusion may appear at the end of the dorsal vertebrae and it may be mistaken for a dislocation. This is an extra bone that has grown, and do not treat it with this method as death may occur.
Chapter Thirty-One
In the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the shoulder joint are only subject to dislocation and not to the slight displacement and pitting that affects all other joints. The hip joint dislocates in four ways.
This is because it dislocates inwards, outwards, forwards, and backwards. It most often dislocates inwards and least often forwards or backwards. A sign of inward dislocation is that if you compare the healthy leg of the patient with the injured one, the injured leg will appear longer.
The knee will be more prominent than the healthy one and the patient will not be able to bend their leg at the hip. The area below the hip will be warm and swollen, indicating that the head of the femur has moved there.
The signs of dislocation outwards are the opposite of these. A sign of dislocation forwards is that the patient can straighten their leg completely, but they cannot bend it without pain. There will be pain in the knee and if they try to walk, they will not be able to move forward.
Urine retention occurs, and the hip is swollen. When walking, they land on their heels. A sign of dislocation backwards is that the knee cannot be straightened and cannot be bent before bending the hip. The leg will also be shorter than the other.
The hip is relaxed and the head of the femur is clearly located near the waist. As for correcting the different types of dislocation, if it is an old dislocation that has been endured by the patient without any attempt to reduce it, then there is no treatment at all.
It should not be treated. But if the dislocation is recent and of any of the four types, you should immediately manipulate the joint, flexing it inwards and outwards and moving it from side to side. This may
If it returns and does not require any other treatment, if it does not return this way, a servant with strong arms should be provided to support its leg from below, either with his hands or with a crutch tied to his leg above the knee, and another servant to support it from above.
He should insert his hands under his armpits and then tighten a soft crutch at the base of the thigh, with another third servant holding the end of the crutch. The third servant will support him either from the front from the side of the armpit to the collarbone or from behind to the back.
All of them should support him at the same time so that the patient can raise himself off the ground with his body and remain suspended. This type of support is a common type for all four types. If the jaw returns as we said, otherwise each type from what I mentioned requires specific treatment.
As for its specific return, if the jaw is inward, the patient should lie on his right side and then a crutch should be placed at the base of the thigh between the head of the thigh and the position under the armpit.
Then the bandage is extended upwards from the armpit to the top of the body to the collarbone, then another strong servant takes hold with his arms and embraces the thick part of the affected thigh and extends it outwards with a strong pull, for it will return to its place.
This type is easier than all other types of treatment in which this member is returned, if it is difficult for you and it does not respond to entering into this type of treatment at all, then the patient's legs should be tied together with a strong soft bandage on the heels and knees.
After each one of them is a distance of four fingers and the affected leg is more extended than the other by two fingers, then the patient is hung from the head on a wooden beam that is in the house, and his distance from the ground should be two arm lengths.
Then you order a strong boy to embrace the head of the thigh and another boy clings to the patient, while the other boy who is embracing the thigh pushes strongly; for the joint will return to its place quickly. As for returning a dislocated one if the jaw is outside.
The patient should lie on the bed as we described in Al-Hadba and his bandages should be tightened, especially on his affected leg and chest, then two planks are placed, one at his feet and the other at his head; then an additional plank is placed in the middle of the bed, firmly secured.
It should be wrapped with damp cloths so as not to hurt the patient, the plank being placed between his thighs so that he does not slide down during traction; then each attendant pulls in his direction and the physician straightens the hip with his hands, if it responds by returning.
If not, put the board on him and press as we mentioned in Al-Hadba, except that the patient should lie on his right side. And if the dislocation is forward, the patient's leg should be extended once while he is in this exact position on the bed.
The physician places the palm of his right hand on the affected thigh, then presses it with his other hand; however, the pressure should be extended downwards towards the knee. If the dislocation is backwards, the patient should not be extended downwards.
He should be raised off the ground, but rather placed on something solid, and also his hip should be flexed outwards as we mentioned when he is lying on the bed on his face with bandages tightened.
As we said before, the pressing with the board should also be used on the place where the joint dislocated to. Once the hip joint has returned properly, and the sign of its return will not be hidden from you; it is that you extend the patient's legs.
If you see them level and the patient draws up his leg and extends it without difficulty, know that the limb has returned to its proper place. Then join the thighs and lift the dressing, and tighten it with a turban so that the hip does not move in any direction.
The patient must remain at rest for three or four days, then the bandage and ligature are removed, and the leg is compared with the other. If they are equal in size, know that the fracture has set, and allow the patient to walk. If you see any laxity in it.
Set it again and bandage it tightly according to your initial binding, and leave it for another three days. Then remove the dressing and allow him to walk on it slowly for several days until it strengthens, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-Two.
On the Treatment of a Knee Fracture.
The knee is fractured in three ways: inward, outward, and downward, meaning backward, and it never fractures forward. The sign of a fracture is to order the patient to bring his leg up to his thigh.
If it does not stick to the thigh, know that the knee is fractured. To set all types of fractures, have the patient sit down with his legs extended if he is able, and place a servant behind him to hold his waist and turn him slightly backward. Then you sit on his thighs.
Press your back against his face and place his leg between yours, then firmly press his knee with both hands, interlocking your fingers on his knee. Then, using both hands, forcefully compress the sides of his knee. Another servant extends his leg until the knee returns to its place.
The sign that it has returned is that the leg adheres to the thigh without strain, then bandage it and firmly attach the leg to the thigh. Bind them all together with a bandage for three or four days, then release them. He should only use it for light walking for several days until it strengthens.
If it is impossible to return it in the way we have described, then use strong traction with the bandages which I have previously described for the treatment of the hip until it returns.
Chapter thirty-three.
On the treatment of a ruptured heel.
The heel may detach slightly or completely, and its rupture can be either inward or outward. The sign of a rupture is that you see the heel swollen and prominent in the direction it has ruptured.
As for the treatment of its slight detachment, it is easy to return it by gently pulling it with the hands and straightening it until it returns. As for its treatment if it has completely ruptured, the patient should sit down and a strong servant should hold him from behind his middle.
Then you grasp his foot with your right hand from above and with your left hand below it at the level of the ankle, then pull the foot towards you with your right hand and then twice with your left hand, then extend it with your left hand and push the front of the foot with your right hand towards the leg without violence.
You do this twice as we have described, then push the front of the foot towards the leg in the third attempt while pulling at the ankle. If it returns in one or two attempts in this manner and you see the foot straight, otherwise repeat the process, for it will return.
If it refuses to return as we have described, otherwise lay the patient on his back on the ground and fix a strong peg firmly in the ground so that it lies between his thighs after having wrapped a cloth around it so as not to hurt the patient, then the servant holds his thigh.
Then another servant extends the leg either with his hands or with a bandage tied around the neck of the leg, then each servant extends in opposition to the extension of the other, and the peg remains fixed between the patient's thighs so that he does not get dragged down during the extension, and then the physician straightens the rupture with his hands.
Another attendant holds the sound leg downwards, for the jaw returns quickly. When the jaw has returned and you have ascertained its soundness, apply a dressing and splint, and tighten it with strong ligatures, and then bind the foot to the splint with a bandage downwards. Care must be taken to avoid the nerve which lies above the heel behind.
So that the bandage is not tight upon it and causes it pain, then leave it for two or three days; if the bandage becomes loose tighten it again, and then release it on the third or fourth day. The patient must abstain from walking for forty days.
If he wishes to walk before this time, you cannot be sure that the jaw will not dislocate and become useless, and will afterwards refuse treatment; if there should appear hot swelling, what we have previously described in another part of the treatment for abscesses and poulticing should be used until it subsides.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On the Treatment of Fractures of the Fingers.
That which is broken should be straightened with a slight extension, without violence; this is not difficult but easy. If the fracture is in one of the phalanges of the toes, the patient should place his foot on a level surface.
Or on a board while standing as if walking, then you should place your foot upon that which is broken from those joints, and then tread upon it firmly with your foot until it returns and you see that it has become straight and there is no protuberance in the place.
Then place a board under the sole of his foot which will take the whole foot and has two heads, then tighten it firmly for three days; then loosen it and keep him from walking for many days until it becomes strong and you are safe from its return, if God wills.
Chapter Thirty-Five.
On the Kinds of Fracture Which Occur With a Wound or With a Break, or With Both.
When something of this nature occurs and it is treated with surgery and bandaging, death often follows.
Therefore, it should not be undertaken except by one who is skilled in the craft, long experienced, gentle, compassionate, deliberate, neither rash nor bold, and who in the beginning uses only medications to soothe hot tumors, and then leaves the patient alone.
For God's sake, only hope for safety from the disease with the lightness of the illness and if some hope appears to you in it, then turn it away from that hour in the first place before the hot tumor occurs.
If the member returns to what you desired, use the treatment that soothes hot tumors and treat the wound with drying ointments suitable for it. If the fracture is with a broken bone and there are loose fragments in the bone, then remove them. Follow in this what we have mentioned about simple diseases previously in their places.
Strive and purify yourself from entering the path of risk, according to my previous advice to you. This is what will ensure your reputation and safety, God willing.
